Purchased Child of a Deceased Mukatab: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on purchased child of mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether a purchased child of a deceased mukatab must work for his father's installments and explain the basis of the dispute.

Core Idea
A child purchased by a mukatab is not bound by the kitaba contract; he may pay the price or be returned.

The mukatab's child born during the kitaba is obligated to work for the installments. But what about a child he purchased? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains Abu Hanifa's position and the Sahibayn's objection, focusing on the role of the term (ajal) as a condition of the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of children
The text distinguishes between a child born during the kitaba (al-mawlud) and a child purchased during the kitaba (al-mushtara). Each has a different ruling according to Abu Hanifa.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling for each
For the born child: he must work (yus'ā) for the installments, and when he pays, the father is deemed freed before death and the child is freed. For the purchased child: he has the option to pay the price (badal) immediately or be returned as a slave.
The born child is bound by the kitaba; the purchased child is not.
3
State the Sahibayn's objection
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the purchased child also must work for the father's installments because he was contracted by following the father (kuṭiba bi-tabaʿiyyat al-ab). Both are considered as one person in the contract.
They analogize the purchased child to the born child.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's distinction
Abu Hanifa argues that the term (ajal) is a condition of the contract, so it applies only to those who entered the contract. The purchased child did not enter the contract; he was merely purchased. Therefore, he is not bound by the deferred payment obligation.
Without necessity to attribute the term, the purchased child remains a slave at the father's death and does not inherit.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the purchased child can choose not to pay and be returned to slavery. Under the Sahibayn, he is forced to work for the installments, and if he pays, he is freed. The practical consequence is the freedom of choice vs. compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يترك وفاء فمَن ولد في كتابتِهِ سعى على نجومه، وإذا أدى حكم بعتق أبيه قبل موته ويعتقه، ومَن شراهُ أدّى البدل حالاً ، أو رد رقيقاً. هذا عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما الولد المشترى يسعى على نجوم الأب أيضاً ، لأنَّه كوتب بتبعية الأب. كانا مكاتبين كل منهما بعقد واحد ؛ لأنَّ الولد إن كان منفرداً بكتابته وأدّاها بعد الموتِ لم يرثه ؛ لأنه مقصود بالكتابة. وإنما يعتق من وقتِ أداء البدل مقصوراً عليه ؛ لأن الاستناد للضرورة، ولا ضرورة في حقه هاهنا ، فإذا لم يستند عتقه كان عبداً عند موت أبيه ؛ فلهذا لا يرثه. كذا في «الكفاية). [1]قوله : كشخص واحد ؛ فإذا حكم بحرية الأب يحكم بحريته في تلك الحالة على ما مر. كذا في «الهداية» (٢). (٢) قوله : حكم بعتق أبيه...الخ ؛ لأن الولد داخل في كتابته، وكسبه ككسبه، فيخلفه في الأداء، وصار كما إذا ترك وفاء. كذا في «الهداية» (۳). [٣] قوله : ومن شراه...الخ ؛ أي إن ترك ولداً مشترى في الكتابة، فله الخيار بين أن يؤدي بدل الكتابة حالاً، وبين أن يردّ رقيقاً. [٤]قوله : هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وله الفرق بين الفصلين أنّ الأجل يثبت شرطاً في العقد، فيثبتُ في حقٌّ مَن دخل تحت العقد، والمشترى لم يدخل ؛ لأنه لم يضف إليه
And if he does not leave sufficient (property), then whoever is born during his kitaba will strive for his installments, and when he pays, it is judged that his father was freed before his death and he is freed. And whoever was purchased by him pays the price immediately or returns him as a slave. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the purchased child also strives for the father's installments, because he was contracted by following the father. Both are mukatib by a single contract; because if the child were alone with his own kitaba and paid after death, he would not inherit from him; because he is intended by the kitaba. And he is only freed from the time of paying the price, limited to him; because attribution is for necessity, and there is no necessity in his case here. So if his freedom is not attributed, he is a slave at the time of his father's death; therefore he does not inherit from him. This is in 'Al-Kifayah'. [1] His saying: 'as one person'; so if the father's freedom is judged, his freedom is judged in that case as previously mentioned. This is in 'Al-Hidayah'. (2) His saying: 'it is judged that his father was freed...' etc.; because the child is included in his kitaba, and his earnings are like his earnings, so he succeeds him in payment, and it becomes as if he left sufficient property. This is in 'Al-Hidayah'. (3) [3] His saying: 'And whoever was purchased by him...' etc.; i.e., if he leaves a child purchased during the kitaba, he has the option between paying the price of the kitaba immediately or returning him as a slave. [4] His saying: 'This is according to Abu Hanifa' and he distinguishes between the two cases: that the term is established as a condition in the contract, so it is established for the one who enters under the contract, and the purchased child did not enter; because he was not added to him.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the status of children of a mukatab who dies insolvent. The matn states the ruling: a child born during the kitaba must work to pay off the father's installments, and when he pays, the father is deemed freed before death, and the child is freed. A child purchased during the kitaba (i.e., a slave bought by the mukatab) has a different ruling: he may either pay the price immediately or be returned as a slave. The commentary explains that this is Abu Hanifa's position. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the purchased child also works for the father's installments because he was included in the contract by following the father. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: the term (deferred payment) is a condition of the contract, so it applies only to those who entered the contract. The purchased child did not enter the contract; he was merely purchased. Therefore, he has the option to pay immediately or be returned. If the purchased child were to have his own kitaba and pay after the father's death, he would not inherit from the father because the kitaba is only for the child's own freedom, not for succession. The commentary from al-Kifayah and al-Hidayah supports this reasoning.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) with his master to pay a sum for his freedom in installments.
نجوم (nujūm): Installments of the kitaba price.
بدل (badal): The price of a purchased child, which must be paid immediately if the child is not to be returned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Abu Hanifa's distinction between a born child and a purchased child?
A) The born child is more deserving of freedom.
B) The term (ajal) is a condition of the contract and only binds those who entered it.
C) The purchased child is too young to work.
D) The master prefers to take back the purchased child.

Key Takeaway
The term (ajal) as a condition of the kitaba contract only binds those who entered it; the purchased child, not being a party, has an option rather than an obligation.

Discerning Ta'jiz: When a Judgment on a Child's Wala' Cancels the Kitaba
Explains when a judgment on a child's wala' cancels the kitaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two scenarios involving a mukatab's child and determine whether a judgment constitutes ta'jiz (cancellation) of the kitaba.

Core Idea
A judgment against a child's wala' only cancels the kitaba if it implies the father died a slave.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents two related cases concerning a mukatab who dies leaving a child from a free woman. In the first case, the child commits a crime and the blood money is imposed on the mother's 'aqila—this does not affect the kitaba. In the second, a dispute over the child's wala' is adjudged to the mother's mawali, which implies the father died a slave and thus cancels the kitaba. The distinction hinges on whether the judgment implicitly declares the father's status at death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: (1) child commits crime, judgment against mother's 'aqila; (2) dispute over child's wala', judgment in favor of mother's mawali.
Both involve a child of a mukatab who dies leaving a debt covering the kitaba.
2
Examine the effect on the father's kitaba
In case 1, the kitaba remains valid; in case 2, it is annulled (ta'jiz). Determine why the outcomes differ.
The key is the implicit message about the father's status.
3
Analyze the legal principle
The Hanafi principle: ta'jiz occurs only when it is proven that the mukatab is unable to pay, or when a judgment indicates he died a slave. Here, the wala' ruling for the mother's mawali implies the father died a slave, so the kitaba is void.
In contrast, the crime judgment does not address the father's ability or status.
4
Apply the distinction to new examples
If a mukatab's child is adjudged to be a slave (e.g., through a wala' dispute), the mukatab's kitaba is nullified. But if the child is adjudged free and liable for blood money, the kitaba remains valid as long as the father's debts cover the price.
The critical factor is whether the judgment directly or indirectly asserts the father's incapacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ترك ولداً من حرَّةٍ معتقة وديناً يفي ببدلها ، فجنى الولد وقضي به على عاقلة أمه لم يكن ذلك تعجيزاً لأبيه. وإن اختصم قومُ إمِّهِ وأبيه في ولائه، فقضي به لقوم أمه فهو تعجيز. [1] قوله : فإن ترك ولداً من حرّة ; ذكر هذه المسألة التي ذكرها بقوله : وإن اختصم ... الخ ; فرقا بينهما صورة المسألة الأولى : مكاتب مات وله ولد حرّ من حرّة، وترك ديناً على الناس وفاءً بمكاتبه، فالكتابة باقية، وولاء الولد لموالى الأم. وصورة المسألة الثانية : مات هذا الولد بعد الأب ، واختصم موالي الأب وموالي الأم، فقال موالي الأم : مات رقيقاً والولاء لنا، وقال موالى الأب : مات حراً والولاء لنا ، فقضى بولائه لموالي الأم. كذا في «حاشية جلبي. [٢] قوله : فهو تعجيز ; أي قضاء بعجز المكاتب وفسخ الكتابة; لأنّ هذا (۲) ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٣١).
If he leaves a child from a free woman who has been set free (mu'taqa) and a debt that suffices for her price, then the child commits a crime and it is adjudged (the liability) upon the 'aqila of his mother, that is not a cancellation ('ajz) for his father. And if the people (tribe) of his mother and his father dispute about his wala', and it is adjudged to the people of his mother, then that is a cancellation.' [1] His saying: 'If he leaves a child from a free woman...' He mentioned this issue which he mentioned by his saying: 'and if they dispute...' etc.; a distinction between them. The scenario of the first case: a mukatab dies and he has a free child from a free woman, and he leaves a debt upon people sufficient for his mukataba, so the kitaba remains, and the wala' of the child goes to the mawali of the mother. The scenario of the second case: this child dies after the father, and the mawali of the father and the mawali of the mother dispute, so the mawali of the mother say: 'He died a slave, so the wala' is for us,' and the mawali of the father say: 'He died a free man, so the wala' is for us,' and it is adjudged that his wala' is for the mother's mawali. This is in 'Hashiyat Chalabi'. [2] His saying: 'that is a cancellation' i.e., a judgment of the mukatab's inability (to pay) and annulment of the kitaba; because this (is from) 'Dhakhiratu al-Uqba' (p. 531).
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents two related cases concerning a mukatab who dies leaving a child from a free woman and debts covering the kitaba price. In the first case, the child commits a crime, and the blood money (gratuitous liability) is imposed on the mother's 'aqila. Since this judgment does not contradict the ongoing kitaba (the debt covers the price), it does not constitute 'ajz (inability/cancellation) for the father. In the second case, after the child dies, there is a dispute between the mawali (client groups) of the father and mother regarding the child's wala'. If the qadi rules that the wala' belongs to the mother's people, that implies the father died a slave, thus the kitaba is nullified ('ajz). The commentary from Hashiyat Chalabi clarifies the distinction: in the first case, the kitaba remains valid because the debt is sufficient; in the second, the ruling on the child's wala' essentially declares the father's kitaba void.

Key Terms
تعجيز (ta'jīz): A judicial declaration that a mukatab is unable to pay his kitaba, resulting in annulment of the contract and return to slavery.
ولاء (walā'): Clientage: the bond of patronage between a freed slave and his former master or his descendants.
عاقلة ('āqila): The tribal group or collective responsible for paying blood money (diya) on behalf of a member who commits manslaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the qadi's ruling on wala' in favor of the mother's mawali constitute ta'jiz?
A) Because it directly declares the mukatab unable to pay.
B) Because it implies the father died a slave, thus the kitaba is void.
C) Because the mother's mawali have a stronger claim.
D) Because the child is no longer alive.

Key Takeaway
A judgment on a child's wala' can annul the kitaba if it implicitly declares the father's slave status, whereas a judgment on blood money does not.

Effect of Child's Crime and Wala' Dispute on Mukatab's Kitaba
Explaining effect of mukatab's child's crime and wala' dispute on kitaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two judicial rulings concerning a mukatab's child and state which one constitutes ta'jiz (declaration of incapacity) and why.

Core Idea
A ruling that implies the mukatab died in slavery rescinds the kitaba; a ruling merely attaching the child to mother's mawali does not.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced issue in mukatab law: when does a court ruling about the child affect the father's kitaba contract? Two scenarios are contrasted: one where a crime judgment is harmless, and another where a wala' dispute judgment constitutes ta'jiz. Understanding the underlying reasoning (ta'lil) is key to applying the rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and identify the two distinct situations presented. Note the key difference: in the first, a crime is adjudged against the mother's 'aqila; in the second, there is a dispute over the child's wala'.
The first scenario involves a living child after the father's death; the second involves the child's death after the father.
2
Determine the ruling in each scenario
For each scenario, state whether the ruling constitutes ta'jiz for the father. The passage explicitly says the first is not ta'jiz, and the second is.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the first scenario
Explain why the crime judgment does not imply the father's death in slavery. The child is free and attached to mother's mawali temporarily; the father may still become free, shifting wala'.
The debt is sufficient for kitaba, so the contract remains viable.
4
Analyze the reasoning for the second scenario
Explain why the wala' ruling implies the father died as a slave. If the child's wala' goes to mother's mawali, the father could not have been free at death, so the kitaba is rescinded.
This is an ijtihadi matter, so the judge's ruling is binding.
5
Evaluate the practical distinction
Summarize the key distinction: rulings that necessarily imply the father's death in slavery cause ta'jiz; those that are compatible with eventual freedom do not.
This distinction protects the mukatab's contract from being voided by incidental judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ترك ولداً من حرَّةٍ معتقة وديناً يفي ببدلها ، فجنى الولد وقضي به على عاقلة أمه لم يكن ذلك تعجيزاً لأبيه وإن اختصم قومُ إمِّهِ وأبيه في ولائه، فقضي به لقوم أمه فهو تعجيز

(فإن ترك ولداً من حرَّةٍ معتقة وديناً يفي ببدلها ، فجنى الولد وقضي به) : أي بموجب الجناية ، (على) عاقلة أمه لم يكن ذلك تعجيزاً لأبيه) ؛ لأنَّ هذا القضاء لا ينافي الكتابة ؛ لأن مقتضى الكتابة إلحاق الولد بموالي الأم، وإيجاب العقل عليهم، لكن على وجه يحتمل أن يعتق الأب فينجر الولاء إلى موالي الأب، وإنما قال : وديناً يفي ؛ لأنه لو كان عيناً لا يتأتى القضاء بالإلحاق بالأم ؛ لأنه يمكن الوفاء في الحال. ( وإن اختصم قومُ إمِّهِ وأبيه في ولائه، فقضي به لقوم أمه فهو تعجيز) ؛ لأن القضاء بكون ولاءِ الولد لموالي الأم، معناه : أن الأب مات رقيقاً، وانفسخَ عقد الكتابة فيكون القضاء في فصل مجتهد فيه، فينفذ فيه وتنفسخ الكتابة، ولا يسري حكمه إليه لانفصاله بخلاف الصورة الأولى، أي المولود في الكتابة ؛ لأنه متصل وقت الكتابة، فيسري الحكم إليه، وحيث دخل في حكمه سعى في نجومه. كذا في «الهداية).

قوله : فإن ترك ولداً من حرّة ؛ ذكر هذه المسألة التي ذكرها بقوله : وإن اختصم ... الخ ؛ فرقا بينهما صورة المسألة الأولى : مكاتب مات وله ولد حرّ من حرّة، وترك ديناً على الناس وفاءً بمكاتبه، فالكتابة باقية، وولاء الولد لموالى الأم. وصورة المسألة الثانية : مات هذا الولد بعد الأب ، واختصم موالي الأب وموالي الأم، فقال موالي الأم : مات رقيقاً والولاء لنا، وقال موالى الأب : مات حراً والولاء لنا ، فقضى بولائه لموالي الأم. كذا في «حاشية جلبي.

قوله : فهو تعجيز ؛ أي قضاء بعجز المكاتب وفسخ الكتابة؛ لأنّ هذا اختلاف في الولاء مقصوداً، وذلك يتبنى على بقاء الكتابة وانتقاضها، فإنّها إذا فسخت مات عبداً، واستقر الولاء على موالي الأم، وإذا بقيت واتصل بها الأداء مات الولد حراً، وانتقل الولاء إلى موالي الأب، وهذا فصل مجتهد فيه، فينفذ ما يلاقيه من القضاء ؛ فلهذا كان هذا القضاء تعجيزاً. كذا في «الهداية».
If [a mukatab] leaves a child from a free woman who has been manumitted, and a debt sufficient to cover his kitaba, and the child commits a crime and it is adjudged against the mother's 'aqila, that is not a declaration of incapacity for the father. And if the people of his mother and his father dispute over his wala', and judgment is given to the mother's people, then that is a declaration of incapacity. Commentary: The first judgment does not contradict kitaba because kitaba requires attaching the child to the mother's mawali and imposing the 'aql upon them, but in a way that if the father is freed, the wala' shifts to the father's mawali. The debt is mentioned as being collectible; if it were cash, the judgment would not be possible because payment could be made immediately. The second judgment means the father died as a slave and the kitaba contract is nullified; this is an ijtihadi matter, so the judgment is executed and the kitaba is dissolved. The ruling does not extend to him due to separation, unlike the first case where the child is born during kitaba, so the ruling extends to him and he pays the installments. Further explanation: The first scenario: a mukatab dies leaving a free child and sufficient debts, kitaba remains, wala' is for mother's mawali. Second scenario: the child dies after the father, a dispute over wala' occurs, judgment for mother's mawali, which is ta'jiz because it implies the father died a slave, so kitaba is dissolved.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah discusses two related rulings concerning a mukatab (a slave who has contracted manumission by payment). The first ruling: if a mukatab dies leaving a child from a free woman who had been manumitted, and also leaves debts that suffice to pay the kitaba, and the child commits a crime for which compensation is adjudged against the mother's 'aqila (tribal guarantors), this judgment does not constitute ta'jiz (declaration of incapacity) for the father. The reason is that the kitaba contract remains valid; the child is attached to the mother's mawali (former masters) for wala' (patronage rights) and the 'aql is on them, but if the father eventually becomes free, the wala' can shift to the father's mawali. The second ruling: if after the father's death, a dispute arises between the mother's and father's mawali over the child's wala', and the judge rules in favor of the mother's mawali, that judgment is ta'jiz for the father. This is because the ruling effectively declares that the father died as a slave (since wala' belongs only to the manumitter, and if the mother's mawali get it, the father must have been unfree at death), thus the kitaba is rescinded. The commentary emphasizes that this is an ijtihadi matter, so the judge's ruling is binding. The distinction between the two cases lies in whether the child is born before or after the father's kitaba; in the first case, the child is separate from the kitaba contract, while in the second, he is part of it? Actually, the text clarifies that in the first scenario, the child is free and not affected by the kitaba, so the judgment does not impact the father. In the second, the child's death triggers a dispute that indirectly determines the father's status.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with his master, agreeing to pay a sum for his freedom.
ولاء (wala'): The right of patronage and inheritance that belongs to the former master (or his heirs) upon manumission of a slave.
تعجيز (ta'jiz): A judicial declaration that a mukatab is incapable of paying his kitaba, resulting in the rescission of the contract and return to slavery.
عاقلة ('aqila): The tribal or group guarantors responsible for paying blood money (diya) on behalf of a member who commits homicide or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario, why does the judgment against the mother's 'aqila not constitute ta'jiz for the mukatab father?
A) Because the judgment is executed after the father's death, so it cannot affect him.
B) Because the judgment does not contradict the kitaba contract, as the child is free and attached to mother's mawali, but the father may still become free.
C) Because the judgment is only about the child's crime, not about the father's kitaba.
D) Because the father had already paid his kitaba.

Key Takeaway
A court ruling implies the mukatab's death in slavery only if it directly determines wala' based on that assumption, voiding the kitaba.

Slave Commits Crime Then Made Mukatab in Ignorance
Ruling on kitaba of a slave who committed a crime when the master was unaware

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master makes a slave mukatab while ignorant of a crime committed by the slave, and explain the effect on the master's liability.

Core Idea
Kitaba made in ignorance is valid but suspends the master's liability; liability returns when the kitaba ends.

A slave commits a crime, then his master, unaware, enters into a kitaba contract with him. Is the kitaba valid? What about the master's liability? This lesson clarifies that the master's ignorance does not invalidate the contract, but it does affect the timeline of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The slave commits a crime (jinaya) that typically makes the master liable. The master then makes the slave a mukatab (enters a kitaba contract) without knowing about the crime.
2
Determine the validity of the kitaba
The text implies the kitaba is valid despite the master's ignorance. Ignorance does not void the contract.
The master's lack of knowledge does not invalidate the act of kitaba.
3
Understand the master's option
If the master had known, he could have chosen to pay the ransom (fida') or surrender the slave. Ignorance means he did not exercise this choice at the time of kitaba.
The option remains after the kitaba ends.
4
Effect of the kitaba on liability
The kitaba contract temporarily prevents the victim from claiming compensation from the master. It is a barrier (mani') to enforcement.
The master is not pursued during the kitaba period.
5
After the kitaba ends
When the kitaba ends (by payment or lapse), the barrier is removed and the original ruling returns: the master becomes liable for the crime.
The liability is not extinguished but only suspended.

Source Text

فإن جنى عبد فكاتبه سيده جاهلاً بها)

قوله : جاهلاً بها ; أي لم يكن المولى عالماً بالجناية عند الكتابة، حتى يصير مختاراً للفداء، إلا أن الكتابة مانعة من الدفع، فإذا زال المانع عاد الحكم الأصلي.
If a slave commits a crime, then his master makes him a mukatab while ignorant of it. Commentary: 'Ignorant of it' means the master was not aware of the crime at the time of kitaba, so that he would have chosen to pay the ransom. However, the kitaba prevents the claim from being enforced, so when the prevention is removed, the original ruling returns.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This brief passage addresses the scenario where a slave commits a crime (jinaya) against a third party, and then his master enters into a kitaba contract with him, unaware of the crime. The issue is that after manumission, the victim might seek compensation from the mawali (patrons) or the slave himself. The ruling implies that the kitaba is valid, but because the master was ignorant, his choice to pay the ransom (fida') was not exercised. The commentary clarifies that 'ignorant' means he did not know at the time of the contract. Since the kitaba contract temporarily prevents the master from being pursued for the crime (the slave is still in the process of buying freedom), the claim is suspended. When the kitaba ends (by payment or lapse), the original liability of the master for the crime returns. This is consistent with the principle that the master's obligation for the slave's crimes is suspended during kitaba.

Key Terms
جناية (jinaya): A crime committed by the slave, typically requiring compensation (diya or qisas) from the master if the slave is unable to pay.
فداء (fida'): Ransom payment made by the master to the victim in lieu of surrendering the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the master's liability for the slave's crime after the kitaba ends if the master was ignorant of the crime?
A) The liability is canceled because of the kitaba.
B) The liability returns to the master because the kitaba only suspended it.
C) The slave becomes personally liable after freedom.
D) The victim loses the right to claim.

Key Takeaway
A slave's kitaba contract is valid even if the master was ignorant of a crime committed by the slave; the master's liability is suspended during the kitaba and returns upon its conclusion.

Ruling on Master Benefiting from Zakat Paid by Mukatab
Clarifies that master may keep zakat paid by mukatab even after ajz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master keeping zakat paid by a mukatab, explain the analogy (qiyas) to the hadith of Barirah, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf with opposing views.

Core Idea
Zakat becomes permissible payment when received as contract consideration, not charity.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario: a mukatab (a slave under kitaba) receives zakat legitimately, then pays it to his master as part of his manumission contract. Later, the mukatab becomes unable to complete the kitaba, and the question arises: can the master keep the money, even though he is rich and normally cannot receive zakat? The Hanafi school rules that it is permissible, drawing an analogy to the famous hadith of Barirah. Understanding this ruling clarifies how the legal character of wealth can change based on the transfer context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The passage describes: (1) a master who is not eligible for zakat, (2) a mukatab who receives zakat because he is among the recipients, (3) the mukatab pays that zakat to his master as badal al-kitaba, (4) the mukatab later becomes unable, so it becomes apparent that the master indirectly received zakat while rich. The ruling: the master may permissibly keep it (yateebu lahu).
2
Locate the legal proof (dalil)
The author cites the hadith: "لها صَدَقَة، وَلَنَا هَدِيَّة" (For her it is charity, for us it is a gift). This is from the story of Barirah when meat was given to her as charity and she offered it to the Prophet.
The proof is that the same item can have a different legal ruling for different parties based on the mode of acquisition.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (relevance of the proof)
The Prophet distinguished between the charity given to Barirah and the same item when it became a gift for him. Similarly, the zakat given to the mukatab becomes a permissible payment for the master because he received it as consideration (iwad) for freedom, not as zakat. The character changes with transfer.
Hanafi usul: the ruling is derived via qiyas (analogy) based on the illa of change in ownership context.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and its basis
Some scholars (e.g., Shafi'is) might argue that the master indirectly benefits from zakat, which is prohibited. They may not accept the analogy because the master is originally prohibited from receiving zakat. The Hanafi response is that the transfer is a valid contractual payment, not a charitable gift.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is whether the master must return the money or may keep it.
5
Apply the ruling to analogous modern cases
This ruling has contemporary relevance: if a zakat-eligible debtor uses zakat funds to repay a debt to a wealthy creditor, the creditor may keep the payment, provided the transaction is a genuine arm's-length settlement and not a disguised charitable transfer.
The underlying principle is that the source of funds does not taint the transaction if the recipient's title is based on a different legal cause (sabab).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطاب لسيده ما أدى إليه من صدقة فعجز) : أي إذا لم يكن المولى مصرفا للزَّكاة، فأخذ المكاتبُ الزَّكاة ؛ لكونه من المصارف، ثم أداه إلى المولى عن بدل الكتابة ، ثمَّ عَجِزَ فظهر أنَّ المولى أخذ الزَّكاة، وهو غني، ومع ذلك يطيب له ، لأنَّهُ أخذَهُ عوضاً عن العتق زمانَ الأخذ ، والعبد قد أخذه صدقة ، وقد قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: [لها صَدَقَة، وَلَنَا هَدِيَّة).
And it is permissible for his master what he paid to him from charity, then he becomes unable: meaning if the master is not a recipient of zakat, so the mukatab took the zakat because he is among the recipients, then he gave it to the master as payment for the kitaba, then he became unable, so it appears that the master took zakat while he is rich, yet it is permissible for him, because he took it as compensation for freedom at the time of taking, and the slave had taken it as charity, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'For her it is charity, and for us it is a gift.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling on a mukatab who receives zakat and pays it to his master towards his kitaba, then later becomes unable to complete the contract. Despite the master being rich and thus normally prohibited from receiving zakat, he may keep the money because at the time he took it, it was a payment for freedom (i.e., consideration for the promised emancipation), not a zakat payment. The hadith of the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) regarding Barīrah supports this: when meat was given to her as charity and she presented it to him, he said 'it is charity for her but a gift for us.' This analogy (qiyās) shows that the character of the money changes based on the recipient's intent and the context of transfer. The Hanafi position is that the master may rightfully retain the payment.

Key Terms
صدقة (sadaqah): Zakat or voluntary charity given to eligible recipients
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitaba) to earn his freedom by paying installments
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitābah): The consideration or payment agreed upon in the kitaba contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the master permissibly keep the zakat paid by the mukatab even after the mukatab becomes unable?
A) Because the master is poor and eligible for zakat
B) Because the master took it as payment for freedom, not as zakat
C) Because the mukatab did not know he was giving zakat
D) Because the hadith says so without explanation

Key Takeaway
The legal character of wealth can change upon transfer; a zakat payment received as a contractual consideration is permissible for a rich recipient.

Jinaaya of a Mukatab and the Effect of Ajz (Incapacity)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on jinaaya and ajz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for a mukatab who commits a crime and then becomes unable to pay, and state the effect on the kitaba and wala.

Core Idea
A mukatab's crime creates options for the master; inability to pay lifts the kitaba's barrier and reinstates the original liability.

A slave commits a crime, then becomes a mukatab, and later becomes unable to pay. How does the kitaba contract interact with criminal liability? This passage from al-Hidayah presents the rules and the intra-madhhab differences. By the end, you will navigate the master's options and the fate of wala.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute about wala
The passage opens with a slave who commits a crime, then is made a mukatab without the master knowing. There is a difference of opinion regarding the wala of children born to this slave. The wala depends on whether the kitaba is dissolved or remains.
2
Understand the effect of 'ajz on the master's options before judgment
When a mukatab commits a crime and no judgment is made, his inability to pay removes the barrier of kitaba, returning the master to the original choice: hand over the slave or pay compensation.
3
Distinguish when judgment has been made
If a judgment is made against the mukatab while he is still able, the liability becomes a debt attached to his servitude. Upon inability, he is sold to pay it.
4
Analyze the ruling on the death of the master
The kitaba does not dissolve upon the master's death because it is the mukatab's right. The mukatab continues paying installments to the heirs.
5
Synthesize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The ruling on wala and the effect of ajz is a matter of ijtihad among Hanafi imams. The text states that whatever the judge decides is enforceable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن جنى عبد فكاتبه سيده جاهلاً بها اختلاف في الولاء مقصوداً، وذلك يتبنى على بقاء الكتابة وانتقاضها، فإنّها إذا فسخت مات عبداً، واستقر الولاء على موالي الأم، وإذا بقيت واتصل بها الأداء مات الولد حراً، وانتقل الولاء إلى موالي الأب، وهذا فصل مجتهد فيه، فينفذ ما يلاقـيـه مـن القضاء ; فلهذا كان هذا القضاء تعجيزاً. كذا في «الهداية»(۲). [١]قوله : من صدقة ; أي من الصدقة التي أخذها المكاتب عن الغير، ثمّ أداه ... الخ; وهذا عند محمد الله ظاهر ; لأنَّ بالعجز يتبدل الملك عنده. وكذا عند أبي يوسف الله ، وإن كان بالعجز يتقرّر ملك المولى . عنده ; لأنه لا خبث في نفس الصدقة، وإنما الخبث في فعل الآخذ ; لكونه إذلالاً به، والأخذ لم يوجد من المولى، فصار كابن السبيل إذا وصل إلى وطنه وكالفقير إذا استغنى، وقد بقي في أيديهما ما أخذا من الصدقة، حيث يطيب لهما. كذا في «الهداية»(۳). [۲] قوله : لأنه أخذه عوضاً ; وتبدل السبب كتبدل العين. [٣] قوله : جاهلاً بها ; أي لم يكن المولى عالماً بالجناية عند الكتابة، حتى يصير مختاراً للفداء، إلا أن الكتابة مانعة من الدفع، فإذا زال المانع عاد الحكم الأصلي. فعجز أو مكاتب فلم يقض به فعجز دفع أو فَدَى وإن قضي به عليه مكاتباً فعجز بيع فيه، ولا تنفسخُ بموتِ السَّيد، وأدى البدل إلى ورثته على نجومه أي بالجناية، فعجز أو مكاتب فلم يقض به فعجز دفع أو فَدَى) : أي جنى مكاتب فلم يقض بموجب الجنايةِ فعجزَ خَيْرَ بين دفعه، وأداء إرش الجناية ; لأنَّ هذا هو موجب جناية العبد لكنَّ الكتابة صارت مانعة عن الدفع ، ثم زال المانع بالعجز، فعاد الحكم الأصلي. [1] (وإن قضي به عليه مكاتباً فعجز بيع فيه) : أي وإن قضى بموجب الجناية على المكاتب حال كونه مكاتباً، ثمّ عجز بيع في ذلك ; لأنه دين متعلق برقيتِهِ بالقضاء، فانتقل إلى قيمته. [۲] ولا تنفسخ بموت السيد، وأدى البدل" إلى ورثته على نجومه [ ] قوله : وإن قضي به ; أي بموجب الجناية عليه ; أي على المكاتب في كتابته، ثم عجز، فما قضى به من موجبها دين في ذمته يباع فيه. كذا في «العناية»(۲). [٢]قوله : ولا تنفسخ ... الخ ; يعني أنّ الكتابة حق المكاتب ; لأنها سبب حريته ، وحرية حقه فهي سبب حقه، وسبب حق المرء حقه لافضائه إلى حصوله، فالكتابة حقه، فإذا مات المولى لم ينفسخ ; لئلا يؤدّي موته إلى إبطال حق غيره. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» (۳) (۳) قوله : وأدى البدل...الخ ; أي قيل له أد البدل إلى ورثته، فيؤدي إلى ورثته على نجومه ; لأنه استحق الحريّة بالأداء على النجوم المقررة من المولى، والسبب كذلك فيبقى بهذه الصفة، ولا يتغيّر إلا أنّ الورثة يخلفون المولى في الاستيفاء، بأن أعتقوه جميعاً، أي ابرأوا عن بدل الكتابة، وهذه لا يسمى تغيراً.
If a slave commits a crime and then his master makes him a mukatab while ignorant of it: there is a difference of opinion regarding the wala (loyalty) as intended, and that depends on the continuity or dissolution of the kitaba. For if it is dissolved, he dies as a slave, and the wala settles on the mother's masters; and if it remains and payment is made, the child dies free, and the wala transfers to the father's masters. This is a matter of ijtihad, so whatever ruling the judge issues is executed; therefore this judgment is a declaration of incapacity. Likewise in al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: from charity, i.e., from the charity that the mukatab took from someone else, then he paid it... etc.; this is apparent according to Muhammad, because with incapacity the ownership changes according to him. Likewise according to Abu Yusuf, although with incapacity the master's ownership is confirmed according to him, because there is no impurity in the charity itself; the impurity is only in the act of the taker because it is degrading, and the taking did not occur from the master, so it becomes like the case of the wayfarer when he reaches his homeland, and like the poor person when he becomes rich, and what they took of charity remains in their hands, so it is permissible for them. Likewise in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: because he took it as compensation; and change of cause is like change of substance. [3] His statement: ignorant of it, i.e., the master was not aware of the crime at the time of kitaba, so that he would have chosen to ransom; rather, the kitaba prevented handing over, so when the prevention is removed the original ruling returns. If a mukatab commits a crime and it is not judged against him, and then he becomes unable, the master has the option to hand him over or pay the blood money. And if a judgment is made against him while he is a mukatab and then he becomes unable, he is sold for it. And it does not dissolve upon the death of the master, and he pays the ransom to his heirs according to its installments, i.e., because of the crime. If a mukatab commits a crime and it is not judged against him, and then he becomes unable, the master has the choice between handing him over and paying the compensation; because this is the consequence of a slave's crime, but the kitaba prevented the handing over, then the prevention is removed by inability, so the original ruling returns. [1] And if a judgment is made against him while he is a mukatab and then he becomes unable, he is sold for it; because it is a debt attached to his servitude by the judgment, so it transfers to his value. [2] And it does not dissolve upon the death of the master, and he pays the ransom to his heirs according to its installments.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex scenario of a slave who commits a crime before being made a mukatab, and then later becomes unable to fulfill the kitaba. It first discusses a dispute regarding the wala (loyalty) of children born to the slave after the kitaba: if the contract is dissolved, the child is born a slave and wala goes to the mother's masters; if the contract remains and payment is made, the child is born free and wala goes to the father's masters. This is a matter of ijtihad, so the judge's ruling is binding. The text then clarifies that if the master was unaware of the crime at the time of the kitaba, the kitaba acts as a barrier to handing over the slave for the crime. Once the slave becomes unable to pay, the barrier is removed and the original ruling (master's choice to hand over or pay) returns. The passage also covers two sub-cases regarding a mukatab who commits a crime after the contract: if no judgment has been rendered and he becomes unable, the master chooses to hand him over or pay compensation. However, if a judgment has been rendered while he was still a mukatab, the crime becomes a debt against his person; after inability, he is sold to pay it. Finally, the ruling that the death of the master does not void the kitaba is explained: the kitaba is a right of the mukatab, and the death of the master should not nullify another's right. The mukatab continues paying the installments to the heirs. The explanation emphasizes that the kitaba remains valid because it is a right (ḥaqq) of the mukatab.

Key Terms
ولاء (walā'): Legal patronage or loyalty that establishes inheritance and other rights between a freed slave and the former master
جناية (jināyah): A criminal offense that requires compensation (arsh or diyah)
عجز (ʿajz): Inability of the mukatab to continue paying the installments of the kitaba contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a mukatab commits a crime and no judgment is made before he becomes unable to pay, what options does the master have?
A) He must pay the blood money and cannot hand over the slave
B) He may choose to hand over the slave or pay the compensation
C) The slave is freed automatically
D) The judge decides whether to execute the slave

Key Takeaway
When a mukatab becomes unable to pay after committing a crime, the master regains the option to hand over or pay, but if the crime was already judged, the mukatab is sold; the death of the master does not dissolve the kitaba.

Rulings on a Makatib's Crime and Continuation of Kitabah after Master's Death
Commentary on rulings concerning a makatib's janiyah and master's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a makatib who commits a crime and explain why the kitabah continues after the master's death, applying the principles of qiyas and istihsan.

Core Idea
A makatib's crime is treated as a debt; kitabah survives master's death because it is the makatib's right.

A makatib is a slave who has contracted to earn his freedom by paying installments. When he commits a crime, the master's choice depends on timing. Moreover, the death of the master does not void the kitabah. This lesson unpacks these rulings using the Hanafi principles of istihsan and the nature of rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of a makatib's crime
Determine whether a judicial judgment (qada) was passed before the makatib became unable to pay (ajz). This timing decides the master's options.
If no judgment precedes ajz, master chooses; if judgment precedes, makatib is sold.
2
Analyze the legal basis for the master's option
The original rule for a slave's crime is that the master may hand him over or pay compensation. The kitabah initially prevents this, but once ajz removes the kitabah, the original ruling returns.
The kitabah is a preventive factor (mani); ajz removes it.
3
Examine the effect of a judicial judgment
When a judge passes a judgment on the makatib while he is still capable, the liability becomes a debt secured by his person (raqabah). Subsequent ajz triggers the sale of the makatib to satisfy this debt.
The judgment transfers the liability to a debt on the raqabah.
4
Understand the continuation of kitabah after master's death
The kitabah is a right (haqq) of the makatib because it is the means to his freedom. A person's right cannot be voided by another's death. Therefore, the contract continues with the heirs.
Heirs step into the master's role for collecting installments.
5
Distinguish collective vs. partial manumission by heirs
If all heirs release the makatib from the kitabah debt, it is considered an ibra' (release) and he becomes free. This is istihsan, overriding qiyas which would require ownership. Partial manumission is invalid because freeing part of a makatib is impossible and part of the debt does not effect freedom.
Qiyas would reject both; istihsan allows the collective release as an ibra'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وإن قضي به عليه مكاتباً فعجز بيع فيه) : أي وإن قضى بموجب الجناية على المكاتب حال كونه مكاتباً، ثمّ عجز بيع في ذلك ؛ لأنه دين متعلق برقيتِهِ بالقضاء، فانتقل إلى قيمته. ولا تنفسخ بموت السيد، وأدى البدل إلى ورثته على نجومه قوله : وإن قضي به ؛ أي بموجب الجناية عليه ؛ أي على المكاتب في كتابته، ثم عجز، فما قضى به من موجبها دين في ذمته يباع فيه. كذا في «العناية». قوله : ولا تنفسخ ... الخ ؛ يعني أنّ الكتابة حق المكاتب ؛ لأنها سبب حريته ، وحرية حقه فهي سبب حقه، وسبب حق المرء حقه لافضائه إلى حصوله، فالكتابة حقه، فإذا مات المولى لم ينفسخ ؛ لئلا يؤدّي موته إلى إبطال حق غيره. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» قوله : وأدى البدل...الخ ؛ أي قيل له أد البدل إلى ورثته، فيؤدي إلى ورثته على نجومه ؛ لأنه استحق الحريّة بالأداء على النجوم المقررة من المولى، والسبب كذلك فيبقى بهذه الصفة، ولا يتغيّر إلا أنّ الورثة يخلفون المولى في الاستيفاء، بأن أعتقوه جميعاً، أي ابرأوا عن بدل الكتابة، وهذه لا يسمى تغيراً. كتاب المكاتب تصرفاته وعجزه وأحكامه فإن أعتقه بعضهم لا يصح، وإن أعتقوه عتق مجاناً فإن أعتقه بعضُهم لا يصح، وإن أعتقوهُ عُتِق مجاناً) ؛ لأنه لا ينتقل من ملك إلى ملك، فلا يصح اعتاق بعض الورثة ، وأما إعتاق الكل فنجعله إبراء تصحيحاً للعتق ، ولا كذلك إعتاق بعض الورثة ؛ لأنه لا يمكن جعله إبراء للبعض تصحيحاً للعتق ، فإن إبراء البعض لا يصحح العتق ، لأنه لا يعتق شيء بإبراء البعض. قوله : فلا يصح إعتاق بعض الورثة ؛ لأنه لم يملكه ؛ إذ المكاتب لا يملك بسائر أسباب الملك ، كالبيع والهبة، فكذا بالإرث ولاعتق فيما لم يملكه ابن آدم. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» قوله : فنجعله...الخ ؛ يعني أن القياس عدم الجواز في الكل أيضاً ؛ لعدم الملك، ووجه الاستحسان : أن يصير إعتاقهم إبراء عن بدل الكتابة، فإنّهم يملكونه بجريان الإرث فيه، فإذا برئ المكاتب عن جميع البدلِ عُتق، كما إذا أبرأه مولاه، ولما توجه عليه جعل إعتاق أحد الورثة إبراء عن نصيبه. الملك، أجاب عنه بقوله : ولا كذلك إعتاق البعض : يعني أنه لا يصح ذلك ؛ لأنا نجعله إبراء اقتضاء تصحيحاً لعتقه، والعتق لا يثبتُ في المكاتب بإبراء بعض البدل أو أدائه ، لا في بعضه، ولا في كله ؛ لأنَّ عتقه معلق بسقوط جميع البدل. ولهذا لو أبرأ المورث عن بعض البدل لم يعتق شيء منه، وإذا لم يمكن إثبات المقتضى لا يثبتُ المقتضى، فلا وجه لإبراء البعض، وكذا حال إبراء الكلّ لحق بقية الورثة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي) ناقلاً عن «العناية».
If a makatib commits a crime and no judgment is made against him, and then he becomes incapable (of paying the kitabah), (the master) chooses between handing him over (for the crime) or paying the compensation. And if judgment is made against him while he is a makatib, then he becomes incapable, he is sold to (pay) that (debt). And (the kitabah) is not dissolved by the death of the master, and the payment is made to his heirs according to the installments. ... If some of them (the heirs) free him, it is not valid, but if they all free him, he is freed gratuitously. The commentary explains the reasoning.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on rulings from the Hanafi school regarding a mukatab (a slave under a kitabah contract) who commits a criminal offence (janiyah). The commentary clarifies two scenarios: First, if the mukatab commits a crime and before the judge passes a judgment on the criminal liability, the mukatab becomes unable to pay the kitabah (ajz), then the master has a choice: either hand over the slave to the victim for retaliation or pay the compensation (arsh) himself. This is because the original ruling for a slave's crime applies, but the kitabah prevented enforcement; once the kitabah falls due to inability, the preventive factor is removed. Second, if the judge passes the judgment while the mukatab is still capable, then the criminal liability becomes a debt on the slave's dhimmah, secured by his raqabah (person). If he later becomes unable, he is sold to pay that debt, because the debt has been transferred to his value by the judgment. The passage then addresses the death of the master. The kitabah does not dissolve (tanfasikh) upon the master's death because the kitabah is a right (haqq) of the mukatab—it is the means to his freedom, and a person's right cannot be invalidated by the death of another. The mukatab continues to pay the installments to the master's heirs. The heirs stand in the place of the master regarding collection; if they all free him, it is treated as an ibra' (release) of the kitabah debt, leading to his freedom. However, if only some of the heirs free him, it is not valid because they do not own the slave (the mukatab is not owned by them individually due to the kitabah). The commentary explains that the preferred ruling (istihsan) allows the collective manumission as an ibra', but the qiyas (analogy) would reject it due to lack of ownership. The partial manumission fails because freeing part of a mukatab is not possible, and the ibra' of part of the debt does not effect freedom since the mukatab's freedom is contingent on the complete dropping of the entire debt.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a kitabah contract with his master, agreeing to pay a sum in installments in exchange for freedom upon completion.
عجز (ʿajz): Inability of the mukatab to pay the kitabah installments, which leads to the annulment of the kitabah and reversion to full slavery.
جناية (jināyah): A criminal offence committed by the mukatab, which creates liability for compensation (arsh) or retaliation (qisas).
إبراء (ibrāʾ): A legal release or discharge from a debt; in the context of kitabah, if all heirs release the mukatab from the remaining installments, he becomes free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a makatib commits a crime and becomes unable to pay the kitabah before a judgment is made on the crime, what is the ruling?
A) The makatib is sold to pay the compensation.
B) The master chooses between handing over the makatib or paying the compensation.
C) The makatib is automatically freed.
D) The kitabah is invalidated and the makatib becomes a full slave again with no liability.

Key Takeaway
A makatib's crime and kitabah continue after the master's death; heirs collect installments, and only collective manumission by all heirs is valid as an ibra'.

The Ruling of Wala': Belongs to the Emancipator
Opening passage of Kitab al-Wala with definition, types, and ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of wala', identify its two types, and apply the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa' to determine that inheritance is one-sided from freed slave to emancipator only.

Core Idea
Wala' is a legal kinship giving inheritance exclusively to the emancipator; the freed slave does not inherit back.

Wala' (clientage) is a unique legal bond created by emancipation. This lesson unpacks its definition, two main types, and the pivotal ruling that the emancipator alone inherits from the freed slave. You will see how a clear hadith and a careful analogy (qiyas) establish this rule, and why the reverse case is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Define wala' linguistically and technically
Identify the linguistic root (wali = closeness) and the technical meaning: a legal kinship (qarabah hukmiyyah) arising from emancipation or a contract of muwalat, giving rights of inheritance and mutual support.
The text also notes that wala' is named because the inheritance becomes close.
2
Identify the two types of wala'
From the passage, wala' is divided into: (1) wala' al-itaqah (emancipation) and (2) wala' al-muwalat (contract of clientage). Note that the book first discusses wala' al-itaqah.
Wala' al-muwalat is only for non-Arabs or those without agnates; not covered in detail here.
3
State the ruling for wala' al-itaqah
The core ruling: 'Wala' belongs to the one who emancipates.' The freed slave's estate goes to his former master (emancipator).
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Analyze the dalil (evidence)
The hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa' is cited. In Hanafi usul, the preposition 'li' indicates exclusive ownership. Additionally, a qiyas is made: the emancipator gives legal life to the slave, akin to a father giving life to a child, so he inherits like a father.
The qiyas reinforces the ruling but the hadith is the primary proof.
5
Evaluate the opposing view and the Hanafi response
Hasan ibn Ziyad argued that the freed slave should also inherit from the emancipator (reciprocal). The Hanafi response: the emancipator is a stranger, and the hadith is a clear text (nass) that cannot be overridden by analogy.
The text says 'we have a text contrary to analogy, so it cannot be analogized upon it.' This shows the principle that nass prevails over qiyas.

Book of Wala'. It is an inheritance that a person deserves due to emancipating a slave in his ownership, or due to a contract of muwalat (clientage). [1] The Book of Wala'. [2] It is an inheritance that a person deserves due to emancipating a slave in his ownership or due to a contract... [1] His statement: Book of Wala'; he placed the Book of Wala' after the Book of the Mukatab because wala' is one of the effects of kitabah (contract of manumission) by the removal of ownership of the slave upon payment of the kitabah price. So in Nata'ij al-Afkar. [2] His statement: al-Wala'; it is from al-wali meaning closeness, so it is a legal kinship arising from emancipation or muwalat. This is linguistic: one says: waliya al-shay' al-shay' if the second occurs after the first without separation. And in al-Nihayah: it was named wala' al-itaqah and wala' al-muwalat because its ruling, which is inheritance, becomes close and occurs when the condition is met without separation; or from al-muwalat, which is the verbal noun of mufa'alah from al-wilayah with fatha (al-nasrah and al-mahabbah), except that it is specifically in sacred law for wala' of emancipation or wala' of muwalat, and among its effects are mutual support, inheritance, and blood-wit. So in al-Tabyin. [3] His statement: It is an inheritance...etc.; an explanation of its lexical meaning considering its effect and ruling, for its meaning in the terminology of jurists is: mutual support that necessitates inheritance and blood-wit. The author of al-Hidayah explicitly stated in explaining its legal concept and meaning therein: mutual support, then he clarified the existence of mutual support in both types: the Arabs used to support each other through them and through alliance and brotherhood. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, confirmed their mutual support through wala' in both types, saying: 'Verily, the mawla of a people is one of them, and their ally is one of them.' So al-wala' of one who emancipates, whether by direct emancipation, or through his branch, or by owning his relative, then his wala'... so al-wala' is two types: wala' of emancipation and wala' of muwalat. So he began with wala' of emancipation saying: (Whoever emancipates by emancipation or through his branch, like kitabah, tadbir, istilad, or by owning his relative, i.e., by his relative owning him, then his wala') and the meaning of al-halif is mawla al-muwalat because they used to strengthen muwalat by alliance. End. So in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [1] His statement: So al-wala' is two types; meaning al-wala' used in the terminology of sacred law, which is the one by which mutual support occurs, is two types. The introduction of fa on al-wala' makes it explicit that its division into two types is due to the two different causes mentioned, as al-Akmal stated. [2] His statement: Then his wala' is for his master, based on his statement: 'Wala' belongs to the one who emancipates'; and because mutual support is through it, so he (the emancipator) is responsible for his blood-wit, and he has given him (the slave) meaning (life) by removing slavery from him, because the slave is legally dead. Do you not see that many rulings pertaining to the living, such as judgeship, testimony, going to Friday prayer, going out for the two Eid prayers, and the like, are not established for him? Through emancipation, these rulings are established for him, so it is giving meaning (life). And whoever gives meaning (life) to another inherits from him like the child; thus the wala' becomes like lineage. And since the cause of the child's life is the father, the master inherits from him just as the father inherits from the child. If it is said: It would be appropriate for the freed slave to also inherit from the master if the master leaves no agnatic relatives, as is the view of Hasan ibn Ziyad. We say: The emancipator is a stranger to him, and a text has come regarding the emancipator that is contrary to analogy, so it cannot be analogized upon it. Correct due to others without his statement: 'and their ally is one of them', and this chain has weakness due to the unknown status of Isma'il ibn 'Ubayd ibn Rifa'ah, for only Ibn Khuthaym narrated from him.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Wala' (Clientage) in the commentary. It first defines wala' lexically and technically: as a legal inheritance right that arises either from emancipating a slave (wala' al-itaqah) or from a contract of mutual clientage (wala' al-muwalat). The commentator expands on the linguistic root (wali = closeness) and explains that wala' creates a legal kinship leading to mutual support, inheritance, and blood-wit liability. The Prophet's hadith is cited: 'The mawla of a people is one of them, and their ally is one of them' to emphasize this bond. Then the text turns to the specific ruling: the wala' of a freed slave belongs to his emancipator. This is proven by the famous hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa' (Wala' belongs to the one who emancipates) and by an analogy (qiyas): the emancipator gives the slave legal life by removing slavery (the slave is legally dead), just as a father gives life to his child; thus the emancipator inherits from the slave as a father inherits from his child. The passage then addresses an objection attributed to Hasan ibn Ziyad: why shouldn't the freed slave also inherit from the emancipator when the emancipator has no blood relatives? The Hanafi response is that the emancipator is a stranger to the slave, and the ruling for the emancipator is based on a specific textual proof (the hadith) which cannot be extended by analogy. Thus the wala' is not reciprocal; only the emancipator inherits from the freed slave, not vice versa.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-walā’): A legal kinship (qarābah ḥukmiyyah) that arises from emancipation or a contract of mutual clientage, entailing rights of inheritance and mutual support.
عتق (‘itq): Emancipation of a slave from ownership, which creates wala' for the emancipator.
موالاة (muwālāh): A contract of clientage between two free Muslims to establish mutual inheritance and support, recognized in Hanafi law only for non-Arabs or those without agnatic relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence (dalil) used in the Hanafi school to prove that wala' belongs exclusively to the emancipator?
A) Qiyas: the emancipator gives legal life like a father.
B) Ijma' (consensus) of the Companions.
C) The hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa'.
D) The Quranic verse on inheritance.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, wala' from emancipation gives the emancipator exclusive inheritance rights from the freed slave; the freed slave does not inherit from the emancipator.

Two Types of Wala' and the Prophetic Hadith
Defines wala' types and provides proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two types of wala', explain why wala' belongs exclusively to the emancipator using the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa', and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding reciprocal inheritance.

Core Idea
Wala' is a right of inheritance for the emancipator only, not reciprocal.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines the two types of wala' (legal clientage) and provides the primary textual evidence for the Hanafi ruling that wala' belongs exclusively to the emancipator. You will examine the prophetic hadith, the analogy to paternity, and a intra-Hanafi objection from Hasan ibn Ziyad. By the end, you will understand the basis and limits of this important legal bond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of wala'
The passage introduces wala' al-'itaqah (emancipation) and wala' al-muwalah (clientage). Note that the first type arises from direct emancipation or its equivalents (kitabah, tadbir, istilad) or from owning a relative.
These types are distinguished by their cause: emancipation vs. mutual contract.
2
Extract the proof: Hadith and analogy
The primary proof is the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa' (wala' is for the one who emancipates). Supportive analogy: the emancipator gives the slave 'life' by removing legal death, resembling a father giving life to a son.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is definitive (qat'i al-dalala) according to Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Hasan ibn Ziyad argued that the freed slave should also inherit from the master when there are no agnatic heirs, based on analogy of reciprocity. The response: the hadith is a text (nass) that prevents qiyas, and the supporting narration 'wa halifuhum minhum' is weak.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) reject Hasan's view.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that only the master inherits from the freed slave, not vice versa. If the master dies without agnatic heirs, the estate goes to the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal).
This is the ruling of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الولاء/ أحكامه
فالولاء من أعتق بإعتاق أو بفرع له أو بملك قريبه فولاؤه فالولاء نوعان : ولاء العتاقة، وولاء الموالاة. فابتدأ بـولاء العتاقة فقال : (مَن أعتق بإعتاق أو بفرع لـه ) : كالكتابة، والتدبير، والاستيلاد ، أو بملك قريبه ) : أي بمالكية قريبهِ إيَّاه ، (فولاؤه) والمراد بالحليف : مولى الموالاة ؛ لأنهم كانوا يؤكدون الموالاة بالحلف. انتهى. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي»" [١] قوله : فالولاء نوعان ؛ أي الولاء المستعمل في اصطلاح الشرع، وهو الذي يقع به التناصر نوعان، فإيراد الفاء على الولاء صريح أن تنوعه إلى نوعين باختلاف السببين المذكورين كما صرح به الأكمل (۳). [٢] قوله : فولاؤه لسيده ، بقوله : «الولاء لمن أعتق (۳) ؛ ولأنّ التناصر به فيعقله وقد أحياه معنى بإزالة الرق عنه ؛ لأنَّ الرقيق هالك حكماً ، ألا ترى أنه لا يثبت في حقه كثير من الأحكام التي تعلقت بالأحياء نحو القضاء والشهادة والسعي إلى الجمعة والخروج إلى العيدين وأشباه ذلك. وبالإعتاق تثبت هذه الأحكام في حقه، فكان إحياء معنى، ومن أحيا غيره معنى ورثه كالولد، فيصير الولاء كالولاء، ومن حيث أن سبب حياة الولد إنما هو الوالد، فيرثه المولى كما يرث الوالد الولد. فإن قيل : ينبغي أن يرث العبد المعتق من المولى أيضاً إذا لم يترك المولى عصبة نسبية، كما هو قول حسن بن زیاد . قلنا : المعتق أجنبي عنه ، وقد جاء في المعتق نص مخالف للقياس فلا يقاس عليه صحیح لغيره دون قوله : وحليفهم منهم ، وهذا إسناد ضعيف لجهالة إسماعيل بن عبيد بن رفاعة فقد انفرد بالرواية عنه ابن خثيم.
Book of Wala'/Its Rulings. Wala' is for the one who emancipates by means of emancipation or by a branch of his or by owning his relative, so his wala' is... Wala' is of two types: Wala' of emancipation and Wala' of clientage. He began with wala' of emancipation and said: (Whoever emancipates by means of emancipation or by a branch of his) such as kitabah, tadbir, and istilad, or by owning his relative) i.e., by his relative owning him, (then his wala' is). And the intended meaning of al-halif is the client of clientage because they used to emphasize clientage by oath. End. Similarly in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'. He said: Wala' is of two types; i.e., wala' as used in legal terminology, which is the basis of mutual support, is of two types. The introduction of the letter fa on wala' indicates explicitly that its division into two types is due to the two different causes mentioned, as stated by al-Akmal. He said: So his wala' is for his master, by his statement: 'Wala' is for the one who emancipates'; and because mutual support is through it, and he has given him life in a metaphorical sense by removing slavery from him; for the slave is legally dead, do you not see that many rulings pertaining to the living are not established for him, such as judgment, testimony, going to Friday prayer, going to the two Eids, and the like. By emancipation, these rulings become established for him, so it is giving life metaphorically, and whoever gives life to another metaphorically inherits from him like the son, so wala' becomes like lineage; and since the cause of the son's life is the father, the master inherits from him as the father inherits from the son. If it is said: It is fitting that the emancipated slave should also inherit from the master if the master leaves no relative by lineage, as is the view of Hasan ibn Ziyad. We say: The emancipator is a stranger to him, and a text has come regarding the emancipated slave that is contrary to analogy, so it cannot be analogized. Sahih lighayrihi without his statement: 'And their ally is from among them', and this chain is weak due to the unknown status of Isma'il ibn 'Ubayd ibn Rifa'ah, as Ibn Khuthaym was the only one to narrate from him.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa'. It begins by defining the two types of wala': wala' al-'itaqah (emancipation) and wala' al-muwalah (clientage). The author then explains the first type, stating that wala' belongs to the one who emancipates, whether directly, through a branch like kitabah (contractual emancipation), tadbir (posthumous emancipation), or istilad (emancipation of an umm walad), or by owning a relative. The commentary elaborates that wala' is a form of mutual support and that the emancipator gives the slave life in a metaphorical sense by removing the legal death of slavery. The proof is the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa' and a rational analogy to paternity. An objection is raised regarding Hasan ibn Ziyad's view that the emancipated slave should also inherit from the master if there are no agnatic heirs. The response is that the emancipator is a stranger, and the hadith is a text contrary to analogy, so qiyas is not applied. Additionally, the hadith 'And their ally is from among them' is rejected as weak due to a missing link in the chain.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala'): A legal bond of inheritance and mutual support arising from emancipation or clientage.
ولاء العتاقة (wala' al-'itaqah): The wala' that results from direct emancipation of a slave.
ولاء الموالاة (wala' al-muwalah): Wala' through a contract of clientage, used when a freed slave has no relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary textual proof for the Hanafi ruling that wala' belongs exclusively to the emancipator?
A) Analogy to paternal inheritance
B) The hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa'
C) Consensus of the companions
D) The weak narration 'wa halifuhum minhum'

Key Takeaway
Wala' is a right of inheritance exclusively for the emancipator, established by the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa', and it does not give reciprocal inheritance to the freed slave.

Conditions in Wala' and Their Rulings
Addresses conditions in wala', tadbir, istilad, and the case of a slave wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a condition contrary to the requirement of a contract (shart mukhālif li-muqtaḍā al-'aqd) is void in the context of wala', and distinguish the wala' of a child born to an emancipated slave woman within six months.

Core Idea
A condition that negates wala' is void; wala' follows emancipation unconditionally.

Wala' (clientage) is a necessary legal consequence of emancipation. If a master tries to stipulate that wala' will not belong to him, such a condition is invalid because it contradicts the very purpose of the emancipation contract. This lesson explores that principle and its application to specific cases, including tadbir, istilad, and the child of a slave wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
Understand the general principle: any condition that opposes the inherent purpose of a contract is void, but the contract itself remains valid. In wala', the purpose is that the master retains the right of inheritance from the freed slave.
This is a foundational Hanafi maxim.
2
Analyze the objection regarding tadbir and istilad
Consider the objection: how can wala' belong to the master if the mudabbar and umm walad are only freed after the master's death? Trace the text's response through the scenario of apostasy, where emancipation occurs earlier, and then examine the wala' ruling.
This shows how the ruling applies even in delayed emancipation contexts.
3
Examine the specific case of the slave wife
Work through the scenario: a man emancipates a slave woman whose husband is a slave. She gives birth within half a year of emancipation. Determine that the wala' of the child belongs to the mother's master, regardless of the father's later emancipation.
The key is the timing of birth relative to emancipation, not the father's status.
4
Evaluate the evidence from hadith
Look at the hadith from al-Nasa'i about the daughter of Hamza. The Prophet divided the inheritance between the daughter and the emancipator, proving that wala' gives inheritance rights to the master. This supports the Hanafi position.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (qat'i al-dalala).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لسيده وإن شرط عدمه) ، فإنَّ ذلك شرط مخالف لمقتضى العقد فينفذ العقد ويبطل الشرط.
فإن قيل : كيف يكونُ الولاء في التدبير والاستيلاد للسيد، والمدبر وأم الولد إنما يعتقان بعد موتِ السَّيد ؟ قلنا : صورتُهُ : أن يرتد السيد – نعوذ بالله منها – ويلحق بدار الحرب حتى يحكم بعت مدبَّرِه وأمّ ولده ، ثمَّ جَاءَ مسلماً فمات مدبَّرُه أو أم ولده فالولاء له. ( ومن أعتق أمةً زوجها مِن ، فولدت لأقل من نصف حول) : أي من وقتِ الإعتاق، (فله ولاء الولد بلا نقل عنه : أي إن أعتق أبوه لا ينتقل ولاء الولد من غيره. كذا في «العناية»(۱)، و«الكفاية»(۲).
[1] قوله : لسيده ؛ أي المعتق - بالكسر - ، وكذلك المعتقة ؛ لما روى النسائي أنّ بنت حمزة «أعتقت غلاماً لها، ثم مات المعتق - أي الغلام – ، وترك ابنته ، فجعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم المال بينهما نصفين (۳).
ويستوي أيضاً في ثبوتِ الولاء الإعتاق بمال وبغيره، وسواء كان العتق حاصلاً ابتداءً أو بجهة الواجب : كالكفّارة ونحوها؛ لإطلاق ما رويناه سابقاً من قوله : الولاء لمن أعتق»، هكذا يفهم من عبارة «العناية).
[٢] قوله : ومن أعتق أمة زوجها ق.... الخ ؛ صورتها : إذا تزوج عبد رجل أمةً لغيره فأعتق مولى الأمة الأمة، فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإعتاق، فلمولى
For his master even if he stipulates its absence, for that is a condition contrary to the requirement of the contract, so the emancipation is executed and the condition is void. If it is said: How can wala' in tadbir and istilad belong to the master, while the mudabbar and umm walad are only freed after the master's death? We say: Its scenario is that the master apostatizes - we seek refuge in Allah from it - and joins the land of war, so that his mudabbar and umm walad are judged to be free, then he comes back Muslim, and then his mudabbar or umm walad dies, so wala' belongs to him. (And whoever emancipates a slave woman whose husband is a slave, and she gives birth to a child less than half a year) i.e., from the time of emancipation, (then the wala' of the child belongs to him without being transferred elsewhere: i.e., if his father is emancipated, the wala' of the child is not transferred from someone else. Similarly in al-'Inayah and al-Kifayah. [1] He said: For his master; i.e., the emancipator (with a kasra), and likewise the emancipatrice; due to what al-Nasa'i narrated that the daughter of Hamza 'emancipated a slave of hers, then the emancipated one died, leaving a daughter, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) divided the property between them in half.' It is also equal in the establishment of wala' whether the emancipation is by money or otherwise, and whether the emancipation occurs initially or by way of obligation, such as expiation and the like, due to the generality of what we narrated previously from his statement: 'Wala' is for the one who emancipates.' This is understood from the expression of al-'Inayah. [2] He said: And whoever emancipates a slave woman whose husband is a slave... etc.; its scenario: If a slave of a man marries a slave woman belonging to someone else, and then the master of the slave woman emancipates her, and she gives birth to a child less than six months from the time of emancipation, then for the master
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of conditions in wala'. The ruling is that a condition contrary to the nature of the contract (e.g., stipulating that the wala' will not belong to the master) is void, and the wala' still belongs to the master. An objection arises regarding tadbir and istilad: how can wala' belong to the master when the mudabbar and umm walad are only freed after the master's death? The answer is that if the master apostatizes and joins the enemy, they become free immediately upon his joining, and then if they die after his return to Islam, the wala' belongs to him. Then a specific case is given: if a man emancipates a slave woman who is married to a slave, and she gives birth within half a year of emancipation, the wala' of the child belongs to the mother's master, not the father's, even if the father is later emancipated. This is supported by a hadith from al-Nasa'i about the daughter of Hamza. Finally, the text asserts that the method of emancipation (by payment or as expiation) does not affect the establishment of wala'; it applies universally.

Key Terms
شرط مخالف لمقتضى العقد (shart mukhālif li-muqtaḍā al-'aqd): A condition that contradicts the inherent purpose of the contract, e.g., wala' not belonging to the master.
أقل من نصف حول (aqall min niṣf ḥawl): Less than half a year (six months) from the time of emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master emancipates a slave woman and stipulates that her wala' will not belong to him. What is the ruling?
A) The condition is valid, and the wala' goes to the state.
B) The condition is void, and the wala' belongs to the master.
C) The emancipation is invalid because the condition is part of the contract.
D) The slave woman is free but wala' is suspended until a judge decides.

Key Takeaway
A condition that contradicts the essential purpose of emancipation (wala') is void; the emancipation is valid and wala' remains with the master.
