Wala' of a Child Born to a Freed Slave Woman Within Six Months
Commentary on wala' transfer with evidence and detailed scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wala' when a freed slave woman gives birth within six months, apply the distinction between intentional and incidental manumission, and determine whether wala' transfers upon the father's later freedom.

Core Idea
Wala' follows the manumitter; a child born within six months is freed intentionally and its wala' never transfers.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on wala' (clientage). When a female slave is freed while pregnant or gives birth within six months, her child is considered part of her manumission. The master who freed the mother retains the child's wala', and it does not transfer even if the father is later freed. The passage provides the evidence and clarifies the principle of intentional vs incidental manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The ruling concerns wala' of a child born to a freed slave woman within six months of her manumission. The child's wala' belongs to the mother's master, and any condition to the contrary is void.
The ruling is based on the principle that manumission includes all parts of the person, including a fetus.
2
Examine the evidence (dalil)
The hadith of the daughter of Hamza shows that the manumitter inherits a share from the freed slave's estate, establishing that wala' is a right of the manumitter and does not transfer automatically.
The hadith is narrated by al-Nasa'i and is used as proof for the permanent attachment of wala' to the manumitter.
3
Understand the principle of intentional vs incidental manumission
If the child is freed intentionally (as part of the mother's manumission because it was inside her at the time), its wala' never transfers. If the child is freed incidentally (e.g., freed separately after birth and later the father is freed), then the father can drag (jar) the wala' to his own patrons.
The key is whether the child was included in the mother's manumission (intentional) or freed independently (incidental).
4
Apply to specific scenarios
Apply the principle to cases: twins where one is born within six months and the other after; mother and child freed together; mother freed while pregnant. In all cases, if the child is deemed intentionally freed (due to birth within six months), wala' stays with the mother's master.
The passage details these scenarios to illustrate the consistency of the rule.
5
Derive the mufta bihi
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that wala' remains with the mother's master and does not transfer, even if the father is later freed. Any condition to the contrary is void.
This is the established Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن أعتق أمةً زوجها قِنْ، فولدت لأقل من نصف حول فله ولاء الولد بلا نقل عنه. لسيده وإن شرط عدمه، فإنَّ ذلك شرط مخالف لمقتضى العقد فينفذ العتق ويبطل الشرط. ومن أعتق أمة زوجها قن، فولدت لأقل من نصف حول: أي من وقت الإعتاق، فله ولاء الولد بلا نقل عنه: أي إن أعتق أبوه لا ينتقل ولاء الولد من غيره. كذا في «العناية» و«الكفاية». قوله: لسيده؛ أي المعتق - بالكسر -، وكذلك المعتقة؛ لما روى النسائي أنّ بنت حمزة «أعتقت غلاماً لها، ثم مات المعتق - أي الغلام –، وترك ابنته، فجعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم المال بينهما نصفين». ويستوي أيضاً في ثبوتِ الولاء الإعتاق بمال وبغيره، وسواء كان العتق حاصلاً ابتداءً أو بجهة الواجب: كالكفّارة ونحوها؛ لإطلاق ما رويناه سابقاً من قوله: الولاء لمن أعتق»، هكذا يفهم من عبارة «العناية». وكذا لو ولدت ولدين أحدهما لأقل من ذلك: أي ولدت الأمة المعتقةُ الأمة ولاء الولد لا ينتقل الولاء عن مولى الأمة إلى مولى العبد إن أعتق عبده؛ لأنّ الحمل كان موجوداً وقت الإعتاق، فإعتاقه وقع قصداً فلا ينتقل ولاؤه من معتقه. قوله: وقع قصداً؛ إذ هو جزء منها يقبلُ الإعتاق مقصوداً، قال أخــي: اعلم أنّ مسألةَ جرّ الولاء وتميّز مواضع الجرّ عن غيرها من مهمات هذا المقام. والأصل في ذلك: إنّ العتق إذا وقع على الولد مقصوداً لا ينتقل ولاؤه أبداً، وإن وقع تبعاً ثم أعتق الأب جرّ ولاء ابنه إلى مواليه. وعلى هذا إذا أعتق الرجلُ أمةً وولدها عتقاً وولاؤهما له، فإن أُعْتِقَ الأب بعد ذلك لا يجرُّ ولائه، لأنه لما كان منفصلاً عن الأم كان مملوكاً لمولى الأم، والعتق تناوله مقصوداً لا تبعاً، فلا يتبع أحداً. وإذا أعتقت الأم وهي حامل، أو أعتقت وولدت بعد العتق لأقل من ستة أشهر، أو ولدت أحد التوأمين لأقل من ستَّةِ أشهر بيوم، ثم أعتق الأب رجل آخر، فكذلك لا ينتقل الولاء إلى موالي الأب؛ لأنّ المولى قصد إعتاق الأم، والقصد إليها بالإعتاق قصداً إلى جميع أجزائها، والحمل جزء منها. فإن كان الحمل ظاهراً وقت الإعتاق فواضح، وإن ولدت لأقل من ستَّةِ أشهر حصل التعيين؛ لقيامه فيه، وكذا إذا ولدت أحد التوأمين؛ لأنهما معلّقان معاً، هذا تفصيل ما أجمله الشارح رحمه الله متقوياً بتقوية العناية. انتهى.
And whoever frees a female slave whose husband is a male slave, and she gives birth within less than half a year [from the time of manumission], then the wala' of the child belongs to him without transfer from him. [The ruling] is for her master even if he stipulated otherwise, for such a condition contradicts the requirement of the contract, so the manumission is executed and the condition is void. And whoever frees a female slave whose husband is a male slave, and she gives birth within less than half a year, meaning from the time of manumission, then the wala' of the child is his without transfer from him, meaning that even if his father is freed, the wala' of the child does not transfer from [the mother's master] to another. Thus in al-`Inayah and al-Kifayah. His statement: 'for her master' means the manumitter (with kasr), and likewise the woman manumitter; because al-Nasa'i narrated that the daughter of Hamza freed a male slave of hers, then the manumitted (i.e., the male slave) died, leaving a daughter, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) divided the property between them equally. Also, in establishing wala', manumission with payment and without it are equal, and whether the manumission occurs initially or by way of obligation, such as expiation and the like, due to the generality of what we narrated earlier of his statement: 'Wala' belongs to the one who frees.' This is understood from the expression of al-`Inayah. And likewise if she gives birth to two children, one of them within less than that, i.e., the freed female slave gives birth to a child [and the other after], the wala' of the child does not transfer from the master of the female slave to the master of the male slave if he frees his male slave, because the pregnancy existed at the time of manumission, so his manumission occurred intentionally, so his wala' does not transfer from his manumitter. His statement: 'occurred intentionally' because it is a part of her that is capable of being manumitted intentionally. My brother said: Know that the issue of dragging wala' (jar al-wala') and distinguishing the places of dragging from others is among the important matters of this topic. The principle in that is: If manumission falls upon the child intentionally, his wala' never transfers; but if it falls incidentally, then if the father is later freed, he drags the wala' of his son to his own patrons. On this basis, if a man frees a female slave and her child together, and their wala' belongs to him, then if the father is freed afterwards, he does not drag his wala', because when he was separate from the mother, he was owned by the mother's master, and manumission reached him intentionally, not incidentally, so he does not follow anyone. And if the mother is freed while pregnant, or she is freed and gives birth after the manumission within less than six months, or she gives birth to one of the twins within less than six months by a day, then another man frees the father, similarly the wala' does not transfer to the father's patrons because the master intended to free the mother, and intention towards her in manumission is intention towards all her parts, and the fetus is a part of her. If the pregnancy was apparent at the time of manumission, it is obvious; and if she gives birth within less than six months, specification occurs due to its existence in her; likewise if she gives birth to one of the twins, because both are attached together. This is the detail of what the commentator, may Allah have mercy on him, summarized, supported by the strengthening of al-`Inayah. End.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding the wala' of a child born to a freed slave woman within six months of her manumission. The matn states that if a man frees a female slave who is married to a male slave, and she gives birth within less than half a year (six months) from the time of manumission, the wala' of the child belongs to the master (the one who freed the mother) and does not transfer to anyone else, even if the father is later freed. The commentator explains that if the manumitter stipulates otherwise, the condition is void because it contradicts the nature of the contract (the wala' automatically follows the manumitter). The proof is the hadith from al-Nasa'i where the Prophet divided the estate between the daughter of the freed slave and the manumitter's heir, showing that wala' is based on the manumission relationship. The commentary also clarifies that the ruling applies whether the manumission is for payment or not, and whether it is voluntary or obligatory (like expiation). The passage then addresses a more complex case: if the woman gives birth to twins, one before six months and the other after, the wala' of the first remains with the mother's master, while the second's wala' may transfer if the father is freed later. The concept of 'jar al-wala' (dragging wala') is discussed: if the child is freed intentionally, his wala' does not transfer; if freed incidentally (as part of freeing the mother), and then the father is freed, the father can drag the child's wala' to his own patrons. The commentator emphasizes that because the mother's manumission includes the fetus as a part of her, the child's wala' stays with her master if born within six months, as the intention of manumission covered the child.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): The right of inheritance and clientage that the manumitter holds over the freed slave; it is a legal bond resembling lineage in terms of inheritance rights.
جر الولاء (jar al-wala'): The transfer of wala' from the mother's master to the father's master when the father is freed after the child's initial manumission, provided the child's freedom was incidental.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the correct Hanafi ruling on wala' of a child born to a freed slave woman within six months?
A) The wala' belongs to the father's master if the father is later freed.
B) The wala' belongs to the mother's master and does not transfer.
C) The child has no wala' until it is independently freed.
D) The wala' is divided equally between the mother's and father's masters.

Key Takeaway
The wala' of a child born to a freed slave woman within six months belongs permanently to the mother's master and cannot be transferred, even if the father is later freed.

Manumission of a Pregnant Slave and the Patronage of Her Child
Explanation of patronage rules for children born to freed slave women, with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the transfer of patronage (wala) when a pregnant slave is freed, apply the six-month pregnancy presumption, and explain the legal principle behind 'jarr al-wala'.

Core Idea
Patronage follows the father when he becomes free, because wala is like lineage—and lineage is to the father.

This passage from a super-commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies a nuanced area of Hanafi fiqh: how the patronage (wala) of a child is affected when its mother is freed while pregnant. The key distinction is whether the child was conceived before or after the mother's manumission, measured by the six-month minimum pregnancy period. If the child is born within six months of manumission, it is considered freed as part of the mother and its wala is fixed with her patron. If born after six months, the child is freed as a consequence, and if the father later becomes free, he can 'draw' the child's wala to his own patrons. The hadith 'Patronage is a bond like the bond of lineage' is the core proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and ruling
Read the passage and determine the basic scenario: a pregnant slave woman is freed. Two possibilities exist: the child is born within six months of her manumission, or after six months. State the ruling for each case regarding the child's patronage (wala).
The six-month period is the minimum duration of pregnancy (nisf al-hawl).
2
Trace the pregnancy timeline and its effect
Note that if the child is born within six months, the pregnancy is deemed to have existed at the time of manumission, so the child is freed intentionally as part of the mother. If born after six months, the child was not conceived before manumission, so it is freed as a consequence (tab'an).
For twins, if one is born within six months, both are considered existing at manumission.
3
Understand the concept of 'jarr al-wala' (drawing of patronage)
When the child is born after six months (or after the mother's freedom), its wala initially goes to the mother's patron. But if the father, who was a slave, is later freed, he 'draws' the wala to his own patrons. This is because patronage is analogous to lineage, and lineage is through the father.
The father must be freed after the child's birth for the drawing to occur.
4
Locate the hadith proof and explain its application
The passage cites the hadith: 'Patronage is a bond like the bond of lineage; it is not sold, nor given as a gift, nor inherited.' Explain how this hadith supports the transfer: just as lineage is traced through fathers, so too should patronage. The mother's patron only held wala due to the father's lack of capacity (slavery).
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear and accepted.
5
Address the objection and the Hanafi response
The passage ends with an objection: if wala is like lineage, it cannot be annulled once established. The Hanafi response is that the transfer is not an annulment but a return to the original cause. The father's wala was merely deferred; upon his freedom, it returns naturally.
This response distinguishes between rescinding a right and reasserting a latent one.

My brother said: know that the issue of drawing patronage and distinguishing the places of drawing from others is among the important matters of this topic. The principle in this is: if manumission occurs on the child intentionally, his patronage never transfers, but if it occurs as a consequence, then if the father is freed, he draws the patronage of his son to his patrons. And upon this, if a man frees a slave woman and her child as a manumission, their patronage is his, then if the father is freed after that, he does not draw his patronage, because since he was separated from the mother, he was owned by the mother's patron, and manumission reached him intentionally, not as a consequence, so he does not follow anyone. If the pregnancy was apparent at the time of manumission, it is obvious; and if she gives birth in less than six months, specification occurs because of its existence therein; similarly if she gives birth to one of the twins; because they are both attached together. [2] His statement: so likewise the other; because the twins are attached together. [3] His statement: then if she gives birth after more than that; meaning if she gives birth after her manumission after more than six months to a child, his patronage belongs to the mother's patrons; because he was freed as a consequence of the mother due to his connection to her after her manumission, so he follows her in patronage, and it is not certain of his existence at the time of manumission; because he was born after more than six months after manumission, so that he is freed intentionally. [4] His statement: then if the father is freed... etc.; meaning if the father's master frees him, the father draws the patronage of his son, so the patronage transfers from the mother's patrons to the father's patrons; because patronage is in the position of lineage. He said: "Patronage is a bond like the bond of lineage; it is not sold, nor given as a gift, nor inherited." Then lineage is to fathers, so likewise patronage, and the attribution to the mother's patrons was due to the father's lack of qualification necessarily, so when he becomes qualified through manumission, patronage returns to him. If it is said: patronage is like lineage, and lineage does not allow annulment after its establishment, so likewise patronage must not be annulled after its establishment.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) on the commentary of Al-Wiqayah, explaining the rules of patronage (wala) when a pregnant slave woman is freed. It is a ruling with a proof (hadith) and includes a response to an objection. The passage clarifies that if a slave woman is freed while pregnant, the child born within six months of her manumission is considered to have been freed as part of her, so his patronage belongs to her patron and cannot be transferred even if the father is later freed. For twins, if one is born within six months and the other after, the first follows the mother's patron and the second follows the father's patron if he is freed. If the child is born after six months, he is freed as a consequence of the mother (since he was not conceived before her manumission), so his patronage initially belongs to the mother's patron, but if the father is later freed, he draws the son's patronage to his own patrons. This transfer is justified by the principle that patronage is like lineage: lineage is traced through fathers, so when the father becomes a free man (and thus capable of having patrons), the child's patronage returns to him. The passage ends with an objection: if patronage is like lineage, it cannot be rescinded. The response is that the transfer is not a rescission but a return to the original cause; the father's patronage was merely deferred due to his slavery, and upon his liberation, it reverts.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala): Patronage/clientage: a legal relationship of loyalty and inheritance between a freed slave and his manumitter.
جرّ الولاء (jarr al-wala): Drawing of patronage: the transfer of a child's wala from the mother's patron to the father's patron upon the father's manumission.
نصف الحول (nisf al-hawl): Half a year, specifically six months, used as the maximum period of pregnancy for the child to be considered as existing at the time of mother's manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A freed slave woman gives birth to a child 5 months after her manumission. Later, the child's father (a slave) is freed. What happens to the child's patronage (wala)?
A) The child's wala remains with the mother's patron; it does not transfer to the father's patron.
B) The child's wala transfers to the father's patron because he is now free.
C) The child has no wala because the mother was freed.
D) The child's wala belongs to the state.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, if a child is born more than six months after the mother's manumission, the father can draw the child's wala to his own patrons upon his own manumission, based on the hadith that wala is like lineage.

Transfer of Wala' upon Father's Emancipation
Ruling with proof on wala' transfer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father's emancipation transfers his child's wala' from the mother's patrons to the father's patrons, and explain the Hanafi proof for this ruling.

Core Idea
Wala' transfers to the father when he becomes eligible, like a closer 'asaba supersedes a distant one.

This passage addresses a subtle scenario: a child is born to an emancipated slave woman, so the child's wala' belongs to the mother's master. Later, the child's father (himself a slave) is freed. Does the wala' shift to the father's patron? According to the Hanafi school, it does—based on the principle that wala' is akin to lineage, and lineage is traced through the father. The father's initial incapacity (slavery) was a temporary obstacle; once removed, the wala' reverts. The passage provides textual proof and an analogy to inheritance among 'asaba heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
Note that a child born more than six months after the mother's emancipation has wala' to the mother's master, because the child was not present at the time of her freedom and its wala' follows the mother's status.
This is the baseline scenario before the father's freedom.
2
Recognize the father's initial incapacity
The father, being a slave at the child's birth, lacks the legal capacity to hold wala' (he cannot be a patron). Therefore, the wala' attaches to the mother's side by necessity.
Slavery is an obstacle (mani') to patron status.
3
Understand the trigger for transfer
When the father later emancipates his own master, he becomes eligible to be a patron. The wala' of his child then transfers to his own patron (the father's emancipator).
The transfer is automatic upon fulfillment of conditions.
4
Analyze the legal analogy
The hadith states that wala' is a bond like lineage (nasab). Lineage is traced through fathers, so wala' likewise follows the father. The father's earlier incapacity was akin to the absence of a close relative; when he becomes capable, the wala' shifts, just as a closer 'asaba heir takes precedence over a distant one without invalidating the distant one's status.
The analogy addresses the objection that wala', once fixed, cannot be rescinded.
5
Apply the timing conditions
The transfer occurs only if the father emancipates before the child dies, and the child dies after the father. If the father emancipates after the child's death, the mother's patron's right is already fixed and cannot be transferred. If the child dies before the father's emancipation, the father inherits as an 'asaba, so wala' does not go to the father's patron.
These conditions prevent unjustified disruption of established rights.

Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the transfer of wala' (clientage) when a father who was originally a slave is later emancipated. The matn states the core ruling: if an emancipated slave woman gives birth after more than six months from her emancipation, the child's wala' belongs to the mother's master. Then if the father later emancipates himself (by freeing his own master), he draws his son's wala' to his own patron. The commentary explains that this is because wala' is like lineage (nasab), and lineage is traced through the father. The father was initially ineligible to have wala' because he was a slave, so the wala' attached to the mother's patrons by necessity. When he becomes eligible through emancipation, the wala' returns to his side. An objection is raised: if wala' is like lineage, it should not be rescindable after being established. The response uses the analogy of a brother who is an 'asaba but is superseded by a closer relative; his right is not invalidated, merely preempted. The commentary further specifies timing conditions: the father must emancipate before the child's death, and the child must die after the father's death for the wala' to transfer to the father's patron. The hadith quoted in the footnote establishes the principle that wala' is a bond akin to lineage and cannot be traded.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-walā'): The right of clientage that arises from manumission; a legal bond between the emancipator and the emancipated person, carrying inheritance rights.
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage or kinship through paternal descent.
لحمة (luḥmah): A bond or tie, as strong as the bond of blood kinship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when does the wala' of a child born to an emancipated mother transfer from the mother's patrons to the father's patrons?
A) Immediately upon the father's emancipation, regardless of timing.
B) When the father emancipates before the child's death and the child dies after the father's death.
C) Only if the child was born before the mother's emancipation.
D) It never transfers; wala' is fixed once established.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that wala' transfers to the father's patron when the father becomes eligible, provided the father predeceases the child, because wala' is akin to lineage and the father's initial incapacity was a temporary necessity.

Wala' of Child When Mother is Mu'taqa and Father is Ajami
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' assignment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default ruling on wala' of a child born to a mu'taqa mother and ajami father, and identify the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf regarding when the father has Islamic ancestors.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the child's wala' follows the mother's patron unless the father has stronger claim through Arab lineage or Islamic ancestors.

Wala' (patronage) determines inheritance and legal responsibility. When an emancipated woman (mu'taqa) marries a free non-Arab ('ajami) who himself has no patron, whose wala' does the child belong to? The default position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani gives it to the mother's patron. However, Abu Yusuf introduces a nuance based on the father's lineage. This lesson unpacks that ikhtilaf and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The case: a free 'ajami (non-Arab) who has no patron of his own marries an emancipated woman (mu'taqa). They have a child. Who owns the child's wala'?
The father may or may not have wala' muwalat (alliance), but he is not a patron from emancipation.
2
State the default ruling (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the child's wala' belongs to the mother's patron: 'فولاء ولدها لمولاها'.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Identify the key variable: father's ancestry
The passage from al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyya introduces variables: (1) If the father is Arab, wala' goes to his people. (2) If the father is 'ajami with ancestors in Islam, Abu Yusuf rules wala' goes to his people.
This shows that the default is not absolute.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's dissenting view
Abu Yusuf holds that if the father has Islamic ancestors, the wala' of the child goes to the father's people (قوم الأب). This is 'without doubt' according to the source.
Later scholars differ on whether this is accepted; some narrations follow Abu Yusuf.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference affects inheritance rights and liability for blood money (diyah). If the father's claim is accepted, the father's clan inherits and bears responsibility; otherwise, the mother's patron does.
In fatwa, Abu Yusuf's view is often preferred when the father has Islamic ancestors.

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the wala' of a child born to an emancipated woman (mu'taqa) who marries a free non-Arab ('ajami) who is not a patron himself (i.e., he has not freed anyone, but may have a wala' muwalat). The matn states that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the child's wala' belongs to the mother's patron. This is the default ruling when the father is not a patron. The commentary then elaborates on the definition of 'ajami and cites al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyya to outline different scenarios based on the father's status: (1) If the father is an Arab, the wala' goes to his people (Arab lineage is stronger). (2) If the father is an 'ajami with ancestors in Islam, then Abu Yusuf rules that the wala' goes to the father's people without doubt, while on the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, there is a difference among later scholars: some (like Abu Bakr al-A'mash) say it goes to the father's people, others say to the mother's. (3) If the father is a new Muslim from a warlike tribe, that is the case of the original text. (4) If the father is a slave or mukatab, the text implies a different ruling. The passage thus shows that the ruling is not monolithic even within the Hanafi school, depending on the father's social and legal status.

Key Terms
العجمي (al-'ajami): A non-Arab, even if fluent in Arabic; specifically refers to a free person not of Arab lineage.
ولاء الموالاة (walā' al-muwālāt): Wala' of alliance; a form of clientage established by mutual agreement for protection, not from manumission.
معتقة (mu'taqah): An emancipated female slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the default wala' of a child follows the mother's patron, but this changes when the father has Arab lineage or Islamic ancestors, reflecting a nuanced ikhtilaf within the school.

Child's Wala' in Mixed Parentage: Father with Muwalat, Mother Freed
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' of a child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the child's wala' in this mixed parentage scenario, identify the thamarah al-khilaf, and apply the tarjih to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Emancipation wala' outranks muwalat wala', even from the mother's side — unless the father is Arab or free.

This lesson tackles a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: a child born to a non-Arab father who has wala' muwalat (clientage by contract) and a mother who is a freed slave. Who gets the child's wala' — the father's master or the mother's? The answer turns on the relative strength of wala' types and the father's status. We will unpack the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf, their proofs, and the practical difference in inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Scenario
The passage presents a specific case: a free non-Arab man who has a wala' muwalat (clientage) marries a freed slave woman (mu‘taqah). She bears a child. The question: to whom does the child's wala' belong?
The father himself is free but non-Arab; he has not freed anyone, but he may have a muwalat master.
2
State the Two Hanafi Positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the child's wala' goes to the mother's master (the one who freed her). Abu Yusuf holds that it goes to the father's master (through muwalat).
The passage explicitly says: 'وهما رجحا ولاء العتاقة وإن كان من جانب الأم' (The two preferred the wala' of emancipation even if from the mother's side) and 'عند أبي يوسف فولاؤه لمولى الأب موالاة' (At Abu Yusuf, his wala' is for the father's master as muwalat).
3
Analyze the Proofs (Dalil)
Abu Hanifa/Muhammad rely on the strength of wala' al-itaq (emancipation) over wala' al-muwalat. Abu Yusuf gives preference to the father's side (tarjih li janab al-ab). The passage also notes that if the father is a slave (essentially dead), even Abu Yusuf would concede the child's wala' to the mother's master.
The proof for the majority includes the analogy that a slave father is 'dead in effect' (halik ma‘nan) so the child is fatherless, necessitating the mother's wala'.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
The difference matters when the child dies without asabah. According to Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, inheritance goes to the mother's master or his asabah. According to Abu Yusuf, it goes to the child's dhawil arham (relatives through females).
The passage cites Al-Tabyin: 'ثمرة الاختلاف يظهر فيما إذا مات هذا الولد وترك عمة أو غيرها من ذوي الأرحام ومعتق أمه أو عصبة معتقها'.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih (Preferment)
The text indicates that the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is preferred (wa huma rajaha). This is the position relied upon in the matn and in later Hanafi fatwa.
The passage says: 'وهما رجحا ولاء العتاقة' — they gave preference to emancipation wala'.

Book of Wala'/Its Rulings. A non-Arab who has a wala' muwalat master married a freed slave woman, and she gave birth. Then the wala' of her child belongs to her master. We say: It does not dissolve, but the new claim takes precedence, just as you say about the brother: He is an asabah, so when someone more entitled arises in inheritance, his asabah is not nullified, but the more entitled is given precedence. Thus in some glosses of Al-Hidayah. His statement: 'A non-Arab...' The non-Arab is the opposite of the Arab, even if he is eloquent. Thus in Al-Mughrib. The scenario: a free non-Arab who has not freed anyone, whether he has a wala' muwalat for someone or not, as in Al-Zad and Sharh Al-Aqta'. In Al-Fawa'id Al-Dhahiriyyah: This issue has several aspects: If she married herself to an Arab, then the wala' of children goes to the father's people in their view, because nobility through Arab causes is stronger. If she married herself to a non-Arab who has ancestors in Islam, then the wala' of children goes to the father's people according to Abu Yusuf without doubt, but according to the two of them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) the scholars differed: it is reported from Abu Bakr al-A'mash and Abu Bakr al-Saffar that it is for the father's people. Others said it is for the mother's people. If she married herself to a man who embraced Islam from the people of war and affiliated himself (wala') to someone or not, this is the issue of the book. If she married herself to a slave or a mukatab. As for according to Abu Yusuf, his wala' belongs to the father's master through muwalat, giving preference to the father's side. The two of them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) preferred the wala' of emancipation even if from the mother's side. He only framed the issue in that the wala' of the child is for the mother's master by consensus, except if the father becomes free, then the wala' transfers from the mother's masters to his people. This is the gist of Al-Kifayah. His statement: 'As for according to Abu Yusuf...' The consequence of the difference appears when this child dies, leaving a paternal aunt or other dhawil arham, and the mother's emancipator or his asabah. According to the two, the wealth goes to the mother's emancipator or his asabah; according to Abu Yusuf, it goes to the dhawil arham. Thus in Al-Tabyin. His statement: 'His wala' belongs to the father's master': so he does not have wala' of emancipation, and his wealth is inherited among his dhawil arham, just as if the father is an Arab and the mother is a freed slave, then his wala' is not for the mother's master, giving preference to the father's side, contrary to when the father is a slave, because he is essentially non-existent. So the objection is not sustained that since lineage is to fathers, the free father and slave father should be equal. It is not so, because we say the slave is essentially non-existent. This meaning is known if the father is free, because freedom is life in terms of the attribute of ownership, and the Arab and non-Arab are equal in this. We only say the slave is essentially non-existent because he owns nothing, and because slavery is an effect of disbelief, and disbelief is a legal death, as Allah said: 'Is one who was dead and We gave him life' meaning a disbeliever and We guided him. So this child becomes as if he has no father, so he is attributed to the mother's masters by necessity. This is the gist of Al-'Inayah and Nata'ij al-Afkar. His statement: 'And the two preferred...' because emancipation is strong and considered in rulings even
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the wala' (patronage/clientage) of a child born to a non-Arab (عجمي) father who has a wala' muwalat (voluntary clientage) and a mother who is a freed slave (معتقة). The author begins by stating the ruling: the child's wala' belongs to the mother's master (the one who freed her). This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The commentator then expands on the reasoning, explaining that the father's claim does not nullify the mother's wala' but is merely superseded. The commentary clarifies that a 'non-Arab' here means a free man who is not an Arab, whether he has a wala' muwalat or not. Several scenarios are distinguished: if the father is an Arab, the child's wala' goes to the father's people due to the strength of Arab nobility. If the father is a non-Arab with ancestors in Islam, Abu Yusuf holds the wala' to the father's people without doubt, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's view is debated among later scholars. The core dispute is that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad give precedence to the wala' of emancipation (ولاء العتاقة) even if it comes from the mother's side, whereas Abu Yusuf favors the father's side (الولاء موالاة). The practical consequence (ثمرة الاختلاف) is who inherits from the child if he dies without asabah: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the mother's master or his asabah inherit; according to Abu Yusuf, the child's dhawil arham (uterine relatives) inherit. The commentator also addresses an objection about a slave father, arguing that a slave is 'essentially non-existent' (هالك معنى) because he owns nothing and slavery is akin to legal death, as supported by the Quranic verse (6:122). Thus, when the father is a slave, the child is effectively fatherless and his wala' goes to the mother's masters by necessity. The passage ends with the assertion that emancipation is strong and effective in legal rulings.

Key Terms
ولاء الموالات (walā' al-muwālāt): A type of clientage established through a contract of mutual alliance between a free man and another, not involving emancipation.
معتقة (mu‘taqah): A female slave who has been freed, either by her master or by someone else.
عجمي (‘ajamī): A non-Arab person, regardless of linguistic ability.
ثمرة الاختلاف (thamarat al-ikhtilāf): The practical legal consequence that arises from a difference of opinion among scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school, to whom does the wala' of a child belong when the father is a non-Arab with wala' muwalat and the mother is a freed slave?
A) To the father's master (through muwalat)
B) To the mother's master (through emancipation)
C) To the father's relatives (asabah)
D) To the mother's relatives (dhawil arham)

Key Takeaway
In a mixed parentage case, the child's wala' follows the stronger emancipation wala' (mother's side) over muwalat (father's side), unless the father is an Arab or free from slavery.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Wala' of Child of Free Arab Father and Freed Mother
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' of child of free Arab father and freed mother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on this wala' issue, explain the thamarah al-khilaf, and apply the rule that the manumitter becomes 'asbah.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the child's wala' belongs to the mother's manumitter, but Abu Yusuf gives it to the father's mawali.

This lesson examines a notable intra-Hanafi dispute: to whom does the wala' (patronage) of a child born to a free Arab father and a freed slave mother belong? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the mother's manumitter inherits the wala', while Abu Yusuf gives it to the father's mawali. The dispute has practical consequences when the child dies without natural heirs, and the lesson also establishes the general principle that the manumitter inherits as an 'asbah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
First, determine the two opposing views: Abu Hanifa & Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf. Note the reasoning: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prioritise the wala' of 'itaqah (manumission) because it is strong, while Abu Yusuf prioritises the father's side because of lineage.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad are considered the majority of the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Understand the practical difference: if the child dies leaving a paternal aunt (or other distant relatives) and the mother's manumitter, under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad the inheritance goes to the mother's manumitter, under Abu Yusuf to the father's mawali or distant relatives.
This is a clear example of how a legal difference changes the outcome.
3
Analyse the Hadith on 'Asbah
The text cites a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) tells the manumitter that if the freed slave dies without an heir, the manumitter is his 'asbah. Identify the operative phrase 'you are his 'asbah' and its implication.
This hadith is used to establish that the manumitter inherits as a residuary heir.
4
Distinguish Between 'Itaqah and Muwalat
Note that wala' al-'itaqah (manumission) arises from freeing a slave, while wala' al-muwalat (clientage) is a contract. The Hanafi school considers 'itaqah stronger, and Arabs generally do not need muwalat because of their tribal structure.
This distinction is key to understanding why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad favour the mother's side.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عند أبي يوسف فولاؤه لمولى الأب موالاة؛ ترجيحاً لجانب الأب، وهما رجحا ولاء العتاقة وإن كان من جانب الأم، وإنما وضع المسألة في ولاء الولد لمولى الأم إجماعاً إلا إذا أعتق الأب فيجر الولاء من موالي الأم إلى قومه. قوله: وأما عند أبي يوسف الله .. الخ؛ ثمرة الاختلاف يظهر فيما إذا مات هذا الولد وترك عمة أو غيرها من ذوي الأرحام، ومعتق أمه أو عصبة معتقها كان المالُ للمعتق أمه أو عصبته عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف له يكون لذوي الأرحام. قوله: فولاؤه لمولى الأب؛ فلا يكون عليه ولاء عتاقة، وإنما يورث ماله بين ذوي أرحامه، كما إذا كان الأب عربياً والأمّ معتقة، فإنّه لا يكون ولاؤه لمولى أمه ترجيحاً لجانب الأب، بخلاف ما إذا كان الأب عبداً؛ لأنه هالك معنى. قوله: المعتق عصبة؛ لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم لمن اشترى عبداً فأعتقه: «هو أخوك ومولاك، إن شكرك فهو خير له وشرّ لك، وإن كفرك فهو خير لك وشر له، ولو مات ولم يترك وارثاً كنت أنت عصبته»، وورث ابنة حمزة على سبيل العصوبة مع قيام وارث وهو بنت الميت.
As for according to Abu Yusuf, his wala' goes to the mawali of the father by muwalat, giving preponderance to the father's side, while they (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) gave preponderance to the wala' of manumission even if it is from the mother's side. And the issue is placed only on the wala' of the child to the mawali of the mother by consensus except if the father manumits, then he draws the wala' from the mawali of the mother to his own people. He said: 'As for according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah...' the practical consequence appears if this child dies leaving a paternal aunt or other distant relatives, and the manumitter of his mother or the 'asabah of her manumitter, the wealth goes to the manumitter of his mother or his 'asabah according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and according to Abu Yusuf, it goes to the distant relatives. He said: 'His wala' is to the mawali of the father'; so there is no wala' of manumission upon him, rather his wealth is distributed among his distant relatives, as if the father is an Arab and the mother is a freedwoman, then his wala' does not go to the mawali of his mother because of giving preponderance to the father's side, contrary to if the father is a slave, because he is dead in meaning. He said: 'The manumitter is an 'asbah'; because of his statement (peace be upon him) to the one who bought a slave and freed him: 'He is your brother and your mawla, if he is grateful to you, it is good for him and evil for you, and if he is ungrateful to you, it is good for you and evil for him, and if he dies and leaves no heir, you are his 'asbah.' And he inherited the daughter of Hamza upon the basis of 'usubiyyah while there was an heir, the daughter of the deceased.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the wala' (patronage) of a child born to a free Arab father and a mother who is a freed slave (mawlat). The dispute centers on whether the child's wala' belongs to the mother's manumitter (by 'itaqah) or to the father's mawali (by muwalat). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that the wala' of 'itaqah (manumission) is stronger, so it remains with the mother's mawali even though it comes from the mother's side. They argue that the father's side is weak because the father is an Arab and Arabs do not typically have muwalat; their lineages are preserved, so muwalat is unnecessary. Abu Yusuf, however, prioritizes the father's side, saying the wala' goes to the father's mawali by muwalat. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) appears if the child dies leaving no close relatives but a paternal aunt (or other dhawi al-arham) and the mother's manumitter (or his 'asabah). According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the inheritance goes to the mother's manumitter or his 'asabah; according to Abu Yusuf, it goes to the father's mawali by muwalat or, if none, to the distant relatives. The passage then addresses the ruling that 'the manumitter is an 'asbah', citing a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) told a man who freed a slave that if the slave dies without an heir, the manumitter is his 'asbah. This is based on the prophetic statement and the precedent of inheriting Hamza's daughter through 'usubiyyah. The commentary explains that the manumitter becomes the residuary heir of the freed slave when there are no other heirs.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): Patronage/clientage relationship that creates inheritance rights between the manumitter and the freed slave or between parties in a muwalat contract.
عتاقة ('itaqah): Manumission of a slave, which establishes wala' between the manumitter and the freed slave.
موالاة (muwalat): A contract of clientage between a free person and another to establish mutual inheritance, typically used in the absence of agnatic relatives.
عصبة ('usbah): The manumitter becomes a residuary heir ('asbah) of the freed slave when the latter dies without any other heir.
ذوو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Distant relatives (e.g., maternal aunts, cousins) who do not have fixed shares and are not 'asabah; they inherit when there are no heirs of the prescribed categories.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the wala' of a child with a free Arab father and a freed slave mother?
A) There is no practical difference; both agree the inheritance goes to the mother's manumitter.
B) If the child dies without a natural heir, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad the inheritance goes to the mother's manumitter, while according to Abu Yusuf it goes to the father's mawali or distant relatives.
C) The inheritance always goes to the father's mawali according to all imams.
D) The child's wala' is always to the father because of his Arab lineage.

Key Takeaway
The wala' of a child of a free Arab father and a freed mother is assigned to the mother's manumitter per the mufta bihi (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), but Abu Yusuf gives it to the father's mawali, with clear inheritance consequences.

Women's Limited Right to Wala'
Defining the limited scope of women's wala' with two detailed scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of wala' that women can inherit in the Hanafi school and explain two illustrative scenarios (mudabbar and jarr al-wala').

Core Idea
Women inherit wala' only from those they freed, those freed by their freedmen, and through jarr al-wala' — never from general relatives.

This lesson unpacks a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh: when a woman emancipates a slave, she gains a limited right of clientage (wala') that can be inherited by her own freedmen and, through the unique mechanism of jarr al-wala', even by the freedmen of her freedmen. Understanding this limitation is crucial for correctly distributing inheritance and avoiding common errors where women might wrongly claim wala' from relatives they did not personally emancipate. The passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah presents the core rule and then expands on two detailed case studies to illustrate its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the beginning of the Arabic text and its translation. State in your own words the fundamental principle regarding women and wala'. What are the three sources of wala' for a woman?
The phrase 'ليس لهن من الولاء إلا ولاءَ مَن أعتقنَه ، أو ولاء من أعتق من أعتقنَه' is key.
2
Analyze the mudabbar scenario
Locate the first detailed scenario involving a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the owner's death). Trace the sequence: a woman makes a tadbir, then apostatizes and leaves, the judge frees the slave, she returns, the slave dies without 'asabah. Why does the woman inherit his wala'?
The woman is considered the 'asabah of her mudabbar because she is the original owner who performed the tadbir.
3
Examine the jarr al-wala' scenario
Find the second scenario about jarr al-wala'. A woman frees a slave, that slave buys and frees another slave, and the freed slave has a child from a free woman. Explain how the woman ends up inheriting wala' from that child through the chain.
The key is that the freed slave 'drags' the wala' of his own freedman's child to him and then to his female master.
4
Review the early juristic evidence
Read the footnote in the passage that cites Ibn Mas'ud, Ali, and Zayd. What was their practice regarding women and wala'? How does this support the Hanafi ruling?
The Companions explicitly restricted women's wala' to their own acts of emancipation.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider: A woman's brother frees a slave, and that slave later dies without blood heirs. Does the woman inherit wala' from that slave? Answer: No, because she did not free him nor did her freedman free him. This demonstrates the strict limitation.
This highlights the contrast with the scenarios where she does inherit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الولاء/ أحكامه
كاتبن، أو دبرن، أو دبر من دبرن، أو جَرَّ ولاء معتقهن، أو معتق معتقهن ) : أي ليس للنساء من الولاء إلا ولاءَ مَن أعتقنَه ، أو ولاء من أعتق من أعتقنَه ، وأما ولاء المدبر فقد عرفته، ففي مدبّر المدبّر يفرض ذلك مرتين، ومسألةُ جَرِّ الولاء قد مرت.
والمقدرة عبارة عن معتوق يتعلق به الإعتاق ؛ فلانه بمنزلة سائر ما يتملك مما لا عقل له ، كما في قوله : أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَتُكُمْ (۳)
وكلمة : من ؛ عبارة عمّن صارَ حراً مالكاً فاستحق أن يعبر عنه بلفظ العقلاء، وقوله : أو جرّ ؛ يحتاجُ إلى أن يقدّر معه أن حتى يصير مؤولاً بالمصدر؛ أي ليس لهن شيء من الولاء، إلا ولاء ما ذكر أو إن جر ولاء معتقهن، أو ولاء معتق معتقهن. انتهى هكذا في حاشية جلبي).
[١] قوله : يفرض ذلك مرتين ؛ قال الفاضل الشريف : صورة ولاء مدبّر هن إن دبرت امرأة عبداً ثم ارتدت ولحقت بدار الحرب، وحكم القاضي بحرية عبدها المدبر، ثمّ أسلمت ورجعت إلى دار الإسلام، ثمّ مات المدبر ولم يخلف عصبة نسبية، فهذه المرأة عصبته.
(۱) قال الزيلعي في نصب الراية» (٤) : ١٥٤) وابن حجر في (الدراية (٢ : ١٩٥): غريب، وأخرجه البيهقي في السنن الكبير ( ١٠: ٣٠٦): من طريق ابن مسعود وعلي وزيد بن ثابت أنهم كانوا يجعلون الولاء للكبير من العصبة ولا يورثون النساء من الولاء إلا ما أعتقن أو أعتق من أعتقن. ومن طريق إبراهيم كان عمر وعلي وزيد بن ثابت لا يورثون النساء من الولاء إلا ما أعتقن. وأخرج ابن أبي شيبة (٦) : (۲۸۹) من طريق الحسن أنه قال : لا ترث النساء من الولاء إلا ما أعتقن أو أعتق من أعتقن. وروى عبد الرزاق (٩ : (٣٦) من طريق يحيى بن الجزار عن علي قال : لا ترث النساء من الولاء إلا ما كاتبن أو أعتقن. ومن طريق ابن مسعود نحوه قال الحكم وكان شريح يقوله .
(۲) النساء : من الآية.
(۳) ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٣٤).
وحكم مدبر هذا المدبر كذلك ؛ أي إذا حكم القاضي بعتق مدبرها . لحاقها ، بسبب فاشترى عبداً أو دبره ثم مات أو رجعت المرأة ثانية إلى دار الإسلام إما قبل موت مدبرها أو بعده ، ثمّ مات المدبر الثاني ولم يخلف عصبة نسبية فولاؤه لهذه المرأة. وصورة جرّ معتق معتقهن الولاء : إن امرأة أعتقت عبداً، فاشترى العبد المعتق عبداً ، فزوجه بمعتقة غيره ، فولد منهما ولدٌ ، وهو حرّ وولاؤه لمولى أمه ، فإذا أعتق ذلك العبد المعتق عبده جرّ بإعتاقه ولاء ولد معتقه إلى نفسه، ثم إلى مولاته. انتهى كلامه الشريف. هكذا في «حاشية الجلبي).
Book of Wala'/ Its Rulings: (They) wrote a contract of kitabah, or made a tadbir, or (one) who made a tadbir on one who made a tadbir, or dragged the wala' of their freedmen, or the wala' of the freedman of their freedman: meaning that women have no wala' except the wala' of those they freed, or the wala' of the one freed by the one they freed. As for the wala' of the mudabbar, you already know it. So in the case of the mudabbar's mudabbar, that is presumed twice. The issue of dragging wala' has already passed. The phrase 'or what your right hands possess' refers to a freed slave to whom emancipation is attached; it is like other owned items without intellect. The word 'man' refers to one who became free and owned, deserving to be expressed by the word of rational beings. The phrase 'or dragged' requires that 'an' be estimated with it so that it becomes a verbal noun; meaning they have nothing of wala' except the wala' of what was mentioned or if the wala' of their freedmen is dragged, or the wala' of their freedman's freedman. This ends as in Hashiyat Jalabi. [1] His saying: 'that is presumed twice'; al-Fadil al-Sharif said: The scenario of the wala' of their mudabbar is that if a woman made a tadbir on a slave, then apostatized and joined the abode of war, and the judge ruled on the freedom of her mudabbar slave, then she became Muslim and returned to the abode of Islam, then the mudabbar died leaving no relative 'asabah, then this woman is his 'asabah. (1) Al-Zayla'i said in Nasb al-Rayah..., and Ibn Hajar in al-Dirayah: Gharib, and al-Bayhaqi narrated in al-Sunan al-Kabir from Ibn Mas'ud, 'Ali, and Zayd ibn Thabit that they used to give wala' to the eldest of the 'asabah and did not give women inheritance of wala' except what they freed or what was freed by those they freed. And from Ibrahim that 'Umar, 'Ali, and Zayd ibn Thabit did not give women inheritance of wala' except what they freed. And Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from al-Hasan that he said: Women do not inherit wala' except what they freed or what was freed by those they freed. And Abd al-Razzaq narrated from Yahya ibn al-Jazzar from 'Ali: Women do not inherit wala' except what they wrote a kitabah for or freed. And from Ibn Mas'ud similarly. Al-Hakam said: and Shurayh used to say it. (2) Women: from the verse. (3) Dhakhirat al-'Uqba p.534. And the ruling of the mudabbar of this mudabbar is similar; i.e., if the judge ruled on the freedom of her mudabbar due to her joining (the abode of war), because she bought a slave or made a tadbir, then she died or returned to the abode of Islam before or after the death of her mudabbar, then the second mudabbar died leaving no relative 'asabah, then his wala' belongs to this woman. The scenario of dragging the wala' of the freedman of their freedman: a woman freed a slave, then that freed slave bought a slave, then he married him to a freed slave of another, and a child was born to them, who is free and his wala' belongs to the master of his mother. Then when that freed slave freed his own slave, he drags by his freeing the wala' of the child of his freedman to himself, then to his female master. End of his noble words. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is an explanation (sharh) of a principal ruling regarding women's inheritance of wala' (clientage). The base text (matn) states that women's wala' rights are limited to three categories: (1) those they personally freed, (2) those freed by their freedmen, and (3) cases of 'jarr al-wala' (dragging wala') where a woman's freedman frees another slave, thereby pulling the wala' of that freedman's relatives to himself and ultimately to the original female owner. The commentator clarifies that wala' for female owners does not extend to general relatives or any category beyond these specific cases. Two detailed scenarios are then presented: first, the case of a mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon owner's death) where a woman apostatizes, her mudabbar is declared free by a judge, then she returns and later the mudabbar dies without 'asabah; the woman inherits his wala'. Second, the scenario of 'jarr al-wala' where a freed slave purchases and frees another slave, affecting the wala' of children born to that slave from a free woman. The passage ends with a footnote documenting early juristic authority (Ibn Mas'ud, Ali, Zayd, etc.) that women do not receive wala' except from their own act of emancipation or that of their freedmen. This ruling is firmly established in the Hanafi school and is relied upon for fatwa.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): The right of inheritance and patronage that a former master has over a freed slave, which can be inherited by the master's 'asabah (agnatic heirs).
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the owner's death (tadbir). If the owner dies, the slave becomes free from one-third of the estate.
جر الولاء (jarr al-wala'): The act of a freed slave pulling the wala' of his own freedmen's descendants to himself, and ultimately to his original female owner, through his act of emancipation.
معتق (mu'tiq): The person who emancipates a slave, thereby establishing a wala' relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, from whom do women inherit wala' (clientage)?
A) From all their relatives through blood ties.
B) Only from those they personally freed and those freed by their freedmen.
C) Only from those they personally freed.
D) From any person they are related to by marriage.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a woman's right to wala' is strictly limited to her own acts of emancipation or those of her freedmen, never extending to other relatives.

Conditions of Wala' al-Muwalat
Defines Wala' al-Muwalat and lists its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Wala' al-Muwalat and identify its five conditions as per the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Wala' al-Muwalat is a contractual clientage — only valid between a freed slave of unknown lineage and a patron who inherits and pays blood-wit.

This passage from a commentary on al-Hidayah defines the contract of Wala' al-Muwalat. Understanding its precise conditions is essential to distinguish it from stronger forms of clientage. Let's examine each condition and the dispute over one alleged requirement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
Recognize that Wala' al-Muwalat is a valid (sahih) contract involving inheritance and blood-wit responsibility.
It is not a form of 'itq (emancipation) clientage.
2
List the five conditions from the passage
Extract each condition explicitly mentioned: non-Arab, freed slave, stipulation of inheritance and blood-wit, no prior transfer, and the disputed condition (Islam at hand).
The fifth condition (Islam at hand) is not required according to the correct view.
3
Understand the rationale behind each condition
Analyze why the client must be of unknown lineage and freed, and why the contract must specify inheritance and blood-wit.
An Arab has tribal support, so clientage is unnecessary.
4
Recognize the disputed condition and the correct Hanafi view
Note that some scholars require Islam at the patron's hand, but the correct (sahih) Hanafi view is that this is not a condition.
The passage explicitly states 'الصحيح أن ذا ليس بشرط'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في ولاء الموالاة

صح إن أسلم رجلٌ على يد رجل وولاه، أو غيره على أن يرثه، ويعقل عنه. قيد أُخْرِجَ مخرج العادة، وهو ليس بشرط لصحة هذا العقد. وصورة ولاء الموالاة: أن يقول مجهول النسب للذي أسلم على يده أو لغيره: واليتك على أن إن مت فميراثي لك، وإن جنيتُ فعقلي عليك وعلى عاقلتك، وقبل الآخر منه، يكون القابل مولى له يرثه إذا مات ويعقلُ عنه إذا جنى، وله شرائط منها: أن يكون من غير العرب؛ لأنّ العربي له نصرة بنفسه: أي قبيلة، وذلك أكد من نصرة الموالاة. ومنها: أن يكون معتقاً. ومنها: أن يشترطا الميراث والعقل. ومنها: أن يكون لم ينتقل عنه غيره. ومنها: الإسلام على يده عند البعض، والصحيح أن ذا ليس بشرط.
Chapter on Wala' al-Muwalat. It is valid: if a man embraces Islam at the hand of a man and takes him as a client, or someone else, on the condition that he inherit from him and pay his blood-wit. This condition is expressed as a custom, and it is not a condition for the validity of this contract. The form of Wala' al-Muwalat is that a person of unknown lineage says to the one at whose hand he embraced Islam or to someone else: 'I take you as my client on the condition that if I die, my inheritance is for you, and if I commit a crime, my blood-wit is upon you and your 'āqila.' And the other accepts that, so that the acceptor becomes a client who inherits from him if he dies and pays blood-wit for him if he commits a crime. It has conditions: that he be from non-Arabs, because the Arab has support from his own tribe, and that is stronger than the support of clientage. And that he be a freed slave. And that they stipulate inheritance and blood-wit. And that he has not transferred his clientage to another before. And that he embraced Islam at his hand according to some, but the correct view is that this is not a condition.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines Wala' al-Muwalat (clientage by contract) and its conditions. The author begins by stating the basic ruling: it is valid if a man embraces Islam at the hand of another and makes him his client, or if someone else takes him as a client, on the condition of inheriting from him and paying his blood-wit. However, the author clarifies that the phrase 'at the hand of' is not a necessary condition; it is merely a customary expression. The actual form of the contract is that a person of unknown lineage (i.e., no known tribal affiliation) says to another: 'I take you as my client so that if I die, you inherit from me, and if I commit a crime, you and your 'āqila pay the blood-wit.' The other accepts, and the acceptor becomes the client. Then the passage lists five conditions: (1) The client must be non-Arab, because Arabs have tribal support which is stronger than contractual clientage. (2) The client must be a freed slave (mu'taq). (3) Both parties must stipulate inheritance and blood-wit in the contract. (4) The client must not have transferred his clientage to another before. (5) Some scholars require that the client embraced Islam at the hand of the patron, but the correct (sahih) view is that this is not a condition. This passage is from a commentary expanding on a matn, and it clarifies the legal parameters of Wala' al-Muwalat in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ولاء الموالاة (walā' al-muwālāt): A contractual clientage established by a formal offer and acceptance, whereby the patron inherits from the client and pays his blood-wit.
العاقلة (al-'āqila): The group of relatives (usually paternal male agnates) who are responsible for paying blood-wit on behalf of a member.
مجهول النسب (majhūl an-nasab): A person of unknown lineage, i.e., one who has no known tribe or paternal relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for the validity of Wala' al-Muwalat according to the passage?
A) The client must be a freed slave.
B) The client must have embraced Islam at the hand of the patron.
C) Both parties must stipulate inheritance and blood-wit.
D) The client must not have transferred his clientage before.

Key Takeaway
Wala' al-Muwalat is a valid contract with five specific conditions; the condition that the client embraced Islam at the patron's hand is not required.

Wala' al-Muwalat: Ruling, Proofs, and Conditions
Expands on rulings, proofs, and conditions of Wala' al-Muwalat with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Wala' al-Muwalat, list its conditions, identify its proofs, and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Wala' al-Muwalat is a valid contract establishing mutual inheritance and liability, but only under strict conditions and only in the Hanafi school.

This passage from a commentary on al-Wiqayah lays out the Hanafi doctrine of contractual clientage (Wala' al-Muwalat). You will see how the Hanafi school proves its validity from the Quran and hadith, and how it responds to the Shafi'i objection. You will also learn the precise conditions that must be met for this contract to be effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the core ruling: the upper party (patron) bears the blood money of the lower (client) and inherits from him. Note that inheritance can be mutual if stipulated.
The passage begins by defining the basic effects of the contract.
2
Locate the Shafi'i objection and its basis
Understand the Shafi'i argument: Wala' al-Muwalat invalidates the right of the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal), similar to invalidating an existing heir.
This is the central inter-madhhab dispute.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the Hanafi evidence: Quran 4:33 and the hadith 'He is the most entitled of people to him in his life and death.' Explain how these support the contract.
The verse was interpreted as referring to muwalat by the Companions.
4
Extract the conditions for validity
List the conditions: no prior wala' (the client is not a freed slave), client of unknown lineage, client not an Arab. Also note the dispute about the Islam condition.
These conditions ensure the contract does not conflict with existing rights.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i outcomes: In Hanafi, a valid muwalat contract creates inheritance and liability; in Shafi'i it is void with no effect.
This difference affects real cases where a person without known heirs makes such a contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية

وعقله عليه وإرثه له، وأخر عن ذي الرحم، وله النقل عنه بمحضره إلى غيره إن لم يعقل عنه. وعقله عليه وإرثه له: أي إن جنى الأسفل فديته على المولى الأعلى، وإن مات فأرثه للأعلى، وهذا عندنا. وعند الشافعي لا اعتبار لعقد الموالاة. وأخر عن ذي الرحم، وله النقل عنه بمحضره إلى غيره إن لم يعقل عنه الإسلام، يعني على المذهب الصحيح كما نقلت آنفاً من «الكفاية»، وعند البعض الإسلام شرط أيضاً.

قوله: أي إن جنى...الخ؛ أقول: ظاهر صريح في أن الإرث مختص من الجانب الأعلى، وليس كذلك؛ لأنه لو شرط الإرث من الجانبين فكان كذلك، ويتوارثان من الجانبين بخلاف ولاء العتاقة، فإنه يرث الأعلى ولا الأسفل من الأعلى؛ لأن سبب الإحياء قد وجد من الأعلى في حق الأسفل، ولم يوجد من الأسفل في حق الأعلى، وهاهنا السبب هو العقد والشرط، فعلى الوجه الذي وجد الشرط ثبت الحكم. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلاً عن «الكفاية».

قوله: وعند الشافعي...الخ؛ لأن فيه إبطال حق بيت المال؛ ولهذا لا تصح في حق وارث آخر، فكذا لا يصح في حق بيت المال؛ لأنه بمنزلة الورثة عند عدمهم. ولنا: قوله تعالى: وَالَّذِينَ عَقَدَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ (النساء 33)، والآية في الموالاة، والحديث: قال صلى الله عليه وسلم: «هو أحق الناس به محياه ومماته»، وهذا يشير إلى العقل.

فإن عقل عنه أو عن ولده فلا، ولا يوالي معتق أحداً. فإن ولاء العتاقة مقدم على ولاء الموالاة، فشرط أن لا يكون معتقاً. وأيضاً من شرطه أن يكون مجهول النسب، وأن لا يكون عربياً؛ لأن للعرب قبائل، فيكون لهم الورثة النسبية. والإرث؛ ولأن ماله حقه، فيصرفه إلى حيث يشاء، والصرف إلى بيت المال ضرورة عدم المستحق. هذا زبدة ما في «الهداية».

قوله: فشرط أن لا يكون معتقاً، وأن لا تكون بينه وبين أحد عقد موالاة، وقد أشار المصنف لهذا بقوله: ولم يعقل عنه...الخ. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي».

قوله: أن يكون مجهول النسب؛ بأن لا ينسب هو إلى غيره، أما نسبة غيره إليه فغير مانع، وقيل: إنه غير شرط، وهو المختار. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي».
Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. And his liability and inheritance for him, and it is deduced from the relative [ruling], and he has the right to transfer from him in his presence to another if he does not bear [the fine] for him. 'And his liability and inheritance for him' means: if the lower commits a crime, his blood money is upon the upper patron, and if he dies, his inheritance is for the upper. This is our [Hanafi] position. According to al-Shafi'i, the contract of muwalat is not recognized. And it is deduced from the relative [ruling], and he has the right to transfer from him in his presence to another if he does not bear [the fine] for him regarding Islam, meaning according to the correct madhhab as I previously transmitted from al-Kifayah, and according to some, Islam is also a condition. His statement: 'i.e., if he commits a crime...etc.'; I say: It is explicit in that inheritance is limited to the upper side, but it is not so because if inheritance is stipulated from both sides, it would be mutual, and they inherit from both sides, unlike wala' of emancipation, where the upper inherits but not the lower from the upper, because the cause of emancipation exists from the upper for the lower, but not from the lower for the upper. Here, the cause is the contract and stipulation, so the ruling is established according to how the stipulation is found. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Kifayah. His statement: 'And according to al-Shafi'i...etc.'; because it invalidates the right of the public treasury; for this reason it is not valid in the right of another heir, so likewise it is not valid in the right of the public treasury because it is in the position of heirs when there are none. Our proof: Allah's saying: 'And those whom your right hands have contracted' (an-Nisa 33), and the verse is about muwalat, and the hadith: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'He is the most entitled of people to him in his life and death', and this refers to liability. If he bears [the fine] for him or for his child, then no [validity], and he may not make a contract of muwalat with a slave who has a manumitter. For wala' of emancipation takes precedence over wala' of muwalat, so it is a condition that he not be a manumitter. Also, among its conditions is that he be of unknown lineage, and that he not be an Arab, because Arabs have tribes and thus have legal heirs. And inheritance [is based on this]; and because his property is his right, he can spend it as he wishes, and spending to the public treasury is a necessity when there is no claimant. This is the gist of what is in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'So it is a condition that he not be a manumitter, and that there be no contract of muwalat between him and anyone else, and the author indicated this by his saying: 'and he does not bear [the fine] for him...etc.' Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. His statement: 'That he be of unknown lineage' means that he himself is not attributed to another, but attribution of another to him is not a hindrance, and it is said that it is not a condition, and this is the chosen view. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings of Wala' al-Muwalat (contractual clientage), a concept recognized only in the Hanafi school. The author first explains the basic ruling: if a lower party (the client) commits a crime, the upper party (the patron) bears the blood money; and if the lower dies, the upper inherits. This is the Hanafi position. The author then presents the Shafi'i opposition, which does not recognize the contract at all, arguing it invalidates the right of the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal). The Hanafi response cites the Quranic verse (4:33) addressing muwalat and a hadith where the Prophet said the person who accepts Islam at the hands of another is most entitled to him in life and death. These proofs establish the validity of the contract.

The passage then lists conditions for the validity of Wala' al-Muwalat: (1) the upper party must not already have a wala' relationship with the lower (i.e., the lower is not already a freed slave), because wala' of emancipation has precedence; (2) the lower must be of unknown lineage (i.e., not belonging to a known tribe that would provide heirs); (3) the lower must not be an Arab, because Arabs have tribes and thus have agnatic heirs. The commentary in the margins clarifies subtleties: the condition of unknown lineage means the person himself is not attributed to another person (father/tribe), but it is not a problem if others attribute him to themselves. Some scholars say this condition is not necessary; the chosen view is that it is not a condition. Also, the margin notes that the author's phrase 'if he does not bear' refers to the condition that no prior wala' exists. The passage emphasizes that if the upper party already bears responsibility (i.e., there is an existing wala'), then the muwalat contract is invalid. The authority for this summary is drawn from al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah, making it part of the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الموالاة (al-muwalat): Contractual clientage: a contract between two Muslims where one agrees to be the patron (upper) and the other the client (lower), establishing mutual inheritance and liability for blood money.
العقل (al-'aql): Liability for blood money; the obligation to pay diyah for a crime committed by the lower party.
ولاء العتاقة (wala' al-'itaqah): Clientage of emancipation: the legal bond between a manumitter and his freed slave, giving the manumitter inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a condition for the validity of Wala' al-Muwalat?
A) The lower party must be an Arab.
B) The upper party must not already have a wala' relationship with the lower.
C) The lower party must have known lineage.
D) The contract must be witnessed by two judges.

Key Takeaway
Wala' al-Muwalat is a valid Hanafi contract with specific conditions, supported by Quran and hadith, and differs from Wala' al-'Itq in that inheritance can be mutual if stipulated.

Conditions for Valid Wala' al-Muwalah in Hanafi Fiqh
Lists conditions for wala' al-muwalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid wala' al-muwalah and identify which scenarios invalidate the contract.

Core Idea
Wala' al-muwalah requires a client of unknown lineage, non-Arab, not emancipated, and no blood money paid.

The Hanafi school recognizes two types of clientage: wala' al-'itaqah (from emancipation) and wala' al-muwalah (by agreement). While the former is stronger, the latter allows a free person of unknown lineage to establish an inheritance bond with another. This lesson unpacks the conditions that make such an agreement valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify clientage type
Determine whether the case involves wala' al-muwalah or wala' al-'itaqah. The two have different rules.
2
Check client's status: free and not mu'taq
The client must not have been emancipated (mu'taq) because wala' al-'itaqah takes precedence.
If client is a mu'taq, wala' al-muwalah is invalid.
3
Verify unknown lineage and non-Arab
The client must be of unknown lineage (majhul al-nasab) and must not be from an Arab tribe, as Arabs have tribal inheritance.
4
Ensure no blood money payment
The client must not have paid blood money ('aql) on behalf of himself or his child, as that establishes a different bond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الولاء / أحكامه

فإن عقل عنه، أو عن ولده فلا، ولا يوالي معتق أحداً. فإن ولاء العتاقة مقدم على ولاء الموالاة، فشُرط أن لا يكون معتقاً، وأيضاً من شرطه أن يكون مجهول النسب، وأن لا يكونَ عربيّاً؛ لأن للعرب قبائل، فيكون لهم الورثة النسبية والإرث؛ ولأن ماله حقه، فيصرفه إلى حيث يشاء، والصرف إلى بيت المال ضرورة عدم المستحق. هذا زيدة ما في «الهداية»).
Book of Clientage / Its Rulings. If he (the client) pays blood money on his behalf or on behalf of his child, then no (clientage is established), and one who has been emancipated does not enter into a clientage agreement. Indeed, the clientage of emancipation takes precedence over the clientage of agreement, so it is stipulated that he should not have been emancipated. Also among its conditions is that he should be of unknown lineage, and that he should not be Arab, because Arabs have tribes, so they have lineal heirs and inheritance; and because his wealth is his right, he may spend it wherever he wishes, and spending to the treasury is only due to the necessity of no entitled person. This is the summary of what is in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Clientage (Kitab al-Wala') discusses the conditions for a valid wala' al-muwalah (clientage by agreement). The text states that if a person has previously paid blood money on behalf of someone or his own child, then the clientage agreement is not valid. Additionally, a person who has been emancipated (mu'taq) cannot enter into a clientage agreement because the clientage of emancipation (wala' al-'itaqah) is stronger and takes precedence. The conditions for wala' al-muwalah are: the client must be of unknown lineage (majhul al-nasab), meaning he has no known paternal relatives, and he must not be an Arab. Arabs have tribes and potential heirs, so they do not need clientage. Furthermore, if a person has wealth, he may dispose of it as he wishes; only when there is no legal heir is it spent to the public treasury (bayt al-mal). This summary is from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
ولاء الموالاة (wala' al-muwalah): Clientage established by agreement between two free persons, one becoming the client (mawla) of the other.
ولاء العتاقة (wala' al-'itaqah): Clientage resulting from emancipation of a slave.
مجهول النسب (majhul al-nasab): Someone whose lineage is unknown; they do not have recognized paternal relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a condition for a valid wala' al-muwalah according to the Hanafi school?
A) The client must have a known Arab lineage.
B) The client must have been emancipated by an earlier master.
C) The client must be of unknown lineage and not Arab.
D) The client must have paid blood money for himself.

Key Takeaway
Wala' al-muwalah is valid only when the client is free of wala' al-'itaqah, of unknown lineage, non-Arab, and has not paid blood money for himself or his child.
