Definition and Types of Coercion (Ikrah)
Defines ikrah and distinguishes its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ikrah and distinguish between its two types based on whether consent is lost or choice is corrupted.

Core Idea
Coercion either kills consent or corrupts choice — and the legal effect depends on which.

The Hanafi school carefully defines coercion because it affects the validity of contracts, divorces, and other legal acts. This passage gives the precise definition and divides coercion into two types based on whether it removes consent (rida) or corrupts choice (ikhtiyar). Understanding this distinction is key to determining when a coerced act is legally effective or not.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ikrah
Read the first sentence carefully. It defines coercion as an act by the coercer that causes loss of consent OR corruption of choice, while the coerced person's legal capacity (ahliyyah) remains intact.
Legal capacity not affected means the person is still responsible for their actions.
2
Identify the two types
The text then divides coercion into two types: (1) that which removes consent (by imprisonment or beating) and (2) that which corrupts choice (by threat of death or cutting a limb). Note that both types also remove consent implicitly, but the second type additionally corrupts choice.
The first type: ikrah ghayr mulji'. The second: ikrah mulji'.
3
Understand the relationship between consent and choice
The author clarifies that loss of consent is more general than corruption of choice. Imprisonment/beating removes consent but leaves a sound choice. Threat of death removes consent and gives a corrupt (fasid) choice.
This means actions done under mild coercion may be valid if the person still has sound choice.
4
Learn the opposites
Consent (rida) is opposite of aversion (karahah). Choice (ikhtiyar) is opposite of compulsion (jabr). This helps define each term precisely.
Aversion exists in both types, but only in the second type is choice unsound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه

هو فعل يوقعُهُ المَكْرِه بغيره، فيفوت به رضاه، أو يفسد اختياره مع بقاء أهليته
يقال : أوقع فلان بفلان ما يسوؤه، ثُمَّ الإكراه نوعان : أحدهما : أن يكون مفوِّتاً للرّضا، وهو أن يكون بالحبس، أو الضرب. والثاني : أن يكون مفسداً للاختيار، وهو أن يكون التهديد بالقتل، أو قطع العضو.
فَفَوت الرضا أعم من فسادِ الاختيار، ففي الحبس، أو الضرب يـفـوتُ الرضاء، ولكن الاختيار الصحيح باق، وفي القتل لا رضاء، ولكن له اختيار غير صحیح، بل اختیار فاسد.
وتحقيقه : إن الرضا في مقابلة الكراهة، والاختيار في مقابلة الجبر، ففي الإكراه بالحبس والضرب لا شك أن الكراهة موجودة ، فالرضا معدوم، لكن
Book of Coercion. It (coercion) is an act performed by the coercer upon another, whereby his consent is lost, or his choice is corrupted while his legal capacity remains. It is said: So-and-so inflicted something harmful upon so-and-so. Then coercion is of two types: one that causes the loss of consent, which is by imprisonment or beating. And the second that corrupts choice, which is threat of death or cutting of a limb. Loss of consent is more general than corruption of choice. So in imprisonment or beating, consent is lost, but proper choice remains. In threat of death, there is no consent, but he possesses an unsound choice, rather a corrupt choice. Its verification: consent is opposite of aversion, and choice is opposite of compulsion. In coercion by imprisonment and beating, there is no doubt that aversion exists, so consent is absent, but
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah) provides the legal definition of ikrah. It defines it as an act by which the coercer causes the coerced person to lose his consent (rida) or corrupts his choice (ikhtiyar) while his legal capacity (ahliyyah) remains intact, meaning the person is still legally accountable. The text divides ikrah into two types: (1) that which eliminates consent, achieved by imprisonment or beating; (2) that which corrupts choice, achieved by threats of death or cutting of a limb. It clarifies that loss of consent is broader than corruption of choice: in imprisonment or beating, consent is lost but the person retains a sound choice (i.e., he can still decide rationally). In threats of death, consent is also lost, but his choice is unsound (fasid) because he acts under duress. Finally, it explains that consent is opposite of aversion (karahah), and choice is opposite of compulsion (jabr).

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion: an act that removes consent or corrupts choice while legal capacity remains.
الرضا (al-rida): Consent: the opposite of aversion; its absence does not necessarily invalidate an action if choice remains.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyar): Choice: the ability to decide; when corrupted, the action may be invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of coercion corrupts choice (ikhtiyar)?
A) Imprisonment
B) Beating
C) Threat of death
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Coercion is defined as the removal of consent or corruption of choice, and the two types have different legal consequences for acts performed under duress.

Definition and Types of Coercion (Ikrāh)
Defines ikrāh and explains its two types and first condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ikrāh, distinguish its two types, and identify the first condition for valid coercion.

Core Idea
Coercion either eliminates consent or corrupts choice, but the coerced person retains legal capacity.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah opens the chapter on coercion (ikrāh) by providing a precise definition and dividing it into two types. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later rulings on contracts, divorces, and other actions performed under duress. The author also sets the first condition: the coercer must have the power to execute his threat.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of ikrāh
Read the first sentence: 'It is an act that the coercer inflicts upon another, thereby either eliminating his consent or corrupting his choice while his legal capacity remains.' Identify the three key elements: the act, the effect on consent or choice, and the retention of legal capacity.
Legal capacity (ahliyyah) means the coerced person is still mukallaf (accountable) because sanity and majority remain.
2
Identify the two types of ikrāh
The passage explicitly states two types: (1) that which eliminates consent, exemplified by imprisonment or beating; (2) that which corrupts choice, exemplified by threats of death or mutilation. Note the examples.
These types are not mutually exclusive in all cases, but the Hanafi school treats them as distinct categories with different legal effects.
3
Understand the distinction between loss of consent and corruption of choice
The author explains that loss of consent is more general: in type 1, consent is absent but sound choice remains; in type 2, choice is corrupted (fāsid). Read the analogy of falling from a height or throwing into fire to see why avoidance is 'nearly compulsive'.
Choice is 'sound' (ṣaḥīḥ) when the person can freely choose between alternatives. It is 'corrupt' (fāsid) when instinct overpowers free deliberation.
4
Recognize the condition for valid coercion
The passage ends with 'its condition: the coercer must be able to carry out what he threatens.' This is the first of several conditions discussed later. If the coercer lacks the ability, the threat does not constitute legal coercion.
This condition ensures that the victim's fear is objectively reasonable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه
هو فعل يوقعه المكره بغيره، فيفوت به رضاه، أو يفسد اختياره مع بقاء أهليته
يقال: أوقع فلان بفلان ما يسوؤه، ثم الإكراه نوعان: أحدهما: أن يكون مفوتاً للرضا، وهو أن يكون بالحبس، أو الضرب. والثاني: أن يكون مفسداً للاختيار، وهو أن يكون التهديد بالقتل، أو قطع العضو.
ففوت الرضا أعم من فساد الاختيار، ففي الحبس أو الضرب يفوت الرضاء، ولكن الاختيار الصحيح باق، وفي القتل لا رضاء، ولكن له اختيار غير صحيح، بل اختيار فاسد.
وتحقيقه: إن الرضا في مقابلة الكراهة، والاختيار في مقابلة الجبر، ففي الإكراه بالحبس والضرب لا شك أن الكراهة موجودة، فالرضا معدوم، لكن الاختيار متحقق مع وصف الصحة، فإن الاختيار إنما يفسد في مقابلة تلف النفس أو العضو، فإن كل أمر فيه هلاك أحدهما فالامتناع عنه مجبول في طبيعة جميع الحيوانات. ألا ترى أن القوة الماسكة، كيف تمسك الإنسان، بل جميع الحيوانات عن الهوى من المكان العالي، ومن الإلقاء في النار عند مظنة التلف، فالامتناع عنه وإن كان اختيارياً، فهو اختيار صورة قريب من الجبر، فكذا في الإكراه عند خوف تلف النفس أو العضو اختيار الامتناع عما فيه مظنة الهلاك اختيار فاسد؛ لأن الإنسان عليه مجبور من حيث إن الطبع عليه مجبول، ومع ذلك الأهلية باقية في الملجئ وغير الملجئ لتحقق العقل والبلوغ.
وشرطه: قدرة المكره على إيقاع ما هدد به
The Book of Coercion. It is an act that the coercer inflicts upon another, thereby either eliminating his consent or corrupting his choice while his legal capacity remains. It is said: so-and-so inflicted upon so-and-so what distressed him. Then coercion is of two types: one that eliminates consent—that is by imprisonment or beating. The second that corrupts choice—that is the threat of killing or cutting off a limb. The loss of consent is more general than corruption of choice: in imprisonment or beating, consent is lost but sound choice remains; in killing, there is no consent, but he has an unsound choice—rather a corrupt choice. The verification: consent is opposite of aversion, and choice is opposite of compulsion. In coercion by imprisonment or beating, there is no doubt that aversion exists, so consent is absent, but choice is present with the attribute of soundness. For choice is only corrupted in the face of destruction of life or limb. Indeed, every matter in which either of these is destroyed, abstention from it is ingrained in the nature of all animals. Do you not see how the restraining power holds back a person—indeed all animals—from falling from a high place and from throwing into fire when destruction is likely? So abstention, though voluntary, is a choice in form that is close to compulsion. Thus in coercion when there is fear of destruction of life or limb, the choice to abstain from that which entails destruction is a corrupt choice, because a person is compelled by nature. Nevertheless, legal capacity remains in both the coerced (mulji') and non-coerced, because sanity and majority are present. Its condition: The coercer must be able to carry out what he threatens.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah, a commentary on Sharḥ al-Wiqāyah, opens the chapter on coercion (ikrāh). The author defines ikrāh as an act inflicted by one person upon another that either eliminates the victim's consent or corrupts his choice, while his legal capacity (ahliyyah) remains intact. This is important: the coerced person is still legally accountable because he retains sanity and majority. The passage then divides coercion into two types: (1) that which eliminates consent (tafwīt al-riḍā), exemplified by imprisonment or beating, and (2) that which corrupts choice (fasād al-ikhtiyār), exemplified by threats of death or mutilation. The author explains that loss of consent is broader than corruption of choice: in imprisonment or beating, consent is lost but the victim still has sound choice—he can choose to obey or resist. In threats of death, however, the victim's choice is corrupted because self-preservation is instinctive; his decision to comply is not a free choice but driven by natural compulsion. The author uses the analogy of a person avoiding falling from a height or throwing himself into fire: such avoidance is voluntary in form but nearly compelled. Similarly, when threatened with death, the victim's compliance is a corrupt choice (ikhtiyār fāsid). Despite this, his legal capacity remains. Finally, the first condition for valid coercion is stated: the coercer must have the power to carry out his threat. This is the first of four conditions later explained in the book.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): An act inflicted by one person upon another that either eliminates consent or corrupts choice while legal capacity remains.
الرضا (al-riḍā): The victim's satisfaction or willingness; in coercion by imprisonment or beating, it is absent but choice remains sound.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): The victim's ability to choose; in life-threatening coercion, it is corrupted (fāsid) but not absent.
المكره (بالكسر) (al-mukrih (bi al-kasr)): The coercer, the one who inflicts the act of coercion.
المكره (بالفتح) (al-mukrah (bi al-fatḥ)): The coerced person, the victim of coercion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi definition of coercion, what distinguishes the two types of ikrāh?
A) The type of threat: physical vs. verbal
B) Whether the coercer is a ruler or a private individual
C) Whether consent is lost or choice is corrupted
D) Whether the coerced person suffers harm or not

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of ikrāh hinges on two distinct elements: consent and choice, and the victim retains legal capacity.

Conditions of Valid Coercion and the Sultan Dispute
Defines three conditions of coercion with an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid coercion (ikrah) and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding who qualifies as a coercer.

Core Idea
Ikrah requires a capable coercer, genuine fear, and severe threat; the coercer need not be the sultan.

Coercion (ikrah) nullifies the consent that underpins legal acts. This lesson unpacks the three conditions that make coercion valid in Hanafi law and examines a famous intra-madhhab dispute: does the coercer have to be the ruler, or can anyone with the power to harm qualify?

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal construct and its purpose
Recognize that ikrah is a legal condition that invalidates consent, not a specific act of worship. The passage defines its conditions.
2
List the three conditions from the text
Extract each condition: (1) coercer's ability to carry out threat, (2) coerced's genuine fear, (3) threat being severe (death, limb, or extreme distress).
3
State the relied-upon Hanafi position on the coercer
Note that the mufta bihi view is that any capable person (sultan or robber) can be a coercer. This is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
4
Identify Abu Hanifa's differing view
Recognize the narrated view from Abu Hanifa that ikrah only applies when the sultan coerces.
This is an early view based on his era's realities.
5
Analyze the basis for the difference
Understand that the difference stems from changes in societal conditions, not a disagreement in principle. The illah (effective cause) is the power to enforce the threat; in later times, non-sultans also had such power.
6
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a non-sultan coerces, the earlier view would not treat the act as coerced; the later view does, affecting the validity of contracts, divorces, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه / تعريفه وشروطه وأحكامه
وشرطه : قدرة المكْرِه على إيقاع ما هدد به، سُلطاناً كان أو لصاً، وخوفُ المُكْرَهِ إيقاعُه، وكونُ المُكْرَه به متلفاً نفساً، أو عضواً، أو موجباً غماً يعدم الرّضاء.
روي عن أبي حنيفة أن الإكراه لا يتحقق إلا من السلطان، فكأنه قال ذلك بناءً على ما كان واقعاً في عصره.
Book of coercion / its definition, conditions and rulings. Its condition: the coercer's ability to inflict what he threatens, whether he be a sultan or a robber, the coerced's fear of its occurrence, and the threat being destructive to life, or a limb, or causing distress that eliminates consent. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that coercion is only realized from the sultan, as if he said that based on what was happening in his era.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya defines the three conditions for legal coercion (ikrah). First, the coercer must have the power to carry out the threat, whether he is a ruler or a common thief. Second, the coerced must genuinely fear that the threat will be executed. Third, the threat must involve loss of life, limb, or such severe distress that it removes voluntary consent. The text then reports a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that coercion can only come from the sultan. The commentator explains that this was not a difference of proof but of circumstance: in Abu Hanifa's time, only the sultan had such power; later, with the spread of tyranny, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) extended it to any capable person. The operative ruling (mufta bihi) today is the view of the companions, as the condition is about capability, not status.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compelling someone to act against their will through a credible threat that overcomes natural resistance.
المكره (al-mukrih): The person who issues the threat and has the ability to carry it out.
السلطان (al-sulṭān): The ruler or governing authority with coercive power.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The threat must be of death or loss of a limb.
B) The coerced must fear the threat will be carried out.
C) The coercer must be a sultan or ruler.
D) The threat must be capable of being carried out.

Key Takeaway
Coercion (ikrah) requires a capable coercer, genuine fear, and severe threat; the Hanafi relied-upon view is that any person with the power to harm qualifies, not just the sultan.

Definition and Conditions of Duress (Ikrah) and Its Effect on Contracts
Defines ikrah, its conditions, and the ruling on contracts under duress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ikrah, identify its conditions, and explain the ruling on contracts concluded under duress.

Core Idea
Ikrah eliminates consent for execution but not for formation; the coerced party has the option to rescind.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi doctrine of duress (ikrah). You will learn what qualifies as a credible threat, who can be a coercer, and why a contract entered under duress is valid yet reversible. The passage also resolves an early difference between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and conditions of ikrah
Read the core definition: coercion by threat of death, loss of limb, or severe pain that eliminates consent, with the condition that the coerced person fears it will be carried out.
The threat must be credible and the harm must be severe enough to eliminate genuine consent.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the source of coercion
Understand Abu Hanifa’s view that only the sultan can coerce versus the Sahibayn’s view that anyone can, and the tarjih based on changing times.
This difference is based on circumstance (ikhtilaf al-zaman), not proof.
3
Apply the ruling on contracts under duress
Recognize that contracts (sale, purchase, lease, confession) are concluded but not executed; the coerced party has khiyar (option to rescind or confirm).
The contract is sahih but its execution is suspended pending the option.
4
Consider subjective variation in determining coercion
Note that what constitutes duress depends on the person: harsh words for nobles may be ikrah, while commoners need severe beating or prolonged imprisonment.
There is no fixed measure; it is based on the judge's assessment of the individual's situation.

حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية

سُلطاناً كان، أو لصاً، وخوفُ المُكْرَهِ إيقاعُه وكونُ المُكْرَه به مُتلفاً نفساً، أو عضواً، أو مُوجباً غماً يعدمُ الرّضاء سُلطاناً كان، أو لصاً). روي عن أبي حنيفة أنَّ الإكراه لا يتحقق إلا من السلطان، فكأنه قال ذلك بناءً على ما كان واقعاً في عصره.

(وخوفُ المُكْرَهِ إيقاعُه): أي يغلب على ظنِّهِ أنَّ المُكْرِهَ يوقِعُه.

(وكونُ المُكْرَه به متلفاً نفساً، أو عضواً، أو موجباً غماً يعدم الرضاء).

[قوله: لصاً؛ بكسر اللام، وضمها لغة فيه، يقال بالفارسية: دزد آشكارا. كذا قال الجلبي.]

[قوله: لا يتحقق إلا من السلطان؛ لما أنّ المنعة له، والقدرة لا تتحقق بدون المنعة، فقد قالوا: هذا اختلافُ عصر وزمان؛ لا اختلاف حجة وبرهان، أشار إليه الشارح بقوله: فكأنه قال أبو حنيفة ... الخ. ذلك بناءً على ما كان واقعاً في عصره؛ لأنّ في زمنه لم يوجد الإكراه إلا من السلطان، ثمّ تغير الزمان وأهله، ونشر الفساد والطغيان، ودفع الإكراه من كل واحد، فلو كان الإمام في زمان صاحبيه لأفتى بقولهما. كذا في «البيانية»، قال أخي جلبي: قد ظهر من هذا التقرير أن مسألة المتن على رأيهما.]

[قوله: يوقعه؛ ليصير به مضطراً على ما دعى إليه من الفعل.]

[قوله: متلفاً نفساً؛ في «الفهستاني»: أي حقيقية أو حكمية، كتلف كل المال، فإنّه شقيق الروح. كما في «الزاهدي»، قال صاحب «رد المحتار»: تقييده بكلّ المال مخالف لما سيشير إليه الشارح آخذا عن «القُنية».]

[قوله: غمّا؛ بفتح الغين المعجمة، وتشديد الميم: الهم.]

[قوله: بعدم الرضا؛ أي مع بقاء الاختيار الصحيح، وإلا فالإكراه ليس بمتلف لعدم الرضاء أيضاً، ولكنه يفسد الاختيار. كذا في «رد المحتار».]

كتاب الإكراه/ تعريفه وشروطه وأحكامه

اعلم أنَّ هذا يختلف باختلاف الناس، فإنَّ الأراذل ربما لا يَعْتَمُونَ بالضرب أو الحبس، فالضربُ اللَّيْنُ لا يكون إكراهاً في حقهم، بل الضرب المبرح، وكذا الحبس إلا أن يكون حبساً مديداً يتضجر منه، والأشراف ينتمون بكلام فيه خشونة، فمثل هذا يكون إكراهاً لهم.

والمُكْرَهُ ممتنعاً عما أُكْرِهَ عليه قبلَهُ لَحَقِّه: كبيع ماله، أو إتلافه، أو إعتاق عبده، أو لحق آخر: كإتلاف مال الغير، أو لِحَقِّ الشرع: كشرب الخمر والزنا.

(فلو أُكْرِهَ بقتل أو ضرب شديد، أو حبس حتى باع، أو اشترى، أو أقر، أو أجر، فَسَخَ أو أمضى)، فإنّ هذه العقود يشترط فيها الرضا، فالإكراه الذي يَعْدِمُ الرضاء، وهو غير الملجئ يمنعُ نفاذها لكنَّها تنعقد، وله الخيار في الفسخ والإمضاء.

[قوله: المبرح؛ بالراء والحاء المهملتين: الشديد المؤلم. كذا يفهم عن «الصحاح».]

[قوله: فمثل هذا ... الخ؛ ولذا قال محمد: ليس في ذلك تقدير لازم، بل ذلك على حسب ما يرى من حال من ابتلي به؛ لأنّ نصب المقادير لا يكون بالرأي. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي».]

[قوله: لحقه؛ أي إمّا أن يكون امتناعُهُ مما أكره عليه؛ لكونه خالص حقـه كإكراهه على إتلاف ماله، ولو بعوض كبيعه. كذا في «رد المحتار».]

[قوله: أو ضرب شديد؛ متلف لا بسوط أو سوطين إلا على المذاكير والعين؛ لأنه يخشى منه التلف.]

[قوله: أو حبس؛ قال في «رد المحتار»: أي حبس نفسه، قال الزَّيْلَعِيُّ: ...]
The beauty of guidance for the end of the commentary on Al-Wiqayah

Whether [the coercer is] a sultan or a thief, and the fear of the coerced that he will carry it out, and that the object of coercion is destructive of life, or a limb, or causes grief that eliminates consent, whether sultan or thief. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that coercion is only realized from the sultan, as if he said that based on what was occurring in his era.

(And the fear of the coerced that he will carry it out): meaning it becomes his predominant belief that the coercer will carry it out.

(And that the object of coercion is destructive of life, or a limb, or causes grief that eliminates consent).

[His saying: 'thief'; with kasra of the lam, and damm is a dialect, in Persian: obvious thief. Thus said al-Jalabi.]

[His saying: 'only realized from the sultan'; because power belongs to him, and capability cannot be realized without power. They said: this is a difference of era and time, not a difference of proof and evidence. The commentator indicated this by his saying: 'as if Abu Hanifa said...' that based on what was occurring in his era; because in his time coercion was only found from the sultan, then times and people changed, and corruption and tyranny spread, and coercion became prevalent from everyone. Had the Imam been in the time of his two companions, he would have given fatwa according to their view. Thus in 'Al-Bayaniyyah', said Akhi Jalabi: it has become apparent from this discussion that the issue of the text is according to their view.]

[His saying: 'he will carry it out'; so that he becomes compelled to do the act to which he is called.]

[His saying: 'destructive of life'; in 'Al-Fihristani': real or constructive, like the destruction of all wealth, for it is the sibling of the soul. As in 'Al-Zahidi', said the author of 'Radd al-Muhtar': his restriction to all wealth contradicts what the commentator will indicate, taking from 'Al-Qunyah'.]

[His saying: 'grief'; with fatha of the ghayn and shadda of the mim: worry.]

[His saying: 'without consent'; meaning with the continuation of sound choice, otherwise coercion does not eliminate consent but spoils choice. Thus in 'Radd al-Muhtar'.]

Book of Coercion / Its Definition, Conditions, and Rulings

Know that this varies according to people. For the base people may not take heed of beating or imprisonment, so light beating is not coercion in their case, rather severe beating. Likewise imprisonment unless it is prolonged imprisonment that causes distress. The noble people are impressed by speech containing harshness, so such is coercion for them.

And the coerced person refrains from what he is coerced to do, and accepts it for his own right: like selling his property, or destroying it, or freeing his slave; or for the right of another: like destroying the property of another; or for the right of the Sharia: like drinking wine and fornication.

(If he is coerced by killing or severe beating, or imprisonment until he sold, or bought, or confessed, or rented, he may rescind or confirm). Indeed these contracts require consent, so coercion that eliminates consent, and which is not compelling, prevents their execution but they are concluded, and he has the option to rescind or confirm.

[His saying: 'severe'; with ra and ha: extremely painful. Thus understood from 'Al-Sihah'.]

[His saying: 'such is...'; so Muhammad said: There is no fixed measure in that, rather it is according to the situation of the one afflicted; because setting measures is not by opinion. Thus in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'.]

[His saying: 'for his right'; meaning either his refraining from what he is coerced to do is because it is solely his right, like coercion to destroy his property, even if with compensation like its sale. Thus in 'Radd al-Muhtar'.]

[His saying: 'or severe beating'; destructive, not with one or two lashes except on the genitals and eye; because destruction is feared from it.]

[His saying: 'or imprisonment'; said in 'Radd al-Muhtar': meaning imprisonment of himself. Said al-Zayla'i: ...]
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah, defining the concept of ikrah (duress/coercion) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating the conditions: the coercer can be anyone (sultan or thief), the coerced must genuinely fear the threat will be carried out, and the threatened harm must be death, loss of a limb, or such severe pain that it eliminates consent. The text then notes a narrated view from Imam Abu Hanifa that ikrah only exists from the sultan, but the commentator explains this was based on the circumstances of his time. In later times, with corruption widespread, coercion can come from anyone, so the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) is preferred. The passage then discusses how coercion varies: what counts as duress for a noble person (harsh words) may not be duress for a commoner, who needs severe beating or prolonged imprisonment. The core ruling is provided: if a person is coerced by death, severe beating, or imprisonment into selling, buying, confessing, or renting, the contract is valid (concluded) but the coerced person has the option (khiyar) to rescind or confirm. This is because consent is a condition for execution, not for the contract's formation. The commentary elaborates on each term: the nature of severe beatings, the loss of all property considered constructive loss of life, and the continued presence of sound choice despite coercion.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Duress or coercion, defined as forcing someone to act against their will by threat of death, loss of limb, or severe pain that eliminates consent.
المُكْرِه (al-mukrih): The person who exerts coercion.
المُكْرَه (al-mukrah): The person being coerced.
الرضا (al-riḍā): Consent; a condition for the execution of contracts, not for their formation.
الخيار (al-khiyār): The option granted to the coerced person to either rescind or confirm the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The sale is valid (sahih) but the seller has the option to rescind.
C) The sale is suspended until the threat is carried out.
D) The sale is valid only if the seller is a noble person.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, duress does not nullify contracts but gives the coerced party the right to rescind.

Guarantee When Seller is Coerced and Goods Perish
Ruling on liability when coerced seller's goods are destroyed after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a coerced seller's goods are destroyed, explain the seller's option to claim from either the coercer or the buyer, and distinguish the effect on subsequent sales.

Core Idea
A coerced sale is fāsid; if the goods perish, the buyer must pay the value, and the seller can claim from the coercer or the buyer.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a specific case of duress in sales. It clarifies the liabilities and options when a seller is coerced, the goods are taken by a non-coerced buyer, and then the goods are destroyed. The ruling exemplifies how Hanafi fiqh balances contractual invalidity with tort-based liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the basic ruling
The seller is coerced, the buyer is not, the goods perish in the buyer's hand. The buyer must pay the value (qīmah) to the seller, not the agreed price, because the sale is fāsid (defective).
The value is determined at the time of destruction.
2
Understand the seller's option to choose the guarantor
The seller may claim the value from either the coercer (who caused the transfer) or the buyer (in whose possession the goods were destroyed). This mirrors the rule in usurpation (ghaṣb).
The coercer is liable like a usurper; the buyer is liable because the loss occurred in his hand.
3
Analyze the effect on subsequent sales
If the seller claims from the buyer, the buyer's ownership is established from the time of possession (istinād), so any sale he made after this purchase is valid. But sales before this purchase are not validated. If the seller claims from the coercer, the coercer's recourse against the buyer does not automatically validate the buyer's sales.
The difference arises because the liability affects the chain of ownership.
4
Examine the objection and response about the coercer as a tool
An objection is raised: if the coercer cannot be a tool (ālāt) in receiving the price, why is he considered a tool in delivering the goods? The answer distinguishes: making the seller a tool in delivery changes the coerced act (from voluntary to coerced), but in receiving the price, the speech (uttering the sale) cannot be attributed to another, so the receipt is not binding.
This shows the nuanced application of the 'tool' concept in duress.
5
Distinguish from the case of ratification (ijāzah)
If the seller later ratifies the sale, all contracts become effective because he waived his right. In contrast, when he claims compensation, only subsequent sales after his claim are validated.
Ratification is a voluntary acceptance, while claiming compensation is a remedy for the duress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو أُكْرِهَ البائع لا المشتري، وهَلَكَ المبيعُ في يده، ضمن قيمته للبائع، وله أن يضمن أياً شاء، فإن ضمّن المكره رجع على المشتري بقيمته، وإن ضمّن المشتري نفذ كل شراء بعده لا ما قبله.
فإن قلت: يُشكل بقبض الثمن، فإنّ الفاعل لا يمكن أن يكون آلة فيه، ومع ذلك لا ينفذ فيه. قلت: لا يلزم هنا من جعله آلة تغير الفعل الذي أكره عليه بخلاف تسليم المبيع.
(قوله: ضمن قيمته) لأنه مضمون عليه بحكم عقد فاسد، وهو البيع بالإكراه، وما هو كذلك فهو مضمون بالقيمة.
(قوله: أياً شاء) أي المشتري، والمكره كالغاصب، وغاصب الغاصب، فإنّ المالك يختار في تضمين أيهما شاء، فإن ضمن المكره رجع على المشتري بقيمته لقيامه مقام البائع، وإن ضمن المشتري نفذ كل شراء كان بعد شرائه، ولا ينفذ شراء كان قبل شراء هذا المشتري الضامن؛ لأنّ استناد ملك المشتري إلى وقت قبضه.
(قوله: من المكره - بالكسر) لأن المكره بالفتح آلة له فيما يرجع على الإتلاف، وإن لم يصلح آلة له من حيث الكلام، فإنّ الكلام بلسان الغير لا يتصوّر فكأن المكره دفع مال البائع إلى المشتري.
(قوله: ومن المشتري) لأنّ الهلاك حصل عنده، فكان كل واحد منهما أحدث سبباً للضمان.
(قوله: لو تناسخت العقود) بأن باع هذا من ذاك، وذاك من آخر، ثمّ ضمن المالك المشتري الثاني مثلاً.
(وقوله: وهذا بخلاف ما إذا أجاز المالك أحد العقود حيث ينفذ الجميع) لأنه أسقط حقه.
If the seller is coerced, not the buyer, and the sold item perishes in his possession, he guarantees its value to the seller. He may guarantee either of them (i.e., the coercer or the buyer) as he wishes. If he guarantees the coercer, the coercer has recourse against the buyer for its value. If he guarantees the buyer, every sale after that (sale) is effective, but not the one before it. If you say: It is problematic regarding the receipt of the price, for the doer cannot be made a tool in it, and yet it is not effective (in that case). I say: Here it does not necessitate that making him a tool changes the act he was coerced into, unlike delivering the sold item. (His statement: 'he guarantees its value') because it is guaranteed by virtue of a defective contract (fāsid 'aqd), which is the sale under duress, and anything so characterized is guaranteed with its value. (His statement: 'as he wishes') meaning the buyer, and the coercer is like a usurper (ghāṣib) and the usurper of a usurper, for the owner may choose which of them to hold liable. If he holds the coercer liable, the coercer has recourse against the buyer for its value because he stands in the place of the seller. If he holds the buyer liable, every sale that occurred after his purchase becomes effective, but not a sale that occurred before this guaranteeing buyer's purchase, because the buyer's ownership is traced back to the time of his taking possession. (His statement: 'from the coercer—with kasr') because the coerced person (with fatḥ) is a tool for him in matters of tort, although he cannot be a tool in terms of speech, for speech through another's tongue is inconceivable; thus the coercer is as if he paid the seller's money to the buyer. (His statement: 'and from the buyer') because the destruction occurred in his possession, so each of them originated a cause for the guarantee. (His statement: 'if contracts had been transferred in succession') such that this one sold to that one, and that one to another, then the owner held the second buyer liable, for example. (And his statement: 'and this is unlike the case where the owner ratifies one of the contracts, for then all are effective') because he waived his right.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the topic of duress (ikrāh). It addresses a specific scenario: the seller was coerced into selling, but the buyer was not coerced. The sold item perishes while in the buyer's possession. The ruling states that the buyer is liable to pay the value (qīmah) to the seller, not the price, because the sale is fāsid (defective) due to the coercion. The seller (the coerced party) has the option to claim compensation from either the coercer or the buyer. This mirrors the rule in cases of usurpation (ghaṣb) where the owner can choose to hold the original usurper or a subsequent possessor liable. If the seller chooses to hold the coercer liable, the coercer then has recourse against the buyer for the value. If the seller chooses to hold the buyer liable, then any subsequent sale by that buyer after this liability is applied is valid and transfers ownership, because the buyer's ownership is established from the time of his acquisition (al-istinād ilā waqt al-qabd). However, sales that occurred before this liability are not effective because the buyer's ownership was not yet established at that earlier time. The text then addresses an objection: why is the coercer considered a tool (ālāt) regarding the delivery of the item but not regarding the receipt of the price? The answer distinguishes between the two acts. In delivery, making the seller a tool changes the nature of the act (from voluntary to coerced). In receiving the price, the tool analogy does not apply because the speech (uttering the sale) cannot be attributed to another. Therefore, the receipt of price is not binding. The commentary further explains that the coercer is liable because he caused the transfer of the seller's property, while the buyer is liable because the destruction occurred in his hand. The distinction between this case and the case where the owner ratifies (ijāzah) one of the contracts is also noted: in ratification, the owner waives his right, so all sales become effective, whereas here compensation only affects subsequent sales.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Coercion or duress that invalidates free consent, rendering a contract fāsid in the Hanafi school.
ضمان (damān): Legal liability to compensate for loss or damage, here the obligation to pay the value (qīmah) of the destroyed item.
قيمة (qīmah): The market value of the item at the time of destruction, as opposed to the agreed price (thaman), used when the contract is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller is coerced into selling, and the buyer (who is not coerced) takes possession but the item perishes in his hand, what options does the seller have?
A) He can only claim the value from the buyer.
B) He can choose to claim the value from either the coercer or the buyer.
C) He must claim from the coercer, and the coercer has no recourse.
D) He can only claim the agreed price from the buyer.

Key Takeaway
In a coerced sale, the buyer bears liability for the value if goods perish, and the seller may choose between pursuing the coercer or the buyer, following the ghaṣb analogy.

Coercion to Eat Forbidden Food: Permissibility and Sin
Ruling on coercion to eat forbidden food and intra-Hanafi dispute on sin for refraining

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between mulji' (life-threatening) and non-mulji' coercion, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a person who refuses to eat under duress commits a sin.

Core Idea
Only life-threatening coercion permits the forbidden; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ on whether refusing sins.

This passage addresses a sensitive fiqh issue: when does coercion make eating forbidden food permissible? It distinguishes between life-threatening and non-life-threatening threats, and then presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether one who chooses to die rather than eat commits a sin. Understanding this ruling is crucial for applying the concept of necessity (darurah) correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels of coercion
Note the distinction between non-life-threatening coercion (imprisonment, beating, chains) and life-threatening coercion (death, amputation). The ruling hinges on this.
Non-life-threatening coercion does not lift the prohibition.
2
Understand the definition of necessity
Necessity (darurah) is defined as fear for life or limb. Only when the threat reaches that level does the prohibition temporarily lift.
The text explicitly says there is no necessity in non-mulji' coercion.
3
Follow the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that if a person refrains from eating when the act has become permissible, he sins because he assists in his own destruction. Abu Yusuf counters with Quran 2:173, arguing that sin is negated for one compelled.
Both positions are recorded; the passage does not give preference.
4
Analyze the Quranic dalil
Abu Yusuf cites 'faman udturra ghayra baghin wa la 'adin fala ithma 'alayhi' to argue that the concession removes sin even if one refrains, since the prohibition remains in place.
The verse is from al-Baqarah 2:173.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a person threatened with amputation if they do not eat pork. The act becomes permissible. If they refuse and lose a limb, does that sin? Abu Hanifa: yes; Abu Yusuf: no.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is whether one is accountable for refusing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أُكْرِهَ على أكل ميتة، أو دَم، أو لحم خنزير، أو شرب خمر بحبس، أو ضرب، أو قيد لم يحلّ، وبقتل أو قطع حلّ؛ لأن هذه الأشياء مستثناة عن الحرمة في حال الضرورة، والاستثناء عن الحرمة حلّ، ولا ضرورةً في إكراه غير مُلْجِيَّ. قوله : فيستند إلى حين العقد؛ لأنه صار بأداء الضمان كأنه اشتراه من هذا المالك في أوّل العقد برضاه، لما قال الشارح له قبيل هذا بقوله: يصير ملكا له؛ أي للضامنِ بالضمان من وقتِ سبب الضمان، فينفذ كأنه باع ملك نفسه لا قبله؛ أي قبل التضمين. قوله : لأن هذه الأشياء... الخ؛ توضيحه: إنّ تناول هذه المحرمات إنما يباح عند الضرورة، كما في حال المخمصة، لقيام المحرَّم فيما وراءها، ولا ضرورة إلا إذا خاف على النفس، أو على العضو حتى لو خيف ذلك بالضرب الشديد، وغلب على ظنه ذلك لا يباح له ذلك. ولا يسعه أن يصير على ما توعد به، وإن صبر... الخ حتى أوقعوا به ولم يأكل فيأثم؛ لأنه لما أبيح كان بالامتناع معاوناً لغيره من إهلاك نفسه فيأثم كما في حالة المخمصة. وعن أبي يوسف له: إنّه لا يأثم؛ لأنه رخصة، يعني أن الإثم ينتفى عن المضطر، ولا تنكشف الحرمة بالضرورة، قال الله: فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ غَيْرَ بَاغٍ وَلَا عَادٍ فَلَا إِثْمَ عَلَيْهِ؛ إذ الحرمة قائمة، فكان آخذاً بالغريمة.
If one is coerced to eat carrion, or blood, or pork, or to drink wine, by imprisonment, or beating, or chains, it does not become permissible. By death or mutilation, it becomes permissible. Because these things are exempted from prohibition in cases of necessity, and exemption from prohibition is permissibility, and there is no necessity in non-life-threatening coercion. His saying: 'so it goes back to the time of the contract' because by paying the guarantee it is as if he bought it from this owner at the beginning of the contract with his consent, as the commentator said before this: 'it becomes his property' i.e., for the guarantor by the guarantee from the time of the cause of guarantee, so it takes effect as if he sold his own property, not before it, i.e., before the guarantee. His saying: 'because these things...' etc.; its clarification: Indeed, consuming these forbidden things is only permitted in necessity, as in the case of severe hunger, because the prohibition remains beyond that. There is no necessity unless one fears for his life or a limb, even if that is feared from severe beating and he is convinced of that, it is not permissible for him. And he may not endure what he is threatened with, even if he is patient... until they carry out the threat and he does not eat, then he sins. Because when it was permitted, by refraining he assists others in destroying himself, so he sins, as in the case of severe hunger. And from Abu Yusuf: He does not sin, because it is a concession, meaning that sin is negated for the one in necessity, and the prohibition is not lifted by necessity. Allah said: 'But whoever is forced [by necessity], neither desiring nor transgressing, there is no sin upon him.' (al-Baqarah 2:173) Since the prohibition remains, he is taking it with its gravity.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage begins as a ruling statement on coercion to eat forbidden items, then expands into an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The main ruling distinguishes between two levels of coercion: non-life-threatening coercion (imprisonment, beating, chains) does not make the forbidden permissible, while life-threatening coercion (death, amputation) does. The reason is that these items are only permitted in cases of true necessity, defined as fear for life or limb. The commentator clarifies that if the coercion is not severe enough to cause such fear, the prohibition remains. Abu Hanifa's position holds that if a person is coerced under threat of death and refrains from eating, he commits a sin because he is assisting his own destruction when the act had become permissible. Abu Yusuf disagrees, citing the Quranic verse that negates sin for one compelled by necessity, arguing that the prohibition remains in place and the person is taking a dispensation without sin. The practical difference lies in whether the person sins if he chooses to die rather than eat the forbidden food.

Key Terms
الميتة (al-maytah): Carrion; an animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter.
الضرورة (al-ḍarūrah): A state of necessity that permits normally forbidden acts, defined as fear for life or limb.
الإثم (al-ithm): Sin; the moral and legal culpability for wrongful acts.
الرخصة (al-rukhṣah): A legal concession that allows an otherwise forbidden act under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of coercion makes eating carrion permissible?
A) Imprisonment
B) Beating
C) Death threat
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only life-threatening coercion (mulji') permits eating prohibited food; non-life-threatening coercion does not.

Uttering Kufr Under Duress: The Case of Ammar ibn Yasir
Explaining the permissibility of uttering kufr under duress based on the hadith of Ammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on uttering kufr under duress, identify the condition of heart's tranquility, and explain the proof from the hadith of Ammar.

Core Idea
Outward kufr under duress is permissible if the heart remains tranquil in iman.

The story of Ammar ibn Yasir is a foundational case in Hanafi fiqh for understanding the limits of compulsion (ikrah). When the Prophet ﷺ asked Ammar how he found his heart and received the answer 'tranquil with faith,' he granted a license to outwardly comply if forced again. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proof, and the critical distinction between this and other prohibitions like wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of uttering words of disbelief (kufr) under duress. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) as a license (rukhsah), provided the heart remains firm in faith.
This is not a general permissibility; it is conditional.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith of Ammar ibn Yasir. The Prophet ﷺ instructed, 'If they return, then return.' This is a command (amr) from the Prophet.
The hadith is ahad (single-chain), thus zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, the lowest degree of a command (amr) is permissibility (ibaha). Therefore, the Prophet's instruction indicates that uttering kufr under duress is permissible, not obligatory.
This interpretation refutes the idea that the command requires literal repetition of kufr.
4
Identify the condition
The permissibility hinges on the heart being 'tranquil with faith' (mutma'inna bil iman). The Prophet explicitly asked Ammar about his heart's state before giving the license.
If the heart doubts or accepts kufr, the act is real apostasy.
5
Distinguish from other prohibitions
Unlike wine or carrion, which become permissible under genuine necessity (darurah), kufr never becomes essentially permissible. Only its outward expression is licensed under duress because inner faith remains intact.
The right of Allah (iman) is not completely lost, while the right of the servant (life) is preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأظهر عمار وكان قلبه مطمئناً بالإيمان، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «فإن عادوا فعد». والفرق بين هذا وبين شرب الخمر أنّ شرب الخمر يحل عند الضرورة ، والكفر لا يحل أبداً فيرخص إظهاره مع قيام دليل الحرمة ؛ لأن حقه يفوت بالكلية، وحق الله تعالى لا يفوت بالكلية ؛ لأن التصديق بالقلب باق. عمار بن ياسر له فلم يتركوه حتى سب النبي ، وذكر آلهتهم بخير، ثم تركوه ، فلما أتى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : ما تركت حتى نلت منك ، وذكرت آلهتهم بخير، قال: فكيف تجد قلبك ؟ قال : مطمئناً بالإيمان ، قال : فإن عادوا فعد. [1] قوله : فعد ؛ أي إلى طمأنينة القلب، وما قيل من أن معناه : فعد إلى ما كان منك من السب والكفر والطمأنينة جميعاً فغلط ؛ لأنّ أدنى درجات الأمر الإباحة ، فتكون إجراء كلمة الكفر ،مباحاً ، وليس كذلك ؛ لأنّ الكفر مما لا ينكشف حرمته. [2] قوله : والفرق ...الخ ؛ هذا جواب سؤال مقدر تقريره أن يقال : ما الفرق بين إجراء كلمة الكفر وبين المحرّمات المتقدمة حتى يصير المكره بالامتناع عنه إلى أن يقتل مأجوراً مفضلاً، وبالامتناع عنها ائماً. وتقرير الجواب : إنّ شرب الخمر يحلّ عند الضرورة، والكفر لا يحل أبداً ؛ لأنّ قبحه في النهاية، والامتناع لإعزاز الدين عزيمة، بخلاف ما تقدم من أكل الميتة وشرب الخمر، فإنّ الحرمة هناك تكون باقية للاستثناء كما تقدم، فيرخص...الخ. [3] قوله : لأن حقه...الخ ؛ دليل عقلي لرخصة إظهار كلمة الكفر مع قيام دليل الحرمة، تقريره : إنّ الإيمان لا يفوتُ بهذا الإظهار حقيقة ؛ لأن الركن الأصلي فيه التصديق، وهو قائم حقيقة والإقرار ركن زائد، وهو قائم تقديراً ؛ لأنّ التكرار ليس بشرط.
And Ammar showed (kufr) while his heart was tranquil with faith, so the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'If they return, then return.' And the difference between this and drinking wine is that drinking wine becomes permissible at necessity, while kufr never becomes permissible, so its expression is licensed while the evidence of prohibition stands; because the right of the servant is completely lost, while the right of Allah Most High is not completely lost because the attestation (tasdiq) of the heart remains. The story of Ammar ibn Yasir: they did not leave him until he reviled the Prophet and mentioned their gods well, then they left him. When he came to the Messenger of Allah, he said: 'I did not leave until I spoke ill of you and mentioned their gods well.' He asked: 'How do you find your heart?' He replied: 'Tranquil with faith.' He said: 'If they return, then return.' [1] His saying: 'then return' means to tranquility of the heart. What is said—that its meaning is return to what you did of reviling and kufr and tranquility together—is wrong, because the lowest degree of a command is permissibility (ibaha), so uttering the word of kufr would be permissible, which is not the case because the prohibition of kufr is never removed. [2] His saying: 'And the difference...' is the answer to an implied question: 'What is the difference between uttering the word of kufr and the previous prohibitions so that the one compelled (mukrah) who refrains until killed is rewarded and preferred, while refraining from those (prohibitions under duress) is sin?' The answer: Drinking wine becomes permissible at necessity, while kufr never becomes permissible because its evil is extreme, and refraining for the honor of the religion is a resolve ('azima), unlike the previous examples of eating carrion and drinking wine, where the prohibition remains with an exception as mentioned, so it is licensed... [3] His saying: 'Because the right...' is a rational proof for licensing the expression of kufr while the evidence of prohibition stands. Its explanation: Faith is not actually lost by this expression because the essential pillar of it is attestation (tasdiq), which remains in reality, while declaration (iqrar) is an additional pillar and remains in estimation (taqdiran), because repetition is not a condition.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the topic of ikrah (duress). It addresses the specific case of uttering words of kufr under duress, using the well-known story of Ammar ibn Yasir. The author begins by stating the ruling: Ammar outwardly expressed kufr, but his heart was tranquil with faith. The Prophet responded with the directive 'if they return, then return,' which is interpreted as a license (rukhsah) to outwardly express kufr if compelled, provided the heart remains firm. The commentary then distinguishes this from other prohibitions like drinking wine: under genuine necessity (darurah), wine consumption is permissible, but kufr is never essentially permissible; only its outward expression is licensed due to duress, because the servant's right (life) is lost if he refuses, while God's right (faith) is not lost since inner belief remains. The story of Ammar is quoted as the primary proof (dalil naqli). The commentary on the word 'fad' (return) clarifies that it means return to tranquility of heart, not to further kufr. The rational proof ('aqli dalil) is that the essential pillar of faith—attestation (tasdiq)—remains intact, while declaration (iqrar) is an additional pillar that is not a condition for the validity of faith under duress. Thus, the ruling is that uttering kufr under duress is permissible (mubah) as a license, but the underlying prohibition remains. The Hanafi position emphasizes that the heart must be settled in belief; otherwise, it is not permissible.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compulsion or duress that forces a person to act against their will, under threat of death or severe harm.
طمأنينة القلب (tuma'ninat al-qalb): Tranquility of the heart; the state of being firmly settled in faith without inward doubt or acceptance of kufr.
إجراء كلمة الكفر (ijra' kalimat al-kufr): Uttering words of disbelief; the outward pronunciation of kufr without inward belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the permissibility of uttering kufr under duress according to the Hanafi school, based on the story of Ammar?
A) The person must be in a state of extreme necessity such that they would die otherwise.
B) The heart must remain tranquil and settled in iman (faith) while uttering.
C) The utterance must be done only once and not repeated.
D) The person must seek a fatwa before uttering.

Key Takeaway
Uttering kufr under duress is permissible only when the heart remains tranquil in iman; otherwise, it is real apostasy.

Uttering Kufr vs. Drinking Wine Under Coercion
Differentiating the ruling on uttering kufr from drinking wine under duress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on uttering kufr under coercion, distinguish it from drinking wine under necessity, and explain the rational proof for the concession.

Core Idea
Kufr remains intrinsically haram; only outward utterance is excused under coercion, unlike wine which becomes halal by necessity.

The Book of Coercion opens with a critical distinction: how can uttering words of disbelief ever be permitted? The Hanafi school answers by citing the famous hadith of 'Ammar ibn Yasir, who was forced to insult the Prophet ﷺ and praise idols. The Prophet ﷺ affirmed his inner faith and gave a measured response. This lesson unpacks that hadith, the rational proof behind it, and the precise difference between a concession (rukhsah) and a genuine dispensation (ibahah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State the act in question (uttering words of kufr under coercion) and its legal classification: permissible as a concession, but the act itself remains haram.
The ruling is not that kufr becomes halal; it is a rukhsah preserving life while inner faith remains.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith of 'Ammar: the Prophet's command 'fad' (return) and the context of coercion.
The dalil is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut), but accepted in the school.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Consider how the imperative 'fad' is interpreted—it indicates permission to outwardly comply to save life, not permission to commit kufr.
The lowest degree of a command is ibahah, but that would contradict the eternal prohibition of kufr; thus the command is for the concession to tranquility of heart.
4
Contrast with drinking wine under necessity
Unlike kufr, wine consumption becomes genuinely halal (mubah) under force of dire necessity (darurah).
The prohibition of kufr is absolute and never removed; only its outward manifestation is excused.
5
Understand the rational proof
The right of the servant (life) would be entirely lost if he refused, whereas the right of Allah (faith) is not entirely lost because inner affirmation persists.
This is a dalil 'aqli (rational argument) supporting the concession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه/ تعريفه وشروطه وأحكامه. وأظهر عمار وكان قلبه مطمئناً بالإيمان، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «فإن عادوا فعد». والفرق بين هذا وبين شرب الخمر أنّ شرب الخمر يحل عند الضرورة ، والكفر لا يحل أبداً فير خُص إظهاره مع قيام دليل الحرمة ؛ لأن حقه " يفوتُ بالكلية، وحق الله تعالى لا يفوتُ بالكلية ؛ لأن التصديق بالقلب باق. عمار بن ياسر له فلم يتركوه حتى سب النبي ، وذكر آلهتهم بخير، ثم تركوه ، فلما أتى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : ما تركتُ حتى نلت منك ، وذكرت آلهتهم بخير، قال: فكيف تجد قلبك ؟ قال : مطمئناً بالإيمان ، قال : فإن عادوا فعد (۱). كذا في «نصب الراية لحديث الهداية» (٢). Book of Coercion / Its definition, conditions, and rulings. And 'Ammar declared [kufr] while his heart was at peace with faith, so the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'If they return, then return.' And the difference between this and drinking wine is that drinking wine is permitted under necessity, but kufr is never permitted, so its utterance is permitted as a concession while the evidence of prohibition remains, because the right of the servant is entirely lost, but the right of Allah is not entirely lost because affirmation with the heart remains. 'Ammar ibn Yasir: they did not leave him until he insulted the Prophet and spoke well of their gods, then they left him. When he came to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), he said: 'I did not stop until I spoke ill of you and spoke well of their gods.' He said: 'How do you find your heart?' He said: 'At peace with faith.' He said: 'If they return, then return.' Thus in 'Nasb al-Rayah li-Hadith al-Hidayah'. [1] His saying: 'then return' means to tranquility of heart. What has been said that its meaning is 'return to what you were doing of insult, kufr, and tranquility together' is mistaken, because the lowest degree of a command is permissibility, so uttering the word of kufr would be permissible, which is not the case, because kufr is such that its prohibition is never removed. Thus in 'al-'Inayah'. [2] His saying: 'and the difference...' This is an answer to an implied question: 'What is the difference between uttering the word of kufr and the previous prohibitions such that the coerced person, by refraining from it until he is killed, is rewarded and virtuous, but by refraining from those prohibitions he is sinful?' And the answer: Drinking wine is permitted under necessity, but kufr is never permitted because its evil is extreme, and refraining is a resolve to honor the religion, unlike the previous cases of eating carrion and drinking wine, where the prohibition remains due to the exception as mentioned, so it is permitted... (3) His saying: 'because his right...' is a rational proof for the concession to utter the word of kufr while the evidence of prohibition stands. Its explanation: Faith is not actually lost by this utterance because the essential pillar is affirmation, which actually remains, while declaration is an additional pillar which exists constructively because repetition is not a condition.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah) by first addressing a critical legal question: what is the ruling on uttering words of kufr under coercion? The author cites the well-known hadith of 'Ammar ibn Yasir, who was forced by the polytheists to insult the Prophet and speak well of their gods. When he reported this to the Prophet ﷺ, the Prophet asked about the state of his heart. 'Ammar replied that his heart was firm in faith, and the Prophet said: 'If they return, then return'—meaning return to that state of inner tranquility, not to the overt act of kufr. The passage then distinguishes this case from drinking wine under necessity. The key distinction is that wine becomes permissible when one is forced by extreme hunger or thirst, as a genuine dispensation that lifts the prohibition. In contrast, kufr (disbelief) is never permissible in itself; only the outward utterance is permitted as a concession (rukhsah) to save one's life, while the inner belief remains intact. The commentator explains that the right of the servant (life) would be entirely lost if he refused, but the right of Allah (the prohibition of kufr) is not entirely lost because the essential faith remains in the heart. This is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) for the concession. The passage also refutes a potential misunderstanding: some might think the Prophet's command 'fad' (return) means to return to both the insult and the tranquility, but that would render the act permissible (mubah) which is incorrect because kufr is never permissible. Rather, the command is to return to the tranquility of the heart, not to the act of kufr.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion: forcing someone to do something against their will under threat of death or severe harm.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity: a situation where one fears for life or limb, permitting otherwise prohibited acts.
رخصة (rukhsah): Concession: a permission granted due to an excuse while the original prohibition remains in effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is uttering words of kufr under coercion permitted while drinking wine under necessity is also permitted?
A) Because both are equally permissible under duress.
B) Because drinking wine actually becomes halal, while uttering kufr remains haram but is allowed as a concession.
C) Because the heart's faith is not lost when uttering kufr, while wine drinking affects the mind.
D) Because the Prophet specifically permitted both actions.

Key Takeaway
Uttering kufr under coercion is a concession that preserves life and inner faith, but kufr itself remains never permissible—unlike wine which becomes halal in dire necessity.

Liability for Property Damage Under Coercion in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on destroying property and who bears liability under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the coercer (mukrih) is liable for property destruction committed by the coerced person, and distinguish between the permissibility of destroying property and the absolute prohibition of killing under coercion.

Core Idea
The coerced is a tool; the coercer bears liability for property damage, but killing a Muslim is never allowed.

In the previous lesson we examined the conditions for coercion to excuse an act. Here we explore a specific application: when a person is forced to destroy another Muslim's property or even kill. The passage establishes a crucial distinction between property and life, and the liability of the coercer. The key is the instrument principle: the coerced acts as a mere tool of the coercer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage addresses two actions under coercion: destroying property (permissible as a concession) and killing (never permissible). State each ruling and its scope.
The concession for property is based on necessity (ḍarūrah), akin to eating carrion in famine.
2
Locate the rational evidence (dalīl ʿaqlī)
The proof is the instrument principle: the coerced person (mukrah) is an instrument (ālah) of the coercer (mukrih). The coercer is the effective cause, so liability falls on him.
The passage gives an example: the coercer can physically throw the coerced onto property to destroy it, making the coercer the true agent.
3
Understand the distinction with uttering kufr
A potential objection: if uttering kufr is permitted under coercion, why is killing not? The answer: the essence of kufr is not affected by utterance, whereas killing results in certain loss of life (a right of the servant).
Uttering kufr is a verbal act that does not destroy faith; killing destroys an inviolable life.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The Hanafi ruling means the coercer must pay compensation (ḍamān) for property damage, while the coerced is not liable. Some schools might hold the coerced liable if he had a choice.
This difference arises from varying views on agency under coercion.

[٢) قوله : وضمن المكره - بالكسر - ؛ لان المكره – بالفتح - ، آلة للمكرِه فيما يصلح آلة له، والإتلافُ من هذا القبيل، إذ المكره يمكن أن يأخذ المكره ويلقيه على المال فيتلفه، وإنما قلنا فيما يصلح احترازاً عن الأكل والتكلم والوطء، فإنّه فيها لا يصلح آلة له. كذا في «العناية» (٢). And a concession is given to him to destroy the property of a Muslim by these two (i.e., killing and cutting), and the coercer (with kasr of ra) is liable, because in actions the doer becomes an instrument for the impeller. For we say: That which is never permissible is the very essence of kufr which is absolutely incompatible with faith, and that which is conceded is its utterance which does not contravene the heart's tranquility with faith. This utterance is permitted only due to an excuse, as the Mufti of the Two Sanctuaries stated, interpreting concession as permissibility due to an excuse while evidence of prohibition remains. Thus it is established that everything that is not permissible by necessity is definitely not granted a concession, such as killing, for example.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on ikrah, now addressing the permissibility of destroying a Muslim's property when one is coerced under threat of death or amputation. The ruling is that it is permissible to destroy the property of another Muslim in order to save oneself from being killed or maimed. The key point is that the coercer (mukrih, with kasr on ra) is held liable for the damage, not the coerced person. The reasoning is that the coerced person acts merely as an instrument (aalah) of the coercer; the coercer is the true agent. The passage also clarifies that killing a Muslim is never permissible, even under such coercion—unlike destroying property, which is allowed due to necessity. The commentator addresses a potential objection: if uttering kufr is permitted as a concession, why is killing not permitted? The distinction is that kufr is only a verbal expression and does not affect the essence of faith, whereas killing results in the certain loss of a human life, which is a right of the servant that cannot be forfeited.

Key Terms
مكره (بالكسر) (mukrih): The coercer: the one who forces another to act under threat.
مكره (بالفتح) (mukrah): The coerced person: the one who is forced to act.
ضمان (daman): Liability: the obligation to compensate for damage or loss.

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, a coerced person is an instrument; the coercer bears liability for property damage, but no necessity justifies killing a Muslim.

Qisas Applies Only to the Coercer
Clarifying that only the coercer is subject to qisas for murder under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas in cases of coercion and explain the rational proof (dalil aqli) that exempts the coerced person.

Core Idea
Only the coercer is liable for qisas; the coerced person is exempt because preserving one's own life overrides the prohibition of killing under duress.

This passage from the sharh explains the Hanafi position on qisas (retribution) when someone is coerced to kill. The ruling is unambiguous: the coercer alone bears criminal liability. The text provides a rational proof based on the certainty of harm to the coerced person. We'll explore the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that only the coercer (mukrih) is subject to qisas. Note that this applies when the killing is intentional and the coercion involves a threat of death.
The ruling covers cases where the coercer is sane, insane, or a minor.
2
Understand the rational proof (al-'illa)
The text reasons: if the coerced person refuses, he will certainly lose his own life. This certain loss is weighed against the conjectural violation of Allah's right (the prohibition of killing). Since one is certain, preserving his own right (life) takes precedence.
This is a classic example of daf' al-darar al-muhaqqaq (repelling certain harm).
3
Examine the clash of rights
The victim's right (haqq al-'abd) is lost certainly, while Allah's right (haqq Allah) is only conjecturally violated. In this conflict, the coerced person may incline to preserve his own life.
The Hanafi school uses this hierarchy to justify the exemption.
4
Apply the ruling to different coercers
The passage explicitly includes insane persons and minors as coercers. Regardless of the coercer's capacity, he is liable for qisas because he caused the killing.
This shows the rule is based on causation, not mental state of the coercer.
5
Contrast with other madhahib
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold the coerced person liable if he had a realistic opportunity to escape or resist. The Hanafi position is stricter in exempting the coerced.
The practical difference (thamarah) is who pays diyah or faces qisas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقاد المكره فقط) : أي إن كان القتل عمداً وفي الامتناع فوت نفسه حقيقة، فكان مما اجتمع فيه فوت حق العبد يقيناً، وفوت حق الله تعالى توهماً، فيسعه الميل إلى إحياء حقه. كذا في «العناية). [٤]قوله : ويقاد المكره فقط ؛ قال في «النهاية»: سواء كان المكره الأمر يانعاً عاقلاً أو معتوهاً أو غلاماً غير يانع ، فالقود على الآمر، ونسبه إلى «المبسوط»، فنسب الشيخ
And only the coercer is subject to retribution: meaning if the killing was intentional and in refraining there is actual loss of his own life, then it is a case where the loss of the right of the servant is certain and the loss of the right of Allah is conjectural, so it is permissible for him to incline to preserve his right. Thus in al-'Inayah. [4] His saying: 'And only the coercer is subject to retribution'; it is said in al-Nihayah: Whether the coercer is a mature sane person, or an insane person, or a non-mature boy, the retribution is upon the commander, and he attributed it to al-Mabsut, then the Sheikh...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on coercion by addressing the question of qisas (retribution) when a coerced person kills someone. The ruling is clear: only the coercer (mukrih) is subject to qisas, not the coerced person. The reasoning is that when the coerced person faces a choice between being killed himself (certain loss of his own life) and killing another (which involves the right of the victim and the right of Allah), he may choose to preserve his own life because the loss of the victim's right is certain while the violation of Allah's right (the prohibition of murder) is conjectural in this extreme situation. The commentary cites al-Nihayah and al-Mabsut, noting that this ruling applies regardless of the coercer's mental state or age—whether sane, insane, or a minor—the retribution is always on the one who issued the command (the coercer). The text cuts off mentioning the Sheikh's attribution, but the ruling is well-established in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
قود (qisas): Retributive punishment: execution of the murderer as a legal penalty.
مكره (بالكسر) (mukrih): The coercer who commands the killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, only the coercer is subject to qisas when murder is committed under coercion that threatens certain death, based on the rational principle that certain harm to the coerced person outweighs conjectural violation of divine right.

Liability for Property Destruction under Duress
Ruling and proof with qiyas and instrumentality concept

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on destroying property under duress and identify who bears liability based on the instrumentality principle.

Core Idea
The coerced is a tool; the coercer pays.

Imagine a man forced at knifepoint to smash a neighbor's window. Who pays? This passage explains the Hanafi answer: the act is permissible for the coerced, but the coercer is financially liable because the coerced is regarded as his instrument. The ruling is analogized to eating forbidden food in extreme hunger.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on destroying property under duress
Identify that the act is permissible (mubah) for the coerced under genuine duress, and that the coercer bears the financial liability.
The coerced is not liable even though he physically performs the act.
2
Find the evidence (dalil)
The author uses qiyas (analogy) to the case of extreme hunger (makhmasa) where property is permissible due to necessity.
The common illa is darura (necessity).
3
Understand the instrumentality principle
The coerced (mukrah) is treated as an instrument (āla) of the coercer (mukri) for acts where the coercer can physically manipulate him, such as destruction.
This principle does NOT apply to volitional acts like eating, speaking, or intercourse.
4
Apply the distinction to other acts
Realize that acts where the coerced's will is indispensable (e.g., committing perjury) are not covered by the instrumentality principle; here the coerced may bear some responsibility.
The passage explicitly excludes eating, speaking, and intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورخص له إتلافُ مالِ مسلم بهما، وضُمِّن المكْرِه. [1] قوله : ورخص له...الخ؛ لأن مال الغير يستباح للضرورة، كما في حالة المخمصة، وقد تحققت الضرورة. [2] قوله : وضمن المكره - بالكسر -؛ لان المكره – بالفتح -، آلة للمكرِه فيما يصلح آلة له، والإتلافُ من هذا القبيل، إذ المكره يمكن أن يأخذ المكره ويلقيه على المال فيتلفه، وإنما قلنا فيما يصلح احترازاً عن الأكل والتكلم والوطء، فإنّه فيها لا يصلح آلة له. كذا في «العناية».
Permission is given to him to destroy the property of a Muslim by both [killing and cutting], and the coercer is liable for compensation. [1] His statement: 'Permission is given to him... etc.' because the property of another is permissible due to necessity, as in the case of extreme hunger, and necessity has been realized. [2] His statement: 'and the coercer is liable' - with a kasra on the ra' - because the coerced person (with fatha on the ra') is an instrument for the coercer in what is suitable as an instrument for him, and destruction is of this type, for the coercer can take the coerced person and throw him onto the property, causing its destruction. Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling that a person under duress may destroy another's property without liability, because necessity makes it permissible, like eating carrion in starvation. The coercer (mukri) is solely liable for compensation because the coerced (mukrah) is regarded as an instrument (āla) of the coercer. The instrumentality applies to acts like destruction where the coercer can physically manipulate the coerced (e.g., throwing him onto property). It does not apply to inherently volitional acts like eating, speaking, or intercourse, where the coerced' s will is indispensable. This distinction is taken from al-'Inayah.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrah): The person coerced under duress; in this passage, the one who commits the act but is not liable.
مكره (mukri): The coercer; the one who compels another under threat, and bears liability.
آلة (āla): Instrument; the coerced person is treated as a tool of the coercer for acts where he can be physically manipulated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

8 min
Killing Under Duress is Never Permissible
States that killing under duress is never permissible and rebuts an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that killing under duress is absolutely forbidden and distinguish it from acts that can be licensed under necessity.

Core Idea
Killing a Muslim is absolutely forbidden; no necessity can license it.

This passage clarifies that killing an innocent Muslim under duress remains haram, even if the duress is life-threatening. It addresses a potential confusion: if one can outwardly display disbelief under duress, why not kill? The distinction lies in the nature of the act — killing is intrinsically harmful and destroys life, while displaying disbelief is a mere outward expression that does not affect inner faith. The Hanafi position is that such absolutely prohibited acts are never subject to concession (rukhsah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that killing a Muslim without right is absolutely forbidden and cannot be licensed by any necessity. Confirm the hukm level: haram.
2
Understand the objection
The author anticipates a query: if disbelief is never permissible yet its display is licensed under duress (Quran 16:106), why is killing not similarly licensed? This objection relies on an analogy between the two.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The response distinguishes between the inner state of disbelief (which is absolutely incompatible with faith) and the outward act of displaying it. Killing, however, is an act that is intrinsically harmful; the harm to another person cannot be excused. Thus the analogy fails.
Key principle: acts that are intrinsically harmful cannot be licensed, unlike statements or actions that only affect the agent.
4
Apply the principle to other cases
Consider property destruction: under duress, one may be forced to destroy another's property. Unlike killing, property can be sacrificed for greater necessity because it is not intrinsically inviolable. The passage implies this contrast.
The Hanafi school holds that property can be destroyed under duress if the coercer threatens life, but the owner is entitled to compensation from the coerced person.
