Not killing him. [3] His statement: 'It is not permissible due to necessity'; meaning that killing a Muslim without right is something that is not permitted for any necessity, so likewise it is not permitted for this necessity. It cannot be said: It is understood from what the author mentioned that whatever is not permissible is not licensed, and disbelief is never permissible yet its display is licensed. For we say: what is never permissible is the very disbelief that is absolutely incompatible with faith, and what is licensed is its display, which does not contradict the heart's tranquility with faith. This display is only permitted due to an excuse, as the Mufti of the two weighty beings stated, interpreting license as permission due to an excuse while the evidence of prohibition remains. Thus it is established that whatever is not permissible due to necessity is absolutely not licensed, like killing for example.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains why killing under duress is never permissible, even when the duress is severe. Unlike destroying property, which can be licensed under necessity, killing an innocent Muslim is absolutely prohibited and cannot be licensed. The author addresses a potential objection: if disbelief (kufr) is never permissible but its display is licensed under duress, why is killing not similarly licensed? The response is that what is licensed is merely the outward display of disbelief, not the inner state of disbelief itself, which is intrinsically evil. Killing, however, is an act that is intrinsically harmful (it destroys life) and cannot be excused. The principle is that if something is absolutely unlawful (like murder), it cannot become lawful under any circumstances, unlike property which can be sacrificed for greater necessity.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsah): License; permission to do an otherwise forbidden act due to an excuse while the prohibition remains in principle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is killing under duress not licensed even though displaying disbelief under duress is licensed?
A) Because killing is a worse sin than disbelief.
B) Because killing is an act that causes direct harm to another, whereas displaying disbelief is a mere outward expression.
C) Because duress for killing is never severe enough.
D) Because killing involves two parties, while disbelief is personal.

Key Takeaway
Killing a Muslim is absolutely forbidden, and no necessity, including duress, can make it permissible.

Coercion in Slave Emancipation and Divorce: Recourse and Half Dower
Explains the ruling on coercion in freeing a slave and divorce, with recourse for value and half dower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on financial recourse when a person is coerced into freeing a slave or divorcing his wife, and explain why coercion does not invalidate vows, oaths, and similar verbal acts.

Core Idea
Coercion shifts liability for property loss to the coercer, but does not affect acts that cannot be rescinded after occurrence.

Coercion (ikrah) can force a person to act against his will, but the law does not leave the victim unprotected. The Hanafi school holds that when a person is compelled to free a slave or divorce his wife, the coercer must compensate the victim for the resulting property loss. However, this principle does not extend to purely verbal commitments like vows, which remain valid regardless of duress. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these rules and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling and its scope
Recognize that the passage rules that the coerced person recovers the value of the slave and half the dower from the coercer. Scope: emancipation always; divorce only before intercourse.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (talil)
Emancipation is destruction of property attributed to the coercer (the cause). For divorce, half the dower is at risk of falling, so its confirmation by divorce is akin to destruction.
The coerced is treated as an instrument for the coercer in property acts.
3
Analyze the counterargument and its response
The passage presents an objection: the dower is due by contract, divorce only triggers payment, so it is not destruction. The Hanafi response emphasizes that the half dower was on the verge of being forfeited, and the divorce ensures payment, making it a loss attributed to the coercer.
This is a key point of talil in the text.
4
Examine the distinction based on intention
If the coerced intended the emancipation as demanded, the coercer is liable. If he intended a false declaration of past freedom, he is considered willing and no recourse.
Intention determines whether the act is truly coerced.
5
Apply the general rule for acts not subject to rescission
Acts like vows, oaths, zihar, and revocation are valid despite coercion because they cannot be undone after occurrence. The coercer has no financial liability for such acts.
The rule: coercion affects bindingness, not validity, for non-rescindable acts.

Book of coercion / its definition, conditions, and rulings. And he recovers the value of the slave and half the dower if he did not have intercourse. Meaning: the coerced person recovers from the one who coerced him in the case of coercion by emancipation the value of the slave, because emancipation insofar as it is destruction is attributed to the cause [the coercer] because destruction is an act, and it is possible in it to make the doer an instrument for the cause, even if that is not possible in speech. And he recovers from him in coercion by divorce half the dower if intercourse did not occur, because the half dower is on the verge of falling by the separation coming from the woman, so it is confirmed by divorce before intercourse, so from this aspect it is destruction and is attributed to the cause, making the doer an instrument for it, contrary to after intercourse because the dowry is established by intercourse. And it can be said: the dowry is obligatory by contract, and divorce is its condition, and the ruling is not attributed to it, and also its falling by separation is mere speculation, so it is not considered. He said: the coerced one recovers up to his saying: the value of the slave. It is said: this is if the coerced one said: I intended by my saying 'he is free' an independent emancipation as was demanded of me, then the slave is freed judicially and religiously, and the coerced one guarantees the value of the slave because he did what he was commanded according to what he coerced him, and likewise if he said: nothing occurred to me except doing what was demanded. And if he said: it occurred to me to declare freedom falsely in the past, and I intended that not the creation of freedom, the slave is freed judicially but not religiously because he deviated from what he was coerced upon, so he was obedient in the confession, so the judge does not believe him in the claim of false declaration, and the coerced one does not guarantee anything because the slave was freed by confession obediently not by coercion. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. He said: And if that is not possible in speech, then emancipation in terms of speech is confined to the emancipator, for if it transferred to the coercer in terms of speech as well due to fear of destruction, the slave would not be freed definitely. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. He said: It is confirmed by divorce, and confirmation has a semblance of obligation, so it is as if he obligated upon the coercer. Because what was upon him was on the verge of falling by the occurrence of separation from her side due to sin like apostasy and kissing the stepson, and that was confirmed by divorce, so it became a stabilization of wealth from this aspect, so its stabilization is attributed to the cause, and stabilization is like obligation, so he was a destroyer of it, so he recovers upon him, contrary to if he had intercourse with her because the stabilized is stabilized here by intercourse not by divorce. That initially was a destruction of wealth. He said: established by intercourse, i.e., not by divorce, so it remains mere destruction of the right of marriage, and it is not wealth, so he is not liable with wealth because there is no equivalence between what is wealth and what is not valuable wealth, and its value at the time of possession by marriage is to show the significance of the dowry, and this significance is for the possessed not for the right upon which it occurs. Do you not see that removal of ownership without witnesses and without a guardian is valid, so there is no need to show significance at the time of destroying ownership; therefore the destroyer does not guarantee anything, and likewise it is not obligatory upon the two witnesses of divorce after upon revocation. Thus in al-Kifaya. Guarantee. He said: And his vow and his oath etc., i.e., his vow is valid. The author intended to explain what coercion affects and what it does not affect. The rule: whatever cannot be invalidated after its occurrence, coercion does not affect it in terms of preventing validity, because coercion is due to lack of consent, and lack of consent affects non-bindingness, and non-bindingness enables the coerced one to annul, so coercion enables the coerced one to annul after realization, so what cannot admit rescission, coercion does not affect it, so the vow is valid with coercion. Thus in al-Inaya. He said in al-Hidaya: And there is no recourse upon the coercer for what became binding upon him because there is no demandant for it in this world, so he is not liable for it therein. Likewise the oath and zihar are not affected by coercion because they do not admit rescission. Likewise revocation, Ila, and Fai' in it by word because they are valid with jest.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the legal rulings of coercion (ikrah) in various acts, particularly the effect of coercion on property and verbal obligations. The commentator explains that when a person is coerced into freeing a slave or divorcing his wife, the coerced party (the one who performed the act under duress) can recover the value of the slave or half the dower from the coercer. This is because the act of destruction (itlaf) is attributed to the coercer, not to the coerced actor. The commentator makes a distinction between property-based actions like emancipation, where loss can be physically attributed, and verbal actions where such attribution is not possible. However, in the case of divorce before consummation, the half dower is considered at risk and thus its confirmation by divorce is seen as a kind of destruction attributed to the coercer. The passage then addresses vows, oaths, zihar, and revocation. It states that coercion does not invalidate these acts because they are purely verbal and cannot be rescinded after occurrence. The underlying principle is that coercion affects the binding nature of an act but not its validity if the act is not subject to subsequent rescission. Thus, if someone is coerced to make a vow, the vow is valid. The commentator also notes that the coerced person has no recourse against the coercer for these acts because there is no material loss to claim. The passage provides a thorough discussion of the legal reasoning (talil) behind these rulings, including the concept of making the coerced person an instrument for the coercer in property matters.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion: compelling someone to perform an act against their will by threat of harm.
إتلاف (itlaf): Destruction or loss of property; the basis for liability.
نذر (nadhr): Vow: promising to perform an act of worship or charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person is coerced into making a vow (nadhr), is the vow valid?
A) Yes, because coercion does not affect acts that cannot be rescinded after occurrence.
B) No, because coercion invalidates all actions.
C) Yes, but only if the coerced person consents afterward.
D) No, because vows require free will.

Key Takeaway
The coercer is liable for property loss resulting from coercion, but purely verbal obligations like vows remain valid and give no recourse.

Coerced Vows, Oaths, and Revocations: The Principle of Non-Rescission
Principle that acts not admitting rescission are valid under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that acts not admitting rescission are valid under coercion and distinguish such acts from those that are affected by coercion.

Core Idea
Vows, oaths, and revocations are valid under coercion because they cannot be rescinded afterwards.

This passage from al-Hidayah and al-'Inayah establishes a key Hanafi principle: coercion does not affect the validity of acts that cannot be rescinded after occurrence. While coercion removes consent (rida) and makes an act non-binding (ghayr lazim), non-bindingness enables rescission. But if the act itself does not admit rescission (faskh), coercion has no effect on its validity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its practical application to vows, oaths, dhihar, and revocation (raj'a).

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
The passage states that vows, oaths, dhihar, revocation, ila', and fay' are valid under coercion. Identify the common feature: these acts cannot be rescinded once performed.
Acts like sale or hire admit rescission and are treated differently.
2
Understand the underlying principle
Coercion removes consent, which makes an act non-binding (ghayr lazim). Non-binding allows the coerced person to rescind the act. If the act does not admit rescission, coercion has no effect on validity.
Rescission is the key variable.
3
Apply the principle to specific acts
For each listed act (vow, oath, etc.), verify if rescission is possible. Vows: once made, cannot be undone. Therefore valid despite coercion.
The same reasoning applies to oaths, dhihar, and revocation.
4
Examine the legal consequence
The coerced person cannot seek compensation from the coercer because the obligation is binding and enforceable.
The text says 'no recourse against the coercer'.
5
Distinguish from acts that admit rescission
Contrast with contracts like sale, where coercion makes the contract non-binding and subject to rescission.
This distinction is critical for understanding Hanafi coercion rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونذره، ويمينه، وظهاره، ورجعته

[٢] قوله : ونذره ويمينه...الخ ؛ أي صح ،نذره، أراد المصنف له أن يبين ما يعمل فيه الإكراه وما لا يعمل. فالضابطة : كلّ ما لا يؤثر فيه الفسخُ بعد وقوعه، لا يعمل فيه الإكراه من حيث منع الصحة ; لأنَّ الإكراه لفواتِ الرضاء ، وفواتُ الرّضاء مؤثر في عدم اللزوم، وعدم اللزوم يمكن المكره من الفسخ ، فالإكراه يمكّن المكره من الفسخ بعد التحقق ، فما لا يحتمل الفسخ لا يعمل فيه الإكراه، فيصح النذر مع الإكراه. كذا في «العناية». قال في «الهداية» : ولا رجوع على المكره بما لزمه ; لأنه لا مطالب له في الدنيا ، فلا يطالب بـه فيها، وكذا اليمين والظهار لا يعمل فيهما الإكراه لعدم احتمالهما الفسخ، وكذا الرجعة والإيلاء والفيء فيه باللسان ; لأنها تصح مع الهزل
And his vow, and his oath, and his dhihar, and his revocation... [2] His statement: 'and his vow and his oath etc.': meaning his vow is valid. The author intended to explain what coercion affects and what it does not. The rule: every act that cannot be rescinded after occurrence is not affected by coercion in terms of preventing validity because coercion causes loss of consent, and loss of consent affects non-bindingness, and non-bindingness enables the coerced person to rescind, so coercion enables rescission after occurrence. Thus, what does not admit rescission is not affected by coercion, so the vow is valid under coercion. Thus in al-'Inayah. He said in al-Hidayah: There is no recourse against the coercer for what became binding because he has no claim against him in the world, so he is not held liable for it therein. Likewise the oath and dhihar are not affected by coercion because they do not admit rescission, and likewise revocation and ila' and fay' therein verbally because they are valid with jest.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof establishing the Hanafi principle that certain acts are valid despite coercion. The commentary explains that the key factor is whether the act admits rescission (faskh). Acts like vows (nathr), oaths (yamin), dhihar, and revocation (raj'a) cannot be undone after being performed; therefore, coercion does not prevent their validity. The reasoning: coercion removes consent (rida), and lack of consent makes an act non-binding (ghayr lazim). Non-binding allows the coerced person to rescind the act. But if the act itself cannot be rescinded, then coercion has no effect, and the act is valid. The text cites al-'Inayah and al-Hidayah to confirm this. Additionally, if someone is coerced into making a vow, he cannot later claim compensation from the coercer because the obligation is enforceable. Similarly, ila' (vow of abstinence) and fay' (return) are valid under coercion because they can be done jokingly and do not admit rescission.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion: compelling someone to do an act against their will by threat of harm.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission: the act of annulling a contract or obligation after its occurrence.
الرضاء (al-rida): Consent: the willingness required for a binding contract. Coercion removes it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, why are vows (nathr) valid even when made under coercion?
A) Because coercion only affects acts that are mubah.
B) Because vows do not admit rescission after occurrence.
C) Because the coerced person can always seek compensation later.
D) Because vows are not considered legal acts.

Key Takeaway
Acts that cannot be rescinded, such as vows and oaths, remain valid and binding under coercion because coercion only enables rescission, and rescission is impossible.

The Hanafi Principle: Validity of Certain Acts Under Compulsion
Opening of the Book of Ikrah with the general principle and specific acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that contracts not admitting rescission and acts valid in jest are valid under compulsion, and apply it to Ila', Fay', and Islam.

Core Idea
Contracts not admitting rescission and acts valid in jest remain effective under compulsion.

Compulsion (ikrah) does not automatically invalidate all legal acts. The Hanafi school distinguishes acts that are inherently effective even when done under duress. In this lesson, we explore the general principle behind this ruling and its application to Ila' (vow of abstinence), Fay' (return within the Ila' period), and conversion to Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Locate the statement: 'Any contract that does not admit rescission, compulsion does not prevent its effectiveness. Similarly, everything that takes effect with jest takes effect with compulsion.' This is the foundation.
2
Apply the Principle to Specific Acts
The passage lists Ila', Fay', and Islam as examples. Verify how each fits the principle: Ila' is a vow and future divorce (does not admit rescission), Fay' is its revocation, and Islam is a verbal declaration that is effective regardless of inner intent.
3
Analyze the Proof for Islam
The hadith 'I have been commanded to fight people until they say La ilaha illa Allah' is cited. It shows that Islam is valid when uttered under fear of death, as the command to fight ceases upon utterance.
The proof establishes validity, but the commentary adds that this validity creates a doubt (shubha) preventing execution for apostasy.
4
Understand the Exception for Apostasy
If a coerced convert later apostatizes, he is not killed because his initial Islam may be invalid (shubha). This protects him from the death penalty.
The shubha arises from the possibility that the coerced conversion was not genuine, preserving original disbelief.
5
Distinguish from Invalid Acts
The passage hints that some acts, like releasing a debtor (ibra'), are not valid under compulsion. Contrast with Ila' and Fay' to understand the boundary of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه / تعريفه وشروطه وأحكامه
[1]
۹۱
وإيلاؤُه، وفيؤهُ فيه، وإسلامه بلا قتل لو رجع، لا إبراؤه مديونه أو كفيله وإيلاؤه، وفيؤه فيه ، وإسلامه بلا قتل لو رجع)، الأصل عندنا أن كل عقد لا يحتمل الفسخ فالإكراه لا يمنع نفاذه، وكذلك كل ما ينفد مع الهزل ينفذ مع الإكراه، والإسلام إنما يصح مع الإكراه ؛ لقوله ل له : «أُمِرْتُ أن أقاتلَ النَّاسِ حَتَّى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله ، فالإسلام يصح مع خوف القتل، لكن إذا أسلم المُكْرَهُ، ثُمَّ ارتد لا يقتلُ لتمكّن الشبهة في إسلامه"
[١] قوله : وإيلاؤه وفيؤه فيه ؛ لأنَّ الإيلاء يمين في الحال، وطلاق في المآل، كما صرح به في بابه، والإكراه لا يمنع كلّ واحد منهما، والفيء الرجوع. والمراد هاهنا أن يقول المولى رجعتُ فيه : أي في زمان الإيلاء، ولأن ترك الفيء إليها أربعة أشهر حتى بانت ولم يكن دخل بها وجب نصف المهر، ولا يرجع به على المكره ؛ لأنه كان متمكناً من القربان في المدة، فإذا لم يفعل كان ذلك رضاً منه بما لزمه
(۲)
من الصداق، وإن قربها وكفِّرَ لم يرجع على المكره بشيء؛ لأنه أتى بضد ما أكرهه عليه. كذا في «العناية». [٢]قوله : لتمكن الشبهة في إسلامه؛ أي لاحتمال عدم الإسلام من الابتداء، فيكون كفره أصيلاً ؛ لعدم صحة إسلامه، والشبهة دارئة للقتل.
Book of Compulsion: Its Definition, Conditions, and Rulings. [1] 91 And his Ila' (vow of abstinence), and his Fay' (return) in it, and his Islam without killing if he returns, not his release of his debtor or his guarantor, and his Ila', and his Fay' in it, and his Islam without killing if he returns). The principle with us is that any contract that does not admit rescission, compulsion does not prevent its effectiveness. Similarly, everything that takes effect with jest takes effect with compulsion. And Islam is valid under compulsion because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'I have been commanded to fight people until they say La ilaha illa Allah.' So Islam is valid with fear of death, but if the coerced person becomes Muslim and then apostatizes, he is not killed due to the presence of doubt in his Islam. [1] His statement: 'And his Ila' and his Fay' in it' because Ila' is an oath in the present and divorce in the future, as clarified in its chapter, and compulsion does not prevent either of them. And Fay' is the return. What is meant here is that the husband says 'I have returned to it' i.e., to the period of Ila'. And because if he refrains from returning to her for four months until she becomes separated (ba'in) and he had not consummated the marriage, half the dower becomes obligatory, and he cannot seek recourse from the one who coerced him because he was able to approach her during the period, so if he did not do so, that was consent to what became incumbent upon him of the dower. And if he approaches her and makes expiation, he cannot seek recourse from the coerced for anything because he did the opposite of what he was coerced to do. Thus in al-'Inayah. [2] His statement: 'Due to the presence of doubt in his Islam' meaning due to the possibility that he was not Muslim from the beginning, so his disbelief is original because his Islam was not valid, and doubt prevents killing.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Compulsion (Ikrah) by establishing a key principle: any contract that cannot be rescinded (like marriage, divorce, etc.) remains effective even if performed under compulsion. Similarly, any act that is valid when done in jest (hazl) is also valid under compulsion. The author then applies this to three specific cases: Ila' (vow to abstain from marital relations), Fay' (return to marital relations within the Ila' period), and Islam (conversion). For Ila' and Fay', the ruling is that they are valid under compulsion because Ila' is both an oath and a potential divorce, and compulsion does not prevent oaths or divorces. The commentary explains that if a husband under compulsion fails to return (Fay') to his wife within four months, making her ba'in, he must pay half the dower and cannot recover it from the coercer, as he had the ability to approach her. If he does approach and expiate, he also cannot recover anything because he acted contrary to what he was coerced to do. For Islam, the hadith 'I have been commanded to fight people until they say La ilaha illa Allah' shows that Islam is valid even under fear of death. However, if the coerced person later apostatizes, he is not killed due to the doubt (shubha) that his initial Islam may not have been genuine. This doubt arises because his Islam under compulsion may not be valid from the start, making his original disbelief (kufr) continue, and the shubha of invalidity of his Islam prevents the application of the death penalty for apostasy.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Compelling a person to do something against their will, which may affect the validity of the act.
إيلاء (ila'): A vow made by a husband to abstain from marital relations for a period of time, which leads to divorce if not broken within four months.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a legal penalty, such as the death penalty for apostasy.
هزل (hazl): Jesting or joking, which in Hanafi law does not invalidate certain acts (like marriage, divorce) that are effective even if done in jest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle stated in this passage, which of the following is valid under compulsion?
A) Ila' (vow of abstinence)
B) Release of a debt (ibra')
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, acts that do not admit rescission or are valid in jest remain effective under compulsion; Ila', Fay', and conversion to Islam are prime examples, while apostasy after coerced conversion is not punishable by death due to doubt.

Ibra' Under Compulsion is Invalid: Rationale and Pre-emption Analogy
Explaining that releasing a debtor or guarantor is not valid under compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ibra' under compulsion, explain why it is invalid, and apply the same principle to shuf'a under compulsion.

Core Idea
Ibra' is invalid under compulsion because it is an acknowledgment that is void in jest; coerced silence does not nullify pre-emption.

In the previous segment, we learned that certain acts like ila', fay', and islam are valid even under compulsion because they take effect in jest. Here, the matn turns to ibra' – release of a debtor or guarantor – and declares it invalid when coerced. The commentary unpacks the reason: ibra' is an acknowledgment of discharge, akin to a confession, which cannot be taken lightly. The same logic extends to the right of pre-emption (shuf'a): coerced silence does not forfeit the right, but voluntary silence does.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states 'Not his release of his debtor or his guarantor' – meaning ibra' performed under compulsion is not valid. Confirm this as the Hanafi position.
Contrast with ila', fay', islam which are valid under compulsion.
2
Understand the rationale: ibra' as acknowledgment
The commentary says ibra' is not valid in jest because it is an acknowledgment (iqrar) that the liability is discharged. Since jest invalidates an acknowledgment, compulsion – being stronger – also invalidates it.
This links ibra' to confessions, which require free will.
3
Apply the principle to shuf'a: coerced silence
If the pre-emptor is coerced into silence, his right remains if he claims after compulsion ends. Voluntary silence after compulsion ceases would nullify it. Similarly, coerced surrender of the property after claiming does not invalidate the right.
This shows compulsion blocks the legal effect of silence/surrender.
4
Note the source authority
The rulings are cited from Radd al-Muhtar, a later authoritative Hanafi work, indicating the mufta bihi position.
Radd al-Muhtar is a key reference for fatwa in the late Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لا إبراؤه مديونه أو كفيله ؛ أي [لا يصح مع الإكراه إبراؤه مديونه أو إبراؤه كفيله بنفس أو مال ؛ لأن البراءة لا تصح مع الهزل ؛ لأنها إقرار لفراغ الذمة ، فيؤثر فيها الإكراه.
وكذا لو أكره الشفيع أن يسكت عن طلب الشفعة فسكت لا تبطل شفعته إذا زال الإكراه فطلب، وإلا فبطلت وكذا لو أكره على تسليمها بعد طلبها لا تبطل. هكذا في رد المحتار (۳).
His statement: 'Not his release of his debtor or his guarantor' i.e., [it is not valid with compulsion: his release of his debtor or his release of his guarantor, whether regarding person or property, because release (bara'a) is not valid with jest because it is an acknowledgment of the discharge of the liability, so compulsion affects it. Similarly, if the pre-emptor (shafi') is coerced to remain silent about claiming pre-emption, and he remains silent, his pre-emption is not nullified if he claims after the compulsion ceases; otherwise it is nullified. Similarly, if he is coerced to surrender it after claiming, it is not nullified. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar (3).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage addresses the second part of the matn: 'Not his release of his debtor or his guarantor'. The commentary explains that releasing a debtor or a guarantor from a debt (ibra') is not valid when done under compulsion. The reason is that ibra' is an act that does not take effect in jest (hazl); it is an acknowledgment that the liability is discharged from the debtor's conscience. Since jest invalidates it, compulsion, which is stronger, also invalidates it. This is in contrast to the earlier acts (ila', fay', islam) which are valid under compulsion because they take effect in jest. The commentary then adds a related ruling: If a pre-emptor (shafi') is coerced to remain silent about claiming his right of pre-emption (shuf'a), his right is not lost if the compulsion is removed and he then claims. However, if he remains silent voluntarily after the compulsion ends, the right lapses. Similarly, if he is coerced to surrender the property after making his claim, the surrender is not valid and his pre-emption right remains. These rulings are from Radd al-Muhtar, a later authoritative Hanafi work.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Release of a debtor from a debt or of a guarantor from liability; it is an act of discharge.
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption right: the right of a co-owner to purchase a sold share from the buyer, with priority over others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it is an acknowledgment that does not take effect in jest
C) Because it requires a judge
D) Because it is a form of contract

Key Takeaway
Ibra' is an acknowledgment of discharge, so compulsion invalidates it just as jest does; coerced silence in pre-emption likewise preserves the right.

Three Cases of Apostasy Under Compulsion
Explaining the three scenarios of apostasy under compulsion and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi scenarios of apostasy under compulsion (qada'an and diyanatan) and apply the correct ruling for each.

Core Idea
Apostasy under compulsion: the ruling depends on intent, not just the utterance — three cases.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a nuanced Hanafi ruling on apostasy when a person is coerced to utter words of disbelief. The text distinguishes between judicial (qada') and inward (diyana) dimensions, and presents three distinct scenarios based on the coerced person's intent. Understanding these cases is crucial for distinguishing between outward rulings and actual belief in the sight of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Note that apostasy relates to belief, not merely utterance. The text states: 'apostasy concerns belief.' This underlies the entire ruling.
2
Distinguish qada'an vs diyanatan
Understand the two dimensions: qada'an (judicial ruling based on outward actions) and diyanatan (inward reality before Allah). A person can be judged apostate in court but not before Allah if his heart is firm.
This distinction is a key feature of Hanafi fiqh.
3
Examine Case 1: Heart tranquil, intends report
The coerced person utters the words but his heart is content in faith and he only meant to narrate what was demanded. He is not apostate in either dimension; his wife remains married.
Necessity excuses the utterance.
4
Examine Case 2: Intends disbelief itself
The coerced person utters the words and intends the disbelief; he had the possibility to intend a past report but did not. He becomes apostate in both dimensions; his wife is separated.
The possibility of escape removes the excuse of necessity.
5
Examine Case 3: Claims intent to report, but in court
The coerced person utters the words and later claims he only meant to report a past event. The judge does not accept this claim because the apparent meaning is willful disbelief. He is treated as apostate judicially (separated in court) but not inwardly (if his claim is true).
His word is not accepted in court due to contrary appearance, but it is accepted diyanatan.
6
Note the istihsan rule for conflicting claims
If the wife claims apostasy and the husband says his heart was firm, the husband's word is taken by istihsan, because the utterance under compulsion does not necessarily indicate a change of belief.
This is a departure from strict analogy that would give weight to the wife's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وردته فلا تبين عرسه (۳)
[١] قوله : وردته ؛ أي بلسانه وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان، اعلم أن المكره على إجراء كلمة الكفر على ثلاث أوجه :
في وجه لا يكفر لا قضاءً ولا ديانة، وهو أنه أكره على الإجراء فأجراها ولم مخلصاً يخطر بباله غير ما طلب منه من الكفر ، وهو الخبر عما مضى، فلم يعلم لنفسه . غیره، فيتعذر بالاضطرار، فلا يكفرُ ولا تبين امرأته بالإجراء وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان. وفي وجه يكفر قضاءً وديانةً ، وهو أنه أكره فأجراها ، وقال : أردتُ ما طلب مني من الكفر، وقد خطر ببالي الخبر عما مضى ؛ لأنه مبتدئ بالكفر هازل به، حيث علم لنفسه مخلصاً غيرَه ؛ لأنه لما خطر أمكنه الخروج عما ابتلي به بأن ينوي ذلك، والضرورة قد اندفعت بهذا الإمكان، فإذا لم يفعل وأنشأ الكفر كان كمن أجرى كلمة الكفر طائعاً على وجه الاستخفاف، مع علمه أنه كفر ، فتبين امرأته في القضاء والديانة. أنه وفي وجه يكفر قضاء ويفرّق القاضي بينه وبين امرأته، ولم يكفر ديانة ، وهو أكره فأجراها فقال في جواب ادّعائها البينونة : أخبرت عن أمر ماض، ولم أكن فعلت ؛ لأنه إقرار أنه طائع بإتيان ما لم يكره عليه؛ لأنه أكـره علـى الإنشاء دون الإقرار، ومن أقرّ بكفر فيما مضى طائعاً، ثمّ قال : عنيت به الكذب، لا يصدقه القاضي ؛ لأنه خلافُ الظاهر، إذ الظاهر هو الصدقُ حالة الطواعية، لكنه يصدق ديانة ؛ لأنه ادعى ما يحتمله لفظه . كذا هاهنا. كذا قال أخي جلبي [٢] قوله : فلا تبين عرسه ؛ لأنّ الردة تتعلّق بالاعتقاد، ألا ترى أنه لو كان قلبه مطمئناً بالإيمان لا يكفر، وفي اعتقاده الكفر شك، فلا يثبت البينونة بالشك، فإن قالت المرأة : قد ثبت منك ، وقال هو : قد أظهرتُ ذلك وقلبي مطمئن بالإيمان، فالقول قوله استحساناً ؛ لأنَّ اللفظ غير موضوع للفرقة، والفرقةُ بتبدل الاعتقاد ومع الإكراه لا يدلُّ هذا اللفظ على التبدل فكان القول قوله.
(1)
بخلاف الإكراه على الإسلام، حيث يصير به مسلماً؛ لأنه لما احتمل الإسلام
(۱) في «ذخيرة العقبی»(ص٥٤٢ - ٥٤٣).
His statement: 'And his apostasy, so his wife does not become separated (from him).' [1] His statement: 'And his apostasy' i.e., with his tongue while his heart is tranquil with faith. Know that the person coerced to utter the word of disbelief is in three cases: In one case, he does not become an unbeliever (kafir) judicially (qada'an) nor inwardly (diyanatan): that is when he is coerced to utter it and he utters it, and nothing other than what was demanded of disbelief occurs to his mind, and he intends it as a report of something past, so he does not ascribe anything else to himself, thus he excuses himself by necessity, so he does not become a disbeliever nor does his wife become separated by the utterance, while his heart is tranquil with faith. In another case, he becomes a disbeliever both judicially and inwardly: that is when he is coerced, utters it, and says, 'I intended what was demanded of me of disbelief,' and the idea of reporting the past occurred to him, because he begins disbelief and jest with it, knowing for himself an excuse other than that, because when it occurred to him, he could have escaped the trial by intending that (i.e., the report), and necessity is removed by this possibility, so if he does not do so and he produces disbelief, he is like one who utters the word of disbelief willingly with contempt, knowing it is disbelief, so his wife becomes separated judicially and inwardly. And in another case, he becomes a disbeliever judicially and the judge separates him from his wife, but he does not become a disbeliever inwardly: that is when he is coerced and utters it, and in response to her claim of separation, he says, 'I reported about a past matter, and I did not do it,' because it is an acknowledgment that he is willingly doing what he was not coerced to do, because he was coerced to create (insha) not to admit (iqrar), and whoever willingly admits to past disbelief, then says, 'I meant by it a lie,' the judge does not believe him because it is contrary to the apparent, since the apparent is truthfulness in a state of willfulness, but he is believed inwardly (diyanatan) because he claims what his words bear. So here. Thus said Akhi Jalbī. [2] His statement: 'So his wife does not become separated' because apostasy concerns belief. Do you not see that if his heart is tranquil with faith, he does not become a disbeliever? And if there is doubt in his disbelief, separation is not established by doubt. If the woman says, 'You have done it,' and he says, 'I displayed that while my heart was tranquil with faith,' then the statement is his word (qaul qauluhu) by istihsan, because the word (kufr) is not placed for separation, and separation is due to change of belief, and under compulsion this word does not indicate change, so his word is taken. (1) Contrary to compulsion for Islam, where he becomes Muslim thereby, because he accepted Islam (1) In 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba' (p.542-543).
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on apostasy (ridda) under compulsion. The matn states that if a person is compelled to utter words of disbelief, his wife does not automatically become separated from him. The commentary divides the scenario into three cases based on the person's intent and the meaning he assigns to the words. Case 1: The coerced person utters the words with his heart content in faith, and he means only to narrate what is demanded, not to adopt disbelief. He is not a disbeliever judicially (in court) nor inwardly (before Allah), and his wife remains married to him. Case 2: He utters the words and intends the disbelief itself, and although the possibility of intending a past report occurred to him, he disregards it and intentionally creates a statement of disbelief. He becomes a disbeliever both judicially and inwardly, and his wife is separated. Case 3: He utters the words but later claims he only intended to report a past event (i.e., he said the words as a narration, not as a statement of his own belief). In court, the judge does not accept this claim because the apparent meaning is that he is willfully uttering disbelief; therefore, the judge separates them judicially. However, inwardly he is not a disbeliever if he truly meant what he claims. The commentary then explains why the wife is not automatically separated: apostasy relates to belief, and if the heart is firm in faith, there is no actual apostasy. If there is doubt about his belief, separation cannot be established by doubt. If the wife claims apostasy and he denies it, his word is taken (by istihsan) because the words of disbelief under compulsion do not necessarily indicate a change of belief. The passage ends with a note distinguishing the case of compulsion to Islam: if a person is compelled to become Muslim, he does become Muslim (unlike apostasy), as he accepted Islam.

Key Terms
ردة (ridda): Apostasy from Islam; legally, it results in separation from spouse and potential execution if established.
قضاء (qada'): Judicial ruling based on outward actions; in this context, the ruling of a judge in a court of law.
ديانة (diyana): Inward reality in the sight of Allah, based on intention and actual belief.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows a ruling to depart from strict analogy due to prevailing custom or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third case of apostasy under compulsion, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He is not a disbeliever judicially or inwardly
B) He is a disbeliever judicially but not inwardly
C) He is a disbeliever both judicially and inwardly
D) The wife is not separated

Key Takeaway
Apostasy under compulsion: the Hanafi school distinguishes three scenarios based on intent; separation depends on whether the utterance truly indicates a change of belief.

The Hanafi Definition of Hajr: Scope and Application
Defines hajr and clarifies its scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of hajr (interdiction) and distinguish between verbal dispositions and physical actions in its application.

Core Idea
Hajr prevents the validity of words, not actions – what is done cannot be undone, but what is said need not take effect.

This passage opens the Book of Hajr in the Hanafi fiqh manual. The author defines hajr as a restriction on the enforceability of verbal statements, not on physical acts. Understanding this boundary is essential to correctly applying the rules of interdiction to minors, the insane, and others. We will unpack the definition and address a nuanced objection concerning slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Note that hajr is defined as 'man' nufadh tasarruf qawli' (prevention of the validity of a verbal disposition). This is the technical Hanafi definition.
2
Why physical actions are excluded
Actions cannot be undone once committed, so preventive restriction is inconceivable; liability still applies (e.g., child pays damages).
3
Lexical and technical meanings
Linguistically, hajr means prevention. Technically, it applies only to verbal acts. The term is also used for the Hijr area around the Kaaba (prevented from the Kaaba) and for intellect (prevents evils).
4
Objection about slaves and response
Some object that a slave’s physical actions are also restricted. The Hanafi response distinguishes that this delay is due to ownership, not hajr; hajr concerns verbal dispositions primarily.
The debate shows the precision of the definition.
5
Types of verbal dispositions
Not all verbal statements are treated equally: a child's divorce is entirely void (no capacity), while a slave's divorce is valid (has capacity but ownership restriction).
The difference lies in the person's capacity (aql and bulugh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحجر) هو منع نفاذ تصرف قولي، إنما قال هذا ؛ لأنَّ الحَجْرَ لا يتحقق في أفعال الجوارح، فالصبي إذا أتلف مال الغير يجب الضمان ، وكذا المجنون.
[١] قوله : كتاب الحجر ; أورده بعد الإكراه ; لأنّ في كل سلب ولاية المختار عن الجري على موجب الاختيار والإكراه أقوى ; لأنَّ فيه السلب ممن له اختيار صحيح ، وولاية كاملة، فكان بالتقديم أحرى. كذا في «ردّ المحتار).
[٢] قوله : الحجر ; هو لغة : المنع ، مصدر حجر عليه حجراً، من باب قتل : منعه عن التصرف، فهو محجور عليه، والفقهاء يحذفون الصلة تخفيفاً، ومنه سمّي الحطيم حجراً بالكسر ; لأنه منع من الكعبة، وكذا العقل لمنعه من القبائح. هكذا في «ردّ المحتار).
[٣] قوله : هو منع ; من نفاذ قولي لا فعلي ; لأنَّ الفعل بعد وقوعه لا يمكن ردُّه ، فلا يتصور الحجرُ عنه ; ولذا قال الشارح الله ; لأنَّ الحجر لا يتحقق في أفعال الجوارح. واستشكل عليه صاحب «الدر المختار» (۳) بقوله : قلت : يشكل عليه الرقيق ; لمنع نفاذ فعله في الحال، بل بعد العتق ، كما صرّح به في «البدائع»، ثم أجاب هو عنه بقوله : اللهم إلا أن يقال : الأصلُ فيه ذلك ، لكنّه أُخر لعتقه لقيام المانع ، فتأمل. ثم اعلم أن المراد من نفاذ تصرّف قولي : لزومه، فإن عقد المحجور ينعقد موقوفاً ، والنافذ أعم من اللازم. «قهستاني»، والحاصل أن المنع من ثبوتِ حكـم التصرُّف فلا يفيد الملك بالقبض ، وفيه : إنّه لا يشمل سوى العقود الدائرة بين النفع والضر، مع أن القول قد يلغو أصلاً ; كطلاق الصبي، وقد يصح كطلاق العبد.
Book of Hajr: It is the prevention of the validity of a verbal disposition. He only said this because hajr is not realized in actions of the limbs. For if a child destroys another's property, he is liable for compensation, and likewise a madman. [1] His saying: Book of Hajr; he mentioned it after coercion because each one deprives the competent person of acting according to his choice, and coercion is stronger because it deprives one who has sound choice and complete authority, so it was more deserving of being placed first. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] His saying: Hajr; lexically it is prevention, a verbal noun from 'hajara 'alayhi' meaning he prevented him from disposing, so he is mahjur 'alayhi (interdicted). The jurists omit the complement for brevity. From it is named al-Hatim (the Hijr area) because it is prevented from the Ka'ba. Similarly, the intellect is called 'aql because it prevents from evils. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. [3] His saying: It is prevention, of the validity of a verbal disposition, not a physical one, because an action after its occurrence cannot be undone, so prevention from it is inconceivable. Hence the commentator said: because hajr is not realized in actions of the limbs. The author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar objected saying: 'I say: it is problematic regarding the slave, because his action is prevented in the present, indeed after emancipation, as stated in al-Bada'i.' Then he answered by saying: 'It may be said that the basic principle is that, but it is delayed until emancipation due to the existence of a preventing factor, so consider.' Then know that what is meant by 'validity of verbal disposition' is bindingness, for the contract of an interdicted person is concluded suspended, and 'validity' is more general than 'binding'. (Qahistani). The result is that prevention from the establishment of the ruling of the disposition, so it does not confer ownership by possession, and it includes only contracts that revolve between benefit and harm, while a statement may be entirely void like the divorce of a child, or valid like the divorce of a slave.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Hajr (Interdiction) by defining the term. In Hanafi fiqh, hajr is the prevention of the legal effectiveness of verbal dispositions (like sales, divorces, etc.), not of physical actions. The reason is that physical actions, once done, cannot be undone, so it is impossible to prevent them retroactively. For example, if a minor or a madman destroys someone's property, he is still liable for damages; the hajr does not prevent the act itself but rather prevents the legal effects of his words. The text then provides lexical and technical explanations. It notes that hajr follows the chapter on coercion because both limit one's freedom of choice, but coercion is stronger because it overrides an agent who otherwise has full capacity. The commentator then addresses an objection regarding slaves: a slave's physical actions (like working) are also restricted in the present, but the response is that this is a different matter and hajr primarily concerns verbal dispositions. The text concludes by clarifying that 'nufadh' (validity/enforcement) in the definition means bindingness, so a suspended contract is not nafidh but can become so later. This differentiates between types of statements: some are entirely void (like a child's divorce) and some are valid (like a slave's divorce, which is effective).

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajr): A legal restriction preventing the validity of a person's verbal dispositions, such as contracts or divorces.
نفاذ (nufadh): Validity or enforceability; here it means the legal effect of a disposition taking immediate effect.
المحجور عليه (al-mahjur 'alayhi): The person subject to interdiction, whose verbal dispositions are restricted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition of hajr, which of the following is subject to hajr?
A) A minor damaging property
B) A madman committing assault
C) A minor selling his property without permission
D) A slave being forced to work

Key Takeaway
Hajr in Hanafi fiqh is specifically the prevention of validity of verbal dispositions, not physical actions, and understanding this boundary is crucial for correct application of the rules of interdiction.

Definition of Al-Hajr and Its Scope
Defines al-hajr and clarifies its scope and one cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of al-hajr and explain the dispute over whether it includes physical acts.

Core Idea
Al-hajr primarily blocks verbal dispositions from taking effect, but later Hanafis extend it to legal rulings of physical acts.

This lesson unpacks the concept of al-hajr (interdiction) as defined in the Hanafi school. The core matn limits hajr to verbal acts, but commentators debate whether it also covers the legal effects of physical actions. We will trace the definition from its narrow origin to its comprehensive formulation in later works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Read the opening statement: 'al-hajr is the prevention of validity of a verbal disposition.' Note that it specifies 'verbal' and 'validity' (nafadh).
The Arabic phrase is 'man' nafadh tasarruf qawli'.
2
Understand the limitation to verbal dispositions
The commentator explains that physical acts cannot be undone, so preventing them is inconceivable. Thus, hajr only applies to verbal acts like contracts.
Example: a minor's sale is suspended, but if he breaks a window, he must pay compensation.
3
Analyze the objection from the slave's physical acts
The author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar objects: a slave's physical acts are also prevented from immediate validity. The response is that this is due to the barrier of slavery, not hajr per se.
This shows the tension between the narrow definition and real cases.
4
Examine the comprehensive definition from al-Idah
Al-Idah defines hajr as prevention from a specific disposition or its validity, and it includes physical acts for slaves (harmful acts) and for minors/insane (verbal acts that are purely harmful or between harm and benefit).
This expands the scope beyond the matn.
5
Note the three ranks of hajr
The margin mentions three ranks: strongest (prevention from the act entirely), medium (prevention from validity), and weak (prevention from immediate validity).
These ranks help categorize different forms of interdiction.

Book of Al-Hajr. It is the prevention of validity of a verbal disposition. Book of Al-Hajr. (It is the prevention of validity of a verbal disposition). He only said this because al-hajr is not realized in physical actions. For if a minor damages another's property, he is liable for compensation, and likewise the insane. [1] His saying: Book of Al-Hajr; he brought it after coercion because in each there is removal of a free person's authority to act according to choice, and coercion is stronger because it removes from one who has sound choice and complete authority, so it was more deserving of precedence. And by this, what the commentator – i.e., the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar – objected from its principle is repelled. As for his reasoning: because the act after its occurrence... we say: the discussion is about prevention of its ruling, not prevention of its essence. And similarly, speech cannot be refuted in its essence after it occurs, but rather its ruling is refuted. If you say: he limited it to verbal because acts are not all prevented, for those that necessitate liability are taken into account. I say: similarly, some speech is not prevented, like that which is purely beneficial, such as accepting a gift or charity, unless one distinguishes by prevalence and frequency, so it merits reflection. As in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His saying: and its cause... i.e., the causes necessitating hajr are three: minority, insanity.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the legal concept of al-hajr (interdiction) from the Hanafi perspective. It begins with the core definition from the matn: 'the prevention of validity of a verbal disposition.' The commentator then explains why only verbal acts are included: physical acts cannot be undone, so it is inconceivable to prevent them after the fact. However, a later objection is raised regarding slaves, whose physical acts are also prevented from immediate validity. The response is that this is delayed due to the barrier of slavery. The passage then provides a more comprehensive definition from al-Idah, which includes three categories: prevention for slaves concerning harmful physical acts, and for minors and the insane, prevention from purely harmful verbal acts and suspension of acts that are between harm and benefit. It further introduces three ranks of hajr: strongest (prevention from the act entirely), medium (prevention from its validity), and weak (prevention from immediate validity). The discussion concludes by affirming that hajr covers prevention of legal rulings even for physical acts like adultery and murder by minors and insane, as they are not subject to criminal penalties. The cause mentioned is minority, with insanity and slavery implied but not fully listed.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal prevention of the validity of a verbal disposition, or more generally, prevention from a specific disposition or its validity.
التصرف القولي (al-tasarruf al-qawli): A verbal act such as a contract, divorce, or acknowledgement.
النفاذ (al-nafadh): The validity or bindingness of a disposition; a nafidh disposition is one that has legal effect.
الصغر (al-sighar): Minority, the state of being a child before puberty, which is a cause for hajr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Al-hajr is a nuanced Hanafi concept that prevents the legal effectiveness of certain dispositions, with later scholarship extending it from purely verbal acts to include the legal rulings of physical acts for minors, insane, and slaves.

Definition and Causes of Legal Interdiction (al-Hajar) in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition of legal interdiction and enumeration of its causes with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define legal interdiction (al-hajar) in Hanafi fiqh and list its agreed-upon causes with their evidentiary basis.

Core Idea
Legal interdiction is a specific prohibition on a specific person from a specific disposition or its effectiveness, rooted in lack of legal capacity.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya opens the Book of Legal Interdiction. We will explore the precise definition of al-hajar as a specific prohibition tied to capacity, distinguish the three-tiered hierarchy of its strength, and examine the three agreed-upon causes—minority, insanity, slavery—along with evidence from hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
The passage defines al-hajar as a specific prohibition on a specific person from a specific disposition or its effectiveness. Understand each component: 'specific prohibition' (man' makhsūs), 'specific person' (shakhs makhsūs), 'specific disposition' (tasarruf makhsūs), and 'or its effectiveness' (aw 'an nafādhihi).
The definition is from al-Idah, a key Hanafi text.
2
Distinguish the three levels of interdiction
The margin note adds a hierarchy: strongest (prohibition from origin of disposition), medium (prohibition from attribute of effectiveness), weak (prohibition from attribute of being effective immediately). Note that for slaves, the interdiction is on effectiveness (medium); for minors and insane, it is on origin (strongest) for harmful verbal dispositions.
This three-tier structure is distinctive to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Recognize the cause of interdiction for each type
For slaves: interdiction from effectiveness of harmful actual dispositions, but they are affirmed with property immediately. For minors and insane: interdiction from origin of verbal dispositions that are purely harmful; suspension for those between harm and benefit.
Physical acts like adultery and murder are also included regarding their rulings (hadd, qisas).
4
List the agreed-upon causes
The three causes by consensus are: minority (sighar), insanity (junun), and slavery (riqq). Insanity includes both predominant (maghlub) and non-predominant, as well as mental deficiency (ma'tuh).
Three additional causes (licentious mufti, ignorant physician, bankrupt carrier) are appended by later scholars, and there is a dispute regarding debtor and spendthrift.
5
Analyze the hadith evidence
The passage cites two hadith: (1) 'Every divorce is valid except the divorce of the mentally deficient whose mind is overcome' (al-Tirmidhi) – this establishes that the insane person's divorce is invalid. (2) 'The pen is lifted from the minor, the insane, and the sleeping' (Ibn Majah) – this establishes no legal accountability for these individuals, forming the basis for interdiction.
The wajh al-dalalah: the hadiths indicate lack of legal capacity, which is the underlying cause for interdiction.

وقد أدخل في التعريف المنع عن الفعل كما ترى ، ودخل فيه نحو الزنا والقتل، في حق الصبي والمجنون ، فإنّه محجور عليهما بالنسبة لحكمه، الحد وهو والقصاص. كما في «الجوهرة».
ويظهر لي أن هذا هو التحقيق، فإنّه إن جعل الحجر هو المنع من ثبوت حكم التصرف، فما وجه تقييده بالقولي ونفي الفعلي، مع أنّ لكلّ حكما ؛ ولهذا يندفع ما استشكله الشارح له ؛ أي صاحب الدر المختار) من أصله، وأما ما علله به من قول قوله : لأنّ الفعل بعد وقوعه...الخ، نقول : الكلام في منع حكمه لا منع ذاته، ومثله القولُ لا يمكن ردّه بذاته بعد وقوعه، بل ردّ حكمه. فإن قلت : قيد بالقوليّ ؛ لأنّ الأفعال لا يحجرُ عنها كلّها ، فإنّ ما وجب الضمان منها يؤاخذ بها. قلت : وكذلك القول بعضه غير محجور عنه ، كالذي تمحض نفعاً ؛ كقبول الهبة والصدقة إلا أن يفرّق بالغلبة والكثرة، فيتأمّل. هكذا في رد المحتار). [١]قوله : وسببه...الخ ؛ أي أسباب الموجبة للحجرِ ثلاثة : الصغر ، والجنون، والرق. فلم يصح طلاق صبي ومجنون غلب والجنون، والرق. فلم يصح طلاق صبي ومجنون" غلب) : أي المجنون المغلوب : هو الذي اختلط عقلُهُ بحيث يمنع جريان الأفعال والأقوال على نهج العقل إلا نادراً وغير المغلوب هو الذي يختلط كلامه فيشبُهُ كلامُهُ مرَّة كلام العقلاء ، ومرة لا ، وهو والرق، هذه الثلاثة بالاتفاق وألحق بها ثلاثة أخرى وهي: المفتي الماجن، والمتطبب الجاهل، والمكاري المفلس، وأمّا حجر المديون، والسفيه بعدما بلغ رشيداً، فعلى قول أبي يوسف ومحمد ه. كذا في الشروح. هذا ما استفاده من نتائج الأفكار». [١] قوله : والجنون ؛ أعم من القوي والضعيف كما في المعتوه ، وحكمه كمميز كما سيجيء في المأذون قال في «الإيضاح»: إنّ سبب الحجر هو مطلق الجنون، ويراد بالقوي : المطبق، وبالضعيف غيره، أو يراد بالقويّ : القسمين، وبالضعيف العته، فالكافُ في : كما في المعتوه ؛ للتنظير على الأوّل، وللتمثيل على الثاني، فتأمل. هكذا في رد المحتار»(۲). [٢]قوله : ومجنون ؛ لما أخرجه التِّرْمِذِيُّ عن أبي هُرَيْرَةَ لله قال : قال رسول الله «كل طلاق جائز إلا طلاق المعتوه المغلوب على عقله»(۳). انتهى. وهو المجنون ، وأخرج ابن ماجة عن علي له قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «رفـع القـلـم عـن الـصغير والمجنون والنائم ). كذا في نصب الراية لتخريج أحاديث الهداية).
Book of Interdiction / Its definition, rulings, and cause: minority. (And its cause: minority). So what is appropriate in its definition is what is in 'al-Idah' with his statement: In the terminology of the jurists, it is an expression of a specific prohibition on a specific person from a specific disposition, or from its effectiveness. And its detail: It is a prohibition on the slave from the effectiveness of his harmful actual disposition, and affirming it with property immediately, and for the minor and the insane concerning the origin of verbal disposition if it is purely harmful, and the suspension of its effectiveness if it is between harm and benefit. End. And written in its margin: Interdiction has levels: Strongest: it is the prohibition from the origin of disposition. Medium: it is the prohibition from its attribute, which is effectiveness. Weak: it is the prohibition from the attribute of its attribute, which is being effective immediately. End. And the prohibition on the act has been included in the definition as you see, and it includes such as adultery and murder regarding the minor and the insane, for they are interdicted concerning their ruling, which is the prescribed punishment and retribution. As in 'al-Jawhara'. And it appears to me that this is the verification, for if interdiction is made the prohibition from the establishment of the ruling of the disposition, then what is the justification for restricting it to the verbal and negating the actual, while each has a ruling? And for this reason, what the commentator (author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar) objected to from its origin is repelled. As for his reasoning with his statement 'because the act after its occurrence...', we say: The discussion is about the prohibition of its ruling, not its essence, and similarly the verbal cannot be repudiated in its essence after its occurrence, rather its ruling is repudiated. If you say: It was restricted to the verbal because not all acts are interdicted from, for what necessitates liability from them is held accountable. I say: Similarly, some verbal acts are not interdicted, such as that which is purely beneficial like accepting a gift or charity, unless a distinction is made by predominance and frequency, so it is to be considered. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His statement: 'And its cause...' i.e., the causes necessitating interdiction are three: minority, insanity, and slavery. So the divorce of a minor and an insane person is not valid. 'Predominant' means the completely insane person: the one whose intellect is confused such that it prevents the flow of actions and words on the path of reason except rarely. And the non-predominant is the one whose speech is confused, so sometimes his speech resembles that of rational people and sometimes not. And these three are by consensus. And three others were appended to them: the licentious mufti, the ignorant physician, and the bankrupt carrier. As for the interdiction of the debtor and the spendthrift after reaching maturity while rational, it is according to the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Thus in the commentaries. This is what is obtained from Nata'ij al-Afkar. [1] His statement: 'and the insane'; it is more general than the strong and weak, as in the mentally deficient (ma'tuh), and its ruling is like that of a discerning minor, as will come in the chapter of the authorized slave. He said in al-Idah: The cause of interdiction is absolute insanity, and by strong is meant continuous, and by weak other than it, or by strong is meant both types and by weak is mental deficiency. So the kaf in 'as in the mentally deficient' is for comparison according to the first, and for exemplification according to the second, so reflect. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] His statement: 'and the insane' due to what al-Tirmidhi narrated from Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of the mentally deficient whose mind is overcome.' End. And he is the insane. And Ibn Majah narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'The pen is lifted from the minor, the insane, and the sleeping.' Thus in Nasb al-Raya li Takhrīj Ahadith al-Hidaya.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya begins the Book of Legal Interdiction (al-Hajar). The passage type is a definition and enumeration of causes, expanding on the matn of al-Wiqayah. The commentator first presents a definition of al-hajar from al-Idah: it is a specific prohibition on a specific person from a specific disposition or from its effectiveness. He then details that for a slave, it prohibits the effectiveness of harmful acts but affirms his liability with property immediately. For a minor and insane person, it prohibits the origin of verbal dispositions that are purely harmful, and suspends effectiveness of those that are between harm and benefit. The margin note adds a three-tier hierarchy of interdiction: strongest (prohibition from origin of disposition), medium (prohibition from its attribute of effectiveness), weak (prohibition from the attribute of being effective immediately). The commentator critically refines the view that interdiction applies only to verbal acts, arguing that it also applies to the rulings of physical acts like adultery and murder for minors and the insane, as they are not subject to hadd punishments or qisas. He defends this by noting that the prohibition is on the ruling (hukm) of the act, not the act itself. The passage then lists the agreed-upon causes: minority (sighar), insanity (junun), and slavery (riqq). For insanity, it distinguishes between the predominant (maghlub) and non-predominant, both of which prevent valid divorce. Three additional causes are appended: the licentious mufti, the ignorant physician, and the bankrupt carrier. There is a dispute regarding interdiction of a debtor and a spendthrift after maturity: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it, while Abu Hanifa's position is not mentioned here. Evidence is provided from hadith: (1) the hadith of al-Tirmidhi that every divorce is valid except the divorce of the mentally deficient (ma'tuh) whose mind is overcome, and (2) the hadith of Ibn Majah that the pen is lifted from the minor, the insane, and the sleeping, which establishes their lack of legal accountability.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajar): A specific prohibition imposed on a specific person regarding a specific disposition or its effectiveness, as defined by Hanafi jurists.
الصغر (al-sighar): Minority; the state of being under the age of legal majority, which is a cause for interdiction in Hanafi fiqh.
الجنون (al-junun): Insanity; a mental disorder that prevents rational thought and action, classified into predominant (maghlub) and non-predominant, both subject to interdiction.
الرق (al-riqq): Slavery; the legal status of being a slave, which is a cause for interdiction regarding harmful actual dispositions but not for verbal acts that are beneficial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three causes of legal interdiction that are agreed upon by consensus?
A) Minority, insanity, and slavery
B) Minority, insanity, and bankruptcy
C) Minority, slavery, and ignorance
D) Insanity, slavery, and licentiousness

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of legal interdiction (al-hajar) is a specific prohibition on a specific person from a specific disposition or its effectiveness, with three agreed causes: minority, insanity, and slavery, supported by hadith evidence.

Defining al-Ma'tuh: Partial Legal Capacity
Definition of ma'tuh and its legal analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-ma'tuh according to the Hanafi school and explain his legal status as having partial capacity akin to a sane minor.

Core Idea
The ma'tuh has partial capacity; he is like a sane minor, not a non-sane minor.

In Hanafi fiqh, understanding who has full legal capacity is essential for valid transactions. This passage clarifies the meaning of 'al-ma'tuh' (the imbecile) and distinguishes him from the completely insane. The key is that the ma'tuh retains partial capacity, similar to a discerning minor, allowing some of his actions to be valid with guardian approval.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term al-ma'tuh in the Arabic text. Note that the commentator says the best definition is 'one who has little understanding, confused speech, corrupt management, but does not strike or insult.'
2
Contrast with al-majnun
Compare the definition with the description of al-majnun al-maghlub (overwhelmed insane). The ma'tuh lacks the violent behavior of the insane. This distinction is crucial for legal capacity.
3
Apply the legal analogy
The passage states: 'His ruling is that he is like a sane minor (al-sabi al-aqil), just as the overwhelmed insane is like a non-sane minor (al-sabi ghayr al-aqil).' Understand that this means the ma'tuh has partial capacity—his contract and divorces may be valid if permitted by a guardian, whereas the majnun's are void.
4
Recognize the ikhtilaf
The text says 'They differed in the interpretation of al-ma'tuh.' Note that Hanafis chose this definition; other schools may define it differently or not distinguish the two categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح طلاق العبد المعتوه. قوله : المعتوه؛ اختلفوا في تفسير المعتوه وأحسن ما قيل فيه : هو من كان قليل الفهم، مختلط الكلام، فاسد التدبير، إلا أنه لا يَضْرِبُ ولا يَشْتِم، كما يفعلُ المجنون. كما في «الدرر». قوله : وسيجيء حكمه؛ وهو أنه كالصبي العاقل، كما أن المجنون المغلوب كالصبي الغير العاقل.
And the divorce of the slave, the ma'tuh, is valid. His saying: al-ma'tuh: They differed in the interpretation of al-ma'tuh and the best that has been said about him is: he is one who has little understanding, confused speech, corrupt management, except that he does not strike nor insult, as the insane person does. As in al-Durar. His saying: And his ruling will come later: and it is that he is like a sane minor, just as the overwhelmed insane is like a non-sane minor.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement. It first defines the term 'al-ma'tuh' (the imbecile). The commentator presents the best definition: a person with limited understanding, confused speech, and poor judgment, but unlike the insane (majnun), he does not physically attack or verbally abuse. Then it states that the ruling for the ma'tuh is that he is treated like a sane minor (al-sabi al-aqil) in legal matters, while the overwhelmed insane (al-majnun al-maghlub) is treated like a non-sane minor (al-sabi ghayr al-aqil). This means the ma'tuh has partial legal capacity, whereas the majnun has no capacity. The specific ruling on the validity of divorce by a ma'tuh slave is referenced but will be discussed later.

Key Terms
المعتوه (al-ma'tuh): Person with limited understanding, confused speech, and poor judgment, but does not physically attack or insult.
المجنون المغلوب (al-majnun al-maghlub): The insane person who is overwhelmed (continuous insanity), with no lucid intervals.
الصبي العاقل (al-sabi al-aqil): A minor who is discerning (able to understand benefit and harm).
الصبي غير العاقل (al-sabi ghayr al-aqil): A minor who is not discerning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Al-ma'tuh has no understanding at all
B) Al-ma'tuh does not strike or insult like al-majnun
C) Al-ma'tuh is treated as a non-sane minor
D) Al-ma'tuh has perfect management

Key Takeaway
The ma'tuh has partial legal capacity, analogous to a sane minor, allowing his transactions to be valid with guardian approval, unlike the overwhelmed insane who has no capacity.

Liability of Restricted Persons for Destruction (ضمان المتلفات)
Ruling on liability for property damage by mahjurs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for destruction by mahjurs and explain the rational proof that liability attaches to physical acts regardless of financial restrictions.

Core Idea
A mahjur is liable for destruction because no restriction applies to the actions of the limbs.

In this lesson, we examine a concise but powerful Hanafi ruling: a person under financial interdiction (mahjur) is still fully liable for property damage they cause through physical acts. The source text draws a sharp distinction between financial transactions (which can be restricted) and physical actions (which cannot). This principle protects the rights of victims and ensures that liability for torts is not suspended by guardianship or bankruptcy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Agent
The source discusses 'they' – a group of persons under hijr (financial restriction), such as minors, the insane, or bankrupt individuals. The act is 'atlafa' – causing destruction to someone's property.
Mahjur includes minors, insane, and bankrupt persons under guardianship.
2
State the Ruling
The ruling is that they are 'damin' – liable to compensate the victim. This liability is wajib (obligatory) and does not depend on the agent's legal capacity for transactions.
The ruling is unconditional: even if the mahjur acted unintentionally, liability still applies.
3
Examine the Proof (Dalil)
The proof provided is a general principle: 'la hajar fi af'al al-jawarih' (there is no restriction regarding the actions of the limbs). This means that hijr only limits financial transactions, not physical acts.
This is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) derived from the purpose of hijr: to protect the mahjur's property, not to exempt them from torts.
4
Apply to an Example
Consider a minor under guardianship who accidentally breaks a merchant's vase. The minor is liable (damin), but since he is under hijr, payment may be deferred until he acquires property or reaches majority.
This contrasts with verbal acts like confessions or sales, which are void due to hijr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أتلفوا شيئاً ضمنوا؛ لما بينا أنه لا حجر في أفعال الجوارح
And if they destroy something, they are liable; because we have explained that there is no restriction regarding the actions of the limbs.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This is a concise ruling establishing that persons under financial interdiction (mahjur) are still fully liable for physical damages they cause (itlaf). The reason provided is that hijr (restriction) applies to financial transactions, not to the actions of the limbs (af'al al-jawarih). Liability for destruction does not depend on intent or legal capacity; even a sleeping person rolling over and causing damage is liable. This is distinct from verbal acts (like confessions) which may be restricted. The commentary notes that this is to protect the rights of the victim (ihya'an haqq al-mutlaf 'alayh).

Key Terms
أتلف (atlafa): To destroy or cause loss of property
ضمن (damin): To be liable for compensation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a person placed under financial interdiction (mahjur) who accidentally destroys someone's property:
A) Is not liable because of the restriction
B) Is liable because no restriction applies to physical acts
C) Is liable only if he acted intentionally
D) His guardian is liable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for physical destruction (itlaf) is not affected by financial interdiction; the person is always liable.
